Allah's Existence
beginner
8 min
The dispute over the possibility of a non-corporeal existent
Author presents two opposing views on the possibility of a non-corporeal existent and states his own position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two contrasting positions on the possibility of a non-corporeal existent and the author's rationalist methodology.

Core Idea
The existence of a non-corporeal being is not self-evidently impossible; it must be established by evidence.

In this passage, the author sets the stage for the rational inquiry into God's existence by contrasting two positions. Some claim that a non-corporeal existent is inconceivable, based on an assumption about existence itself. Others, including the author, argue that this is not a matter of intuition but of evidence. This distinction is crucial for understanding how kalam approaches the existence of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation, to grasp the two positions.
Pay attention to the strong language used by the first group.
2
Identify the two views
Notice how the author first presents the view of some people who claim impossibility, then the majority view which suspends judgment pending evidence.
3
Understand the author's methodology
The author endorses the majority view, emphasizing that theological claims require evidence, not mere intuition.
This is a key methodological point in kalam.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does it matter whether existence of a non-corporeal being is intuitive or dependent on evidence? This shapes the entire approach to proving God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم . أن كثيرا من الناس ، يزعمون : أن كل موجودين . فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه في جهة من الجهات الست . وزعموا أن إثبات موجود لا يكون حالاً في هذا العالم الجسماني، ولا مبايناً عنه بحسب شيء من الجهات : ممتنع الوجود . ثم زعموا : أن العلم بهذا الامتناع : علم بديي ضروري ، غني عن الحجة والدليل . وأما الجمهور الأعظم من العقلاء فإنهم اتفقوا على أن إثبات موجود ليس بمتحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز ، وليس في العالم، ولا في خارج العالم : ليس معلوم الامتناع في بديهة العقل ، بل الأمر في إثباته ونفيه موقوف على الدليل فإن دل الدليل على إثباته وجب القضاء به ، وإلا وجب التوقف في إثباته ونفيه وهذا القول هو الذي نذهب إليه ونقول به .
Know that many people claim that every two existents must be such that one is permeating the other or separate from it in one of the six directions. And they claimed that affirming an existent that is neither indwelling in this corporeal world nor separate from it in any direction is impossible of existence. Then they claimed that knowledge of this impossibility is intuitive necessary knowledge, independent of proof and evidence. As for the vast majority of rational people, they agreed that affirming an existent that is not a locus (mutahayyiz) nor indwelling in a locus, neither inside the world nor outside the world, is not known to be impossible in the intuition of the intellect; rather, the matter of affirming or denying it is dependent on evidence. If evidence indicates its affirmation, it must be judged accordingly, otherwise one must suspend judgment on its affirmation and denial. This is the opinion we adopt and profess.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage sets up a foundational dispute in kalam about the possibility of a non-corporeal being. The author begins by presenting the view of some people who hold a strong empiricist or physicalist assumption: that every two existents must either be interpenetrating (one inside the other) or separate in one of the six spatial directions. Based on this, they argue that affirming an existent that is neither a body (jism) nor indwelling in a body, and is not in any direction, is impossible. They further claim that this impossibility is known by immediate intuition (badihah) and needs no proof. The author then contrasts this with the position of the overwhelming majority of rational people. This majority holds that affirming an existent that is not a locus (mutahayyiz) nor indwelling in a locus, neither inside nor outside the world, is not known by intuition to be impossible. Instead, its affirmation or denial depends entirely on evidence (dalil). If evidence supports its existence, it must be accepted; otherwise, one must suspend judgment (tawaqquf). The author explicitly endorses this second position as his own. This is a crucial methodological stance: the existence of God is not self-evident in the sense of being impossible to deny, but rather it is a matter of rational inference. The passage demonstrates the kalam approach of appealing to common sense and argument rather than claiming immediate certainty for theological tenets.

Key Terms
بديهة (badihah)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)
حال (hāll)
جسم (jism)
موجود (mawjūd)
دليل (dalīl)
توقف (tawaqquf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of a non-corporeal existent is self-evident and requires no proof.
Correction: The author argues that this is not intuitively known; rather, it depends on evidence, and the majority of rational people suspend judgment until evidence is provided.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the position of the majority of rational people regarding the possibility of a non-corporeal existent?
A) It is impossible and known by intuition.
B) It is impossible but not known by intuition.
C) It is not impossible by intuition; its existence depends on evidence.
D) It is necessary and self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a non-corporeal existent is not a matter of intuition but of evidence; rational theology proceeds by argument, not by claiming self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on intuition or on careful evidence when it comes to your belief in a non-corporeal Creator? If you have never examined the proofs, what is stopping you from seeking them out this week?
?
When you encounter someone who dismisses the existence of God as impossible, like the physicalists in the passage, does your own faith rest on anything more than a feeling? This lesson demands that you consider the evidence; will you personally examine one rational proof for God's existence before the day ends?
?
If the majority of rational people suspend judgment until evidence is provided, have you ever suspended judgment on something in your faith that you haven't investigated? Where in your life are you believing without evidence, and what will you do to change that?

The Soul's Self-Ignorance as Proof of Human Limitation
Al-Razi argues that the soul's unknowability implies greater difficulty in knowing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's seventh argument: that the soul's inability to know itself demonstrates human limitation and points to the impossibility of knowing the Creator fully, thus supporting the need for revelation.

Core Idea
If the soul cannot know itself, how can it know its Creator?

Al-Razi presents a fascinating argument from the human soul's own self-ignorance. He points out that even though the soul is the most apparent thing to itself, it remains mysterious. If we cannot fully know our own essence, then knowing the Creator, who is entirely different from creation, is even more impossible. This argument challenges the rationalist claim that unaided reason can comprehend God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Razi begins by stating that intellects are confused about the soul's substance, which is ironic because the soul is the most apparent thing.
The passage is incomplete; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the two comparisons
First comparison: since the soul cannot know itself, it is even more unable to know the Creator. Second comparison: a person awake cannot perceive the unseen world, but when asleep they gain access to secrets.
These parallels reinforce the argument.
3
Reflect on the unstated conclusion
Ask yourself what Al-Razi intends us to conclude. If ordinary wakeful reason is limited, then full knowledge of God is beyond human intellect, and we must rely on revelation.
This argument supports the broader theme of human epistemic humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن العقول والأفهام اضطربت في معرفة جوهر النفس أنا بينا في أول هذا الكتاب : أنها أظهر المعلومات وأقربها إلى الإنسانية مع العقل والعلم والفهم . ولما عجزت النفس عن معرفة نفسها فلأن تعجز عن معرفة خالق الكل - مع أنه لا نزاع في كونه مخالفا لجميع المخلوقات بالحقيقة والماهية - كان أولى . بل نقول : الإنسان حال ما يكون يقظان فإنه لا يمكنه الوقوف على شيء من أحوال عالم الغيب . فإذا نام وزال عقله واستولت الغفلة على جسده قدر على الاتصال بعالم الغيب والاستفادة من تلك الأسرار . وذلك
The seventh argument: that intellects and understandings have been confused in knowing the substance of the soul. We showed at the beginning of this book that it is the most apparent of known things and the closest to humanity along with intellect, knowledge, and understanding. Yet when the soul is incapable of knowing itself, then for it to be incapable of knowing the Creator of all — especially given that there is no dispute that He is different from all creatures in reality and essence — is even more appropriate. Rather, we say: a person while awake cannot ascertain anything of the states of the unseen world. But when he sleeps and his intellect departs and heedlessness overtakes his body, he becomes able to connect with the unseen world and benefit from those secrets. And that…
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which is incomplete in the text. The author begins by noting that intellects have been perplexed about knowing the substance (جوهر) of the soul, despite it being the most apparent thing to humanity, as stated earlier in the book. If the soul, which is closest and most apparent, cannot fully know itself, then it is even more fitting (أولى) that it cannot know the Creator, who is undeniably different from all creatures in essence and reality. The author then gives an example: a person while awake cannot perceive the unseen world (عالم الغيب), but when asleep and the intellect is absent, he gains access to hidden secrets. This suggests that ordinary wakeful reasoning is limited, and full knowledge of the Creator is beyond normal human faculties. The argument is cut off at 'وذلك', but the intended conclusion is clear: just as the soul fails to know itself, it fails to know God, and this supports the exclusion of imagination in theology.

Key Terms
جوهر النفس
عالم الغيب
يقظان
نوم
الغفلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's closeness to us means it is easily known and transparent.
Correction: Al-Razi argues the opposite: despite being the most apparent and closest to us, the soul's substance remains a mystery, which shows that even what is 'nearest' can be unknowable, let alone the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh argument, what happens to a person when they sleep?
A) They become more rational.
B) They lose the ability to connect with the unseen.
C) They gain access to the unseen world and its secrets.
D) They become like animals.

Key Takeaway
The soul's self-ignorance is evidence of human epistemic limitation regarding God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot fully know your own soul, which is closer to you than anything, how can you claim to have grasped the reality of Allah? What does this demand of your humility in theology?
?
When you sleep and your intellect departs, you connect to the unseen. What does that reveal about the limitations of your everyday reasoning? How should that change the way you seek knowledge of Allah?
?
Are you willing to accept that your understanding of Allah is inherently limited, or do you secretly believe you can fully comprehend Him? How would embracing this limitation transform your worship and reliance on Allah?

Eighth Proof: The Limit of Perception
Razi's eighth proof uses self-reflexive limits of perception to argue for non-perceptible existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of perceiving powers that cannot perceive themselves demonstrates that existence is not confined to the perceptible or imaginable.

Core Idea
Not everything that exists can be sensed or imagined; existence is broader than perception.

Razi presents a simple yet profound argument: the very tools we use to perceive and imagine cannot perceive or imagine themselves. This shows that reality includes things beyond our immediate sensory and imaginative grasp. The argument paves the way for affirming a non-corporeal divine reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let Razi's own phrasing come through before you interpret.
2
Notice the self-reflexive limitation
The argument points out that the power of sight cannot see itself, and the imaginative power cannot imagine itself. Both powers exist but are not objects of their own operation.
This is a key logical observation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Since these powers exist yet are not perceived or imagined, existence is not limited to what can be sensed or imagined. This opens the door to affirming a non-corporeal reality.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنا نبصر الأشياء . إلا أن القوة الباصرة نفسها وكذلك القوة الخيالية تتخيل الأشياء إلا أن هذه القوة لا يمكنها أن تتخيل نفسها ، فوجود القوة الباصرة والقوة الخيالية يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون كل شيء محسوساً متخيلا .
The eighth proof: We see things. However, the power of sight itself, and likewise the imaginative power, imagines things, except that this power cannot imagine itself. So the existence of the power of sight and the imaginative power indicates that it is not necessary that everything be sensed or imagined.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) for the existence of a non-corporeal being. The argument begins by stating that we see things (objects) through the power of sight (al-quwwa al-basira). However, this very power of sight cannot see itself. Similarly, the imaginative power (al-quwwa al-khayaliyya) can imagine objects but cannot imagine itself. Since both powers exist and are not themselves sensed or imagined, this demonstrates that not everything that exists must be perceptible or imaginable. Therefore, there can be existents beyond the physical, making room for a non-corporeal reality.

Key Terms
القوة الباصرة
القوة الخيالية
محسوس
متخيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everything that exists must be perceptible or imaginable.
Correction: This argument shows that the powers of sight and imagination exist yet are not perceived or imagined by themselves, so existence is broader than perception.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what does the fact that the power of sight cannot see itself demonstrate?
A) The power of sight is imperfect.
B) Not everything that exists is perceptible or imaginable.
C) The power of sight is the only real thing.
D) Imagination is stronger than sight.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the powers of sight and imagination, though they cannot perceive themselves, proves that not all existents are perceptible or imaginable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even your own ability to see and imagine cannot be seen or imagined. How does that humble you when you realize how much of reality—including your own self—lies beyond your perception? What do you need to trust Allah for that you have been demanding to see first?
?
When you struggle with doubts about Allah because you cannot sense Him, does this proof challenge your assumption that seeing is believing? Write down one area of your life where you act as if only the visible matters, and commit to changing that perspective today.
?
If your very power of sight exists but is invisible, how much more must the Creator of that power exist beyond your senses? In what practical way will you increase your awareness of the unseen realities this week—such as through prayer, reflection, or seeking knowledge?

The Existence of Space as a Real Non-Corporeal Entity
Razi argues for the existence of space as a real entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that space (al-makan) is a real existent, not merely a mental construct, and that its reality points to a non-corporeal being.

Core Idea
Space is real, not imaginary, and itself not located in space.

Razi begins his ninth proof by asserting that space and time exist and are not confined to space or time. This immediately suggests a reality beyond physical dimensions. In this lesson, we examine his initial claim and the detailed demonstration that space—the void in which bodies exist—is an objective entity, independent of the mind. Understanding this foundation is key to grasping the broader proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first few sentences where Razi states that space and time exist and are not in space/time, leading to an existence not encompassed by them.
This is the thesis for the entire proof.
2
Follow the mountain example
Razi uses the example of a mountain moving from one place to another to show that the location remains and is distinct. Note how he argues that the location must be real because the transfer occurs objectively.
3
Consider the refutation of the mutakallimun
Razi dismisses the view that location is merely a mental assumption. Identify why he considers this view false.
The key is the objective transfer independent of mind.
4
Grasp the conclusion about space
Razi concludes that space (the void) is an existent and that it is not itself located in another space, avoiding infinite regress. This yields an existent not in a place.
This is the first part of the proof for a non-corporeal being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : أن نقول : المكان والزمان [ موجودان . ولم يحصلا البتة في المكان والزمان. فقد يثبت وجود لا يحيط به المكان والزمان (۱) ] فنفتقر ههنا إلى بيان أمرين : الأول : إن الزمان والمكان [ موجودان (۲) ] ونقول : أما المكان فالمراد منه الفضاء والخلاء الذي يحصل الجسم فيه . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا فرضنا أن الجبل قد انقلع من موضعه وانتقل بكليته إلى جانب آخر ، فإن المنتقل هو الجبل . وأما تلك الجهة التي كان الجبل حاصلا فيها فإنها لم تنتقل البتة . والعلم بذلك ضروري. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : تلك الجهة موجود من الموجودات . والدليل عليه : أن تلك الجهة مغايرة لما سواها من الجهات بالعدد وبالإشارة الحسية. ولذلك فإنا نقول : إن هذا الجبل انتقل من تلك الجهة إلى جهة أخرى ولولا أن الجهة الأولى مغايرة للجهة الثانية وإلا لكان انتقال الجبل من إحداهما إلى الأخرى محالا . والذي يقوله المتكلمون : إن الجهة والحيز لا وجود لها في أنفسها وإنما هي أمور يفرضها العقل ويقدرها الوهم . فهو كلام باطل . وذلك لأن هذا الانتقال حاصل في نفس الأمر ، سواء وجد العقل والذهن أو لم يوجد . وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون الجهة موجودة في نفسها سواء وجد الذهن أو لم يوجد . فثبت أن الفضاء والخلاء أمر موجود. وظاهر أنه ليس مكانا آخر ، وإلا لزم التسلسل . فقد حصل في الوجود موجود ليس حاصلا في المكان والحيز . وأما الزمان فهو أيضا موجود. والدليل عليه : هو أنا نقسمه إلى السنين ، ونقسم السنين إلى الشهور ، ونقسم الشهور إلى الأيام. ونقسم اليوم إلى الساعات . ونحكم بأن الساعة الواحدة أقل قدرا من اليوم، الذي هو أقل من الشهر . الذي هو أقل من السنة . والعدم المحض والنفي الصرف ، يمتنع كونه قابـلا للتقسيم إلى الأجزاء ، ويمتنع وصفه بكونه أقل من الأجزاء أو أكثر منها . فيثبت أنه شيء موجود . وذلك الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً أو حالا في المتحيز ، أولا متحيزا ولا حالا في المتحيز ويمتنع كونه متحيزا وإلا لكان شيء يكون أقرب إلى ذلك المتحيز ، كان أقرب إلى الزمان ، وكل ما كان أبعد عن ذلك المتحيز ، كان أبعد من الزمان . ومعلوم بالضرورة : أن ذلك باطل قطعا . لأن الأشياء الموجودة في هذا اليوم [ وليس بعضها في المفهوم من الحضور في هذا اليوم (۱) ] أكمل من بعض ، والعلم به ضروري. وبهذا الطريق ثبت أيضا : أنه يمتنع عرضا حالا في المتحيز ، وإذا بطل هذان القمان ، ثبت أن الزمان والمدة موجود من الموجودات مع أنه غير متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز . وذلك هو المطلوب . واعلم : أنك إذا تأملت ما لخصناه في مسألة الخلاء والمدة في هذا الكتاب ، ووقفت على مذاهب الناس فيهما ، ووقفت على أن الحق هو القول بإثبات الخلاء ، وعلى أن الحق هو أن المدة ليس بمتحيز ولا حركة ، ولكنه موجود مجرد . عرفت قطعا أن القول بإثبات موجود مجرد عن الجسمية : أمر واجب الاعتراف به . وهذا الوجه أقوى الوجوه المذكورة في هذا الباب
The ninth proof: We say: Space and time exist, and they are not at all located in space and time. Thus an existence not encompassed by space and time can be established. So we need here to clarify two matters. The first: that space and time exist. We say: As for space, what is meant is the void and emptiness in which the body occurs. The evidence for it: that if we suppose that a mountain has been uprooted from its place and transferred entirely to another side, then what is transferred is the mountain. But that side in which the mountain was occurring did not transfer at all. Knowledge of this is necessary. If this is established, we say: That side is one of the existents. The evidence for it: that that side is distinct from other sides in number and by sensory indication. Therefore we say: Indeed this mountain transferred from that side to another side, and were it not that the first side is distinct from the second side, then the transfer of the mountain from one to the other would be impossible. And what the mutakallimun say—that side and location have no existence in themselves but are matters that the intellect assumes and the imagination reckons—is false speech. That is because this transfer occurs in reality, whether the intellect and mind exist or not. And if so, then the side must exist in itself whether the mind exists or not. So it is established that the void and emptiness are an existing thing. And it is apparent that it is not another place, otherwise there would be infinite regress. Thus there has occurred in existence an existent that is not occurring in place and location. As for time, it also exists. The evidence for it: that we divide it into years, and we divide years into months, and we divide months into days, and we divide the day into hours. And we judge that one hour is less in magnitude than the day, which is less than the month, which is less than the year. Pure non-existence and absolute negation cannot be susceptible to division into parts, and cannot be described as being less than parts or greater than them. So it is established that it is an existing thing. That existing thing is either corporeal or an accident in a corporeal, or neither corporeal nor an accident in a corporeal. And it is impossible that it be corporeal, because then something nearer to that corporeal would be nearer to time, and whatever is farther from that corporeal would be farther from time. And it is known necessarily that that is absolutely false. Because the things existing in this day—and some of them are not in the concept of being present in this day—are more complete than others, and knowledge of this is necessary. And by this method it is also established that it is impossible that it be an accident in a corporeal. And when these two possibilities are invalidated, it is established that time and duration are an existent among existents while being neither corporeal nor an accident in a corporeal. That is what is sought. And know that if you contemplate what we have summarized in the matter of the void and duration in this book, and come to know the positions of people concerning them, and come to know that the truth is the statement affirming the void, and that the truth is that duration is neither corporeal nor motion, but it is a pure existent, you will certainly know that the statement affirming an existent abstracted from corporeality is a matter that must be acknowledged. And this aspect is the strongest of the aspects mentioned in this chapter.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof (hujja) for the existence of a non-corporeal being, divided into two main sections: demonstrating the existence of space (al-makan) and time (al-zaman) as independent realities that are not themselves located in space or time. The argument begins by stating that space and time exist and are not confined to space or time, which directly points to an existence not limited by them. To prove space, the author uses the example of a mountain moving from one place to another: the location that the mountain vacated remains and is distinct from the mountain itself. This location is not transferred; it is a real entity. This refutes the claim of some theologians (al-mutakallimun) that location is merely an intellectual construct. The author argues that because the transfer occurs objectively, the location must exist objectively. If the location were only mental, the transfer would not be real. Thus, space is an existent that is not itself in a place. Similarly, for time, the author argues that we divide time into years, months, days, and hours. Non-existence cannot be divided, so time must exist. Next, he examines the nature of this existing time: it cannot be a body (muttahayyiz), because if it were, proximity to that body would affect time, which is false; nor can it be an accident (hāl al-muttahayyiz), because accidents are contingent. Therefore, time is a non-corporeal existent. The final paragraph serves as a summary and confirmation, stating that this argument is the strongest in the chapter and that affirming a non-corporeal being is necessary.

Key Terms
المكان
الخلاء
الفضاء
الجهة
الحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Space and location are merely mental constructs with no objective reality.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the transfer of a mountain between distinct locations is objectively real, proving that locations exist independently of the mind.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to prove the objective existence of space?
A) A bird flying from branch to branch.
B) A mountain moving from one side to another.
C) Water flowing from a jug.
D) A person walking across a room.

Key Takeaway
Space (the void) is a real, objective existent, not a mental construct, and it is not itself in a place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the empty space around you right now, do you treat it as a mere void of no significance, or do you recognize it as a real existent created by Allah? How does this shift your awareness of Allah's creation in the seemingly 'empty' parts of your life?
?
If space is real and independent of your mind, what does that tell you about the Creator who brought it into existence? Are you living as if the unseen realities—like Allah's knowledge and decree—are as certain as the space you occupy?
?
Razi proves that even the most basic realm of space is not a mental illusion but an objective truth. Where in your life do you ignore the objective reality of Allah's signs because they seem 'imaginary' or 'abstract' to you? What would change if you took them as seriously as you take the space in front of you?

Time as a Non-Corporeal Existent and the Proof's Conclusion
Razi proves time's existence and establishes it as a non-corporeal entity, completing the ninth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that time is a real existent, its nature as neither corporeal nor an accident, and how this completes the argument for a non-corporeal being.

Core Idea
Time exists, is divisible, and is neither a body nor an accident.

Having established the reality of space, Razi turns to time. He argues that because we can meaningfully divide time into years, months, and days, time must be a real thing—nonexistence cannot be divided. He then examines what kind of existent time is and eliminates the possibilities that it is a body or an accident, concluding that it is a non-corporeal simple entity. This final part of the proof directly supports the existence of a being abstracted from matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the case for time's existence
Pay attention to Razi's reasoning: time can be divided into parts, and judgments of 'more' and 'less' apply, which cannot be true of nothingness. Therefore, time exists.
This is a common argument: divisibility implies reality.
2
Identify the three possibilities for the nature of time
Razi lists what time could be: corporeal (muttahayyiz), an accident in a corporeal, or neither. Note how he systematically eliminates the first two.
This is a classic three-fold classification.
3
Understand why time cannot be corporeal
If time were a body, then proximity to that body would affect time—nonsense. Therefore, time is not a body.
4
See why time cannot be an accident
By the same reasoning, time cannot be an accident in a body because that would cause similar absurdities.
5
Grasp the conclusion and the overall proof
Razi concludes that time is a non-corporeal existent, and together with space, this proves a being abstracted from corporeality. Read the final paragraph's emphasis on this being the strongest proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : أن نقول : المكان والزمان [ موجودان . ولم يحصلا البتة في المكان والزمان. فقد يثبت وجود لا يحيط به المكان والزمان (۱) ] فنفتقر ههنا إلى بيان أمرين : الأول : إن الزمان والمكان [ موجودان (۲) ] ونقول : أما المكان فالمراد منه الفضاء والخلاء الذي يحصل الجسم فيه . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا فرضنا أن الجبل قد انقلع من موضعه وانتقل بكليته إلى جانب آخر ، فإن المنتقل هو الجبل . وأما تلك الجهة التي كان الجبل حاصلا فيها فإنها لم تنتقل البتة . والعلم بذلك ضروري. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : تلك الجهة موجود من الموجودات . والدليل عليه : أن تلك الجهة مغايرة لما سواها من الجهات بالعدد وبالإشارة الحسية. ولذلك فإنا نقول : إن هذا الجبل انتقل من تلك الجهة إلى جهة أخرى ولولا أن الجهة الأولى مغايرة للجهة الثانية وإلا لكان انتقال الجبل من إحداهما إلى الأخرى محالا . والذي يقوله المتكلمون : إن الجهة والحيز لا وجود لها في أنفسها وإنما هي أمور يفرضها العقل ويقدرها الوهم . فهو كلام باطل . وذلك لأن هذا الانتقال حاصل في نفس الأمر ، سواء وجد العقل والذهن أو لم يوجد . وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون الجهة موجودة في نفسها سواء وجد الذهن أو لم يوجد . فثبت أن الفضاء والخلاء أمر موجود. وظاهر أنه ليس مكانا آخر ، وإلا لزم التسلسل . فقد حصل في الوجود موجود ليس حاصلا في المكان والحيز . وأما الزمان فهو أيضا موجود. والدليل عليه : هو أنا نقسمه إلى السنين ، ونقسم السنين إلى الشهور ، ونقسم الشهور إلى الأيام. ونقسم اليوم إلى الساعات . ونحكم بأن الساعة الواحدة أقل قدرا من اليوم، الذي هو أقل من الشهر . الذي هو أقل من السنة . والعدم المحض والنفي الصرف ، يمتنع كونه قابـلا للتقسيم إلى الأجزاء ، ويمتنع وصفه بكونه أقل من الأجزاء أو أكثر منها . فيثبت أنه شيء موجود . وذلك الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً أو حالا في المتحيز ، أولا متحيزا ولا حالا في المتحيز ويمتنع كونه متحيزا وإلا لكان شيء يكون أقرب إلى ذلك المتحيز ، كان أقرب إلى الزمان ، وكل ما كان أبعد عن ذلك المتحيز ، كان أبعد من الزمان . ومعلوم بالضرورة : أن ذلك باطل قطعا . لأن الأشياء الموجودة في هذا اليوم [ وليس بعضها في المفهوم من الحضور في هذا اليوم (۱) ] أكمل من بعض ، والعلم به ضروري. وبهذا الطريق ثبت أيضا : أنه يمتنع عرضا حالا في المتحيز ، وإذا بطل هذان القمان ، ثبت أن الزمان والمدة موجود من الموجودات مع أنه غير متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز . وذلك هو المطلوب . واعلم : أنك إذا تأملت ما لخصناه في مسألة الخلاء والمدة في هذا الكتاب ، ووقفت على مذاهب الناس فيهما ، ووقفت على أن الحق هو القول بإثبات الخلاء ، وعلى أن الحق هو أن المدة ليس بمتحيز ولا حركة ، ولكنه موجود مجرد . عرفت قطعا أن القول بإثبات موجود مجرد عن الجسمية : أمر واجب الاعتراف به . وهذا الوجه أقوى الوجوه المذكورة في هذا الباب
The ninth proof: We say: Space and time exist, and they are not at all located in space and time. Thus an existence not encompassed by space and time can be established. So we need here to clarify two matters. The first: that space and time exist. We say: As for space, what is meant is the void and emptiness in which the body occurs. The evidence for it: that if we suppose that a mountain has been uprooted from its place and transferred entirely to another side, then what is transferred is the mountain. But that side in which the mountain was occurring did not transfer at all. Knowledge of this is necessary. If this is established, we say: That side is one of the existents. The evidence for it: that that side is distinct from other sides in number and by sensory indication. Therefore we say: Indeed this mountain transferred from that side to another side, and were it not that the first side is distinct from the second side, then the transfer of the mountain from one to the other would be impossible. And what the mutakallimun say—that side and location have no existence in themselves but are matters that the intellect assumes and the imagination reckons—is false speech. That is because this transfer occurs in reality, whether the intellect and mind exist or not. And if so, then the side must exist in itself whether the mind exists or not. So it is established that the void and emptiness are an existing thing. And it is apparent that it is not another place, otherwise there would be infinite regress. Thus there has occurred in existence an existent that is not occurring in place and location. As for time, it also exists. The evidence for it: that we divide it into years, and we divide years into months, and we divide months into days, and we divide the day into hours. And we judge that one hour is less in magnitude than the day, which is less than the month, which is less than the year. Pure non-existence and absolute negation cannot be susceptible to division into parts, and cannot be described as being less than parts or greater than them. So it is established that it is an existing thing. That existing thing is either corporeal or an accident in a corporeal, or neither corporeal nor an accident in a corporeal. And it is impossible that it be corporeal, because then something nearer to that corporeal would be nearer to time, and whatever is farther from that corporeal would be farther from time. And it is known necessarily that that is absolutely false. Because the things existing in this day—and some of them are not in the concept of being present in this day—are more complete than others, and knowledge of this is necessary. And by this method it is also established that it is impossible that it be an accident in a corporeal. And when these two possibilities are invalidated, it is established that time and duration are an existent among existents while being neither corporeal nor an accident in a corporeal. That is what is sought. And know that if you contemplate what we have summarized in the matter of the void and duration in this book, and come to know the positions of people concerning them, and come to know that the truth is the statement affirming the void, and that the truth is that duration is neither corporeal nor motion, but it is a pure existent, you will certainly know that the statement affirming an existent abstracted from corporeality is a matter that must be acknowledged. And this aspect is the strongest of the aspects mentioned in this chapter.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof (hujja) for the existence of a non-corporeal being, divided into two main sections: demonstrating the existence of space (al-makan) and time (al-zaman) as independent realities that are not themselves located in space or time. The argument begins by stating that space and time exist and are not confined to space or time, which directly points to an existence not limited by them. To prove space, the author uses the example of a mountain moving from one place to another: the location that the mountain vacated remains and is distinct from the mountain itself. This location is not transferred; it is a real entity. This refutes the claim of some theologians (al-mutakallimun) that location is merely an intellectual construct. The author argues that because the transfer occurs objectively, the location must exist objectively. If the location were only mental, the transfer would not be real. Thus, space is an existent that is not itself in a place. Similarly, for time, the author argues that we divide time into years, months, days, and hours. Non-existence cannot be divided, so time must exist. Next, he examines the nature of this existing time: it cannot be a body (muttahayyiz), because if it were, proximity to that body would affect time, which is false; nor can it be an accident (hāl al-muttahayyiz), because accidents are contingent. Therefore, time is a non-corporeal existent. The final paragraph serves as a summary and confirmation, stating that this argument is the strongest in the chapter and that affirming a non-corporeal being is necessary.

Key Terms
الزمان
المدة
متحيز
حال في المتحيز
موجود مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is merely a relation between events or a property of motion, without independent existence.
Correction: Razi argues that time's divisibility and comparability prove its reality as an entity distinct from bodies and accidents.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi conclude that time cannot be a body?
A) Because bodies are too large.
B) Because if time were a body, then proximity to that body would affect time, which is absurd.
C) Because bodies are divisible and time is not.
D) Because bodies are created and time is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Time is a real, non-corporeal existent, and together with the reality of space, this proves the existence of a being abstracted from matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about time, do you treat it as a mere measurement or as a real entity created by Allah? How would your awareness of time as a non-corporeal existent change the way you spend each hour, knowing it is not just a concept but something real that you can never get back?
?
Razi argues that time cannot be a body or accident, so it must be a abstract existent. If time is abstract and from Allah's creation, what does that tell you about the Creator who is beyond time? How should that awe affect your daily prayer and consciousness of His presence?
?
The passage concludes that affirming an abstract existent leads to affirming a being abstracted from matter—Allah. Have you ever used this rational argument personally to strengthen your certainty in the unseen? If not, what is holding you back from internalizing it and letting it transform your faith?

The Tenth Proof: A Dichotomy of Co-Existing Beings
Razi introduces a proof based on the two possible relationships between co-existing beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of Razi's tenth proof, which lays out two possible relationships between any two coexisting beings.

Core Idea
The tenth proof begins with a dichotomy: one being either permeates the other or is separate from it.

Razi here begins his tenth argument against the corporist view. He presents the starting point that the corporist himself would accept: any two coexisting entities must relate in one of two ways. This sets the stage for a reductio argument that will lead to the denial of divine corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single sentence presenting a dichotomy.
2
Identify the two relationships
The passage lists two possible relationships between two coexisting beings. Find each one and note the example given for the first.
The first is permeation (saryān) like an accident in a body; the second is separation by direction (mubāyana bi-jiha).
3
Consider the dialectical context
Think about why Razi attributes this premise to the corporist (al-mujassim). How might this premise be used to argue against corporeality?
The argument continues on the next page, but you can already see that the dichotomy might be incomplete for a non-corporeal being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن المجسم يقول : كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر، سريان العرض في الجسم، أو يكون مباينا عنه بجهة
The tenth proof: The corporist (al-mujassim) says: Every two existents must be such that one of them permeates the other, like the permeation of an accident in a body, or it is separate from it by a direction.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage begins the tenth proof (hujja). It quotes the position of the 'mujassim' (those who attribute a body to God, often used pejoratively). The mujassim states that for any two existing things, one of two possibilities must hold: either one permeates (sārī) the other in the way an accident (ʿaraḍ) permeates a body, or they are separate (mubāyin) from each other by a direction (jiha). This is a dichotomy that the mujassim would accept. The argument continues on the next page to use this premise to deduce a necessary conclusion about a being that is neither permeating nor separate in a direction—namely, a non-corporeal existent. However, only the premise is present in this window. The full argument would then show that such a being must exist.

Key Terms
مجسم
ساريا
مباينا
جهة
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'mujassim' refers only to a historical sect that believed God has a body.
Correction: While historically associated with certain anthropomorphist groups, in this context 'mujassim' is used pejoratively for anyone who affirms a body for God. The argument uses their own dichotomy to refute them.

Checkpoint
According to the beginning of the tenth proof, how many possible relationships are listed between two coexisting beings?
A) One.
B) Two.
C) Three.
D) Four.

Key Takeaway
The tenth proof begins with a premise that for any two coexisting beings, the only possibilities are permeation or separation by direction, setting up a logical trap for the corporist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The corporist assumes that every two existents must be either one permeating the other or separate by direction. Pause and ask yourself: In your daily life, do you unconsciously limit Allah by assuming He must exist like other beings? What specific example comes to mind where you treated His existence as comparable to yours? Commit to noticing this assumption and replacing it with the understanding that Allah is beyond such categories.
?
The passage quotes the corporist's premise as their starting point. Do you ever fall into the trap of thinking that Allah must fit into a human framework of existence? How does realizing that this dichotomy is not absolute change the way you relate to Him? What step will you take today to remind yourself that Allah's existence is unique and incomparable?

The Opponents' Threefold Division and Its Flaw
Presents the opponents' first argument: a threefold division about God creating the world inside, outside, or neither, claiming the third is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' argument that divides creation into three spatial possibilities and why it falsely assumes that spatial categories apply to God.

Core Idea
The opponents' threefold division falsely assumes God exists in space.

This lesson introduces the first of three expressions used by opponents to argue that the premise 'no existent is neither inside nor outside the world' is self-evident. They pose a threefold division: either God created the world within His essence, outside it, or neither. The passage presents their reasoning leading to the conclusion that the third option is absurd. Understanding this argument is crucial because the author will refute it by denying that spatial categories apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the threefold division presented by the opponents.
Notice that the passage begins with 'فالعبارة الأولى' indicating this is the first of multiple expressions.
2
Identify the three options
The passage enumerates three possibilities: creation in God's essence, outside His essence, or neither. Write them down.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each option
For option one (in the essence), note the rejection due to mixing with impure bodies. For option two (outside), note that it implies separation by space and direction. For option three (neither), note that they declare it intellectually unacceptable.
The opponents accept option two as the correct one.
4
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Ask yourself: what assumption must be made for the third option to be considered absurd? The opponents assume that spatial categories (inside/outside) apply to God. The author will later deny this assumption.
This is key to understanding the refutation later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعبارة الأولى : قالوا : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته أولا في ذاته ولا خارج ذاته ؟ والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن تكون ذاته مخالطة لهذه الاجسام المستقذرة. والثاني يوجب القول بأنه تعالى مباين للعالم بالحيز والجهة . وأما الثالث وهو أنه تعالى خلق هذا العالم لا في ذاته ولا خارج ذاته فهذا . قول لا يقبله العقل البتة فكان باطلا
The first expression: They said: Did He, exalted, create the world in His essence or outside His essence, or neither in His essence nor outside His essence? The first is false, otherwise His essence would be mixed with these filthy bodies. The second necessitates the statement that He, exalted, is separate from the world by space and direction. As for the third, that He, exalted, created this world neither in His essence nor outside His essence, this is a statement that the intellect does not accept at all, so it is false.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three expressions used by opponents who claim that the premise 'no existent is neither inside nor outside the world' is self-evident. They pose a threefold division: either God created the world within His essence, or outside it, or neither. They reject the first because it would entail His essence being mixed with impure bodies; they accept the second, which implies separation by space and direction; and they declare the third to be intellectually unacceptable and therefore false. The author is here reporting the opponents' argument, not endorsing it; he will refute it in later lessons.

Key Terms
الذات
المخالطة
المباينة
الحيز
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the third option (neither inside nor outside) is indeed absurd.
Correction: This conclusion only follows if one assumes that spatial categories (inside/outside) apply to God, which the author denies.

Checkpoint
According to the first expression, what is the conclusion about God creating the world neither in His essence nor outside it?
A) It is the most reasonable option.
B) It is impossible and intellectually unacceptable.
C) It implies God is separate by space.
D) It leads to mixing of essences.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' argument that the third option is absurd only holds if we assume God is subject to spatial categories, which the author denies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God as being 'somewhere' — like above the heavens or outside the universe? Where did you get that idea, and how does this lesson's warning about applying spatial categories to God challenge you to rethink it? This week, when you pray, consciously remind yourself that God is not confined to any place.
?
The opponents considered the third option 'intellectually unacceptable.' Are there beliefs you hold about God simply because they seem 'obvious' to your mind, without realizing they rest on assumptions you've never examined? What would it cost you to let go of those assumptions and accept that God transcends even your most basic categories?
?
If God is not inside the world nor outside it in a spatial sense, how does that affect your sense of closeness to Him? Do you feel distant because you cannot locate Him, or does this truth invite you to seek a different kind of nearness? Commit today to asking Allah to increase your awareness of His presence without confining Him to space.

Main Syllogism: Necessary Being is Not Spatial
Opening syllogism of the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the main syllogism that proves the Necessary Existent is not spatial, and why the minor premise is self-evident while the major requires further justification.

Core Idea
The first proof is a simple syllogism: no necessary being is contingent; all spatial things are contingent; therefore no necessary being is spatial.

The author begins with the most direct argument: if we can show that everything spatial is contingent, and the Necessary Existent is not contingent, then the Necessary Existent cannot be spatial. This initial syllogism sets the stage for deeper reasoning about composition and contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism
Read the first two sentences which state the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion. Note the logical form.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure.
2
Identify the premises
Locate the minor premise (self-evident) and the major premise (needs proof). Understand why the minor is self-evident.
3
Ask: What needs justification?
Reflect on the major premise: 'every spatial thing is possible by essence.' This is not obvious and requires proof, which the author provides in the following arguments.
The rest of the passage justifies this premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : لا شيء من واجب الوجود لذاته بممكن الوجود لذاته ، وكل متحيز فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته، ينتج فلا شيء من واجب الوجود لذاته بمتحيز. أما الصغرى فبديهية ، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل متحيز مركب ، وكل مركب ممكن لذاته، ينتج : أن كل متحيز ممكن لذاته. وإنما قلنا : إن كل متحيز مركب لوجوه : الأول : إن كل متحيز فإن يمينه مغاير ليساره ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو مركب ، ينتج : أن كل متحيز مركب . وتمام القول فيه مقرر بالدلائل المذكورة في نفي الجوهر الفرد . والثاني : قالت الفلاسفة : كل جسم فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة . الثالث : كل متحيز فإنه يشارك سائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً ، ويخالفها بتعينه ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فوجب أن يكون كل فرد من أفراد المتحيزات مركباً من عموم التحيز، الذي به المشاركة ، ومن ذلك التعين الذي به المخالفة. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن كل متحيز مركب . أما بيان أن كل مركب فهو ممكن . فلأن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى حيزه ، وحيزه غيره ، فكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى غيره ، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره ، فهو ممكن لذاته ، ينتج أن كل مركب ممكن لذاته . فقد بان : أن كل جسم مركب ، وينتج أن كل جسم ممكن لذاته، فيثبت : أنه لا شيء من واجب الوجود ممكن لذاته. فيثبت : أن كل جسم ممكن لذاته ، ينتج من واجب الوجود لذاته بجسم . وهو المطلوب.
The first proof: Nothing that is necessary of existence by essence is possible of existence by essence, and every spatial thing is possible of existence by essence; therefore, nothing that is necessary of existence by essence is spatial. As for the minor premise, it is self-evident. As for the major premise, because every spatial thing is composite, and every composite is possible by essence, it follows that every spatial thing is possible by essence. We say that every spatial thing is composite for several reasons: First, every spatial thing has a right side distinct from its left, and everything that has that is composite; thus every spatial thing is composite. The full discussion of this is established by the proofs mentioned in the refutation of the indivisible atom. Second, the philosophers said: every body is composed of matter and form. Third, every spatial thing participates with other spatial things in being spatial, and differs from them by its individuation, and that by which it participates is not that by which it differs; therefore each individual of spatial things must be composed of the generality of spatiality (by which it participates) and that individuation (by which it differs). By these three reasons it is established that every spatial thing is composite. As for the explanation that every composite is possible: because every composite is dependent on its space, and its space is other than it; thus every composite is dependent on something other than itself, and everything dependent on something other than itself is possible by essence; it follows that every composite is possible by essence. Thus it is clear that every body is composite, and it follows that every body is possible by essence. Therefore it is established that nothing of the Necessary Existent by essence is possible by essence; rather, every body is possible by essence; it follows that the Necessary Existent by essence is not a body. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) that God is free from corporeality and being spatial. It is a categorical syllogism: the major premise states that no Necessary Existent is contingent; the minor premise states that every spatial thing is contingent; the conclusion is that no Necessary Existent is spatial. The author then justifies the minor premise by showing that every spatial thing (mutahayyiz) is composite (murakkab), and every composite is contingent. Three arguments are given for why every spatial thing is composite: (1) A spatial thing has distinguishable right and left parts, implying composition. (2) The philosophers' view that every body is composed of matter and form. (3) The argument from participation and differentiation: every spatial thing shares spatiality with others but differs by individuation, so it is composed of a common factor and a specific factor. Next, the author proves that every composite is contingent: because it depends on its space, which is other than itself, and dependence on another implies possibility (since the necessary is independent). Combining these, the proof concludes that the Necessary Existent cannot be a body (jism). The reasoning is tightly logical and foundational for Islamic theology's rejection of anthropomorphism.

Correction: The author immediately provides justification: every spatial thing is composite (to be proved next) and every composite is contingent.

?

Three Reasons: Why Every Spatial Thing is Composite
Three proofs that every spatial thing is composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct arguments used to prove that every spatial thing (mutahayyiz) must be composite (murakkab).

Core Idea
Every spatial thing is composite because it has distinguishable parts, is made of matter and form, or combines common and specific aspects.

To prove that every spatial thing is contingent, the author must first show it is composite. He gives three reasons: the right-left distinction, the philosophers' matter-form composition, and the participation-individuation argument. These cover physical, metaphysical, and logical perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Read the three reasons
Read each reason carefully. Notice that the first uses right/left, the second cites the philosophers, and the third uses a logical argument about participation and individuation.
2
Analyze the first reason
The right side is different from the left side; having distinct parts implies composition. This is the simplest argument.
It depends on the existence of spatial dimensions.
3
Analyze the second reason
The philosophers (Aristotelian) hold that every body is composed of matter (hyle) and form (form). This is a metaphysical claim.
4
Analyze the third reason
Every spatial thing shares 'spatiality' with others but differs by its specific individuation. The shared and unique aspects are separate principles, so it is composite.
This is a logical argument based on the one-over-many.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : لا شيء من واجب الوجود لذاته بممكن الوجود لذاته ، وكل متحيز فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته، ينتج فلا شيء من واجب الوجود لذاته بمتحيز. أما الصغرى فبديهية ، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل متحيز مركب ، وكل مركب ممكن لذاته، ينتج : أن كل متحيز ممكن لذاته. وإنما قلنا : إن كل متحيز مركب لوجوه : الأول : إن كل متحيز فإن يمينه مغاير ليساره ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو مركب ، ينتج : أن كل متحيز مركب . وتمام القول فيه مقرر بالدلائل المذكورة في نفي الجوهر الفرد . والثاني : قالت الفلاسفة : كل جسم فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة . الثالث : كل متحيز فإنه يشارك سائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً ، ويخالفها بتعينه ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فوجب أن يكون كل فرد من أفراد المتحيزات مركباً من عموم التحيز، الذي به المشاركة ، ومن ذلك التعين الذي به المخالفة. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن كل متحيز مركب . أما بيان أن كل مركب فهو ممكن . فلأن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى حيزه ، وحيزه غيره ، فكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى غيره ، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره ، فهو ممكن لذاته ، ينتج أن كل مركب ممكن لذاته . فقد بان : أن كل جسم مركب ، وينتج أن كل جسم ممكن لذاته، فيثبت : أنه لا شيء من واجب الوجود ممكن لذاته. فيثبت : أن كل جسم ممكن لذاته ، ينتج من واجب الوجود لذاته بجسم . وهو المطلوب.
The first proof: Nothing that is necessary of existence by essence is possible of existence by essence, and every spatial thing is possible of existence by essence; therefore, nothing that is necessary of existence by essence is spatial. As for the minor premise, it is self-evident. As for the major premise, because every spatial thing is composite, and every composite is possible by essence, it follows that every spatial thing is possible by essence. We say that every spatial thing is composite for several reasons: First, every spatial thing has a right side distinct from its left, and everything that has that is composite; thus every spatial thing is composite. As for the explanation that every composite is possible: because every composite is dependent on its space, and its space is other than it; thus every composite is dependent on something other than itself, and everything dependent on something other than itself is possible by essence; it follows that every composite is possible by essence. Thus it is clear that every body is composite, and it follows that every body is possible by essence. Therefore it is established that nothing of the Necessary Existent by essence is possible by essence; rather, every body is possible by essence; it follows that the Necessary Existent by essence is not a body. From Composite to Contingent: Final Conclusion
Proof that every composite is contingent and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every composite thing is contingent (possible by essence) and how this leads to the final conclusion that the Necessary Existent is not a body.

Core Idea
Every composite depends on its parts or space, and dependence on another entails contingency, so the self-sufficient Necessary Being cannot be composite.

After establishing that all spatial things are composite, the author now proves that every composite is contingent. This final link completes the argument that the Necessary Existent is not a body, because bodies are spatial and thus contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for composite to contingent
The passage says every composite is dependent on its space, and that space is other than itself. Read the full reasoning carefully.
2
Identify the key steps
Note the chain: composite → dependent on space → dependent on something else → possible by essence. Understand why dependence implies possibility.
The Necessary Existent is independent by definition.
3
Connect to the overall proof
Combine with previous lessons: spatial → composite → contingent → not necessary. Therefore the Necessary Existent cannot be spatial or a body.

Third Proof: Every Body is Composite and Contingent
Author presents the third proof that every body is composite and thus possible, so God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third proof that the Necessary Being cannot be a body, because every spatial thing is composite and every composite is contingent.

Core Idea
Spatial things are composite; composites are contingent; thus God is not spatial.

This passage presents a rigorous rational proof that the Necessary Being cannot be a body. The argument proceeds in two stages: first showing that anything occupying space (mutahayyiz) is inherently composite, and then proving that whatever is composite is contingent. Since God is necessarily existent, He cannot be composite or spatial.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall strategy
The proof aims to show God is not a body by establishing two premises: (1) every body is composite, and (2) every composite is contingent. Since God is necessary, He cannot be a body.
2
Analyze the first sub-proof (composition)
Read how the author argues that every spatial thing (mutahayyiz) is composite: it shares spatiality with other spatial things but differs by its specific determination (ta‘ayyun). The common and specific elements are distinct, so the thing is composed of both.
This uses the principle of composition from universal and particular.
3
Analyze the second sub-proof (contingency)
Notice why every composite is contingent: a composite depends on its parts, specifically its space, which is other than itself. Dependence on another entails possibility (contingency).
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the two sub-proofs: bodies are spatial, so they are composite and therefore contingent. Since the Necessary Being is not contingent, it cannot be a body. This is the required conclusion.
The argument is a valid syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : كل متحيز . فإنه يشارك سائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً ، ويخالفها بتعينه ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فوجب أن يكون كل فرد من أفراد المتحيزات مركباً من عموم التحيز، الذي به المشاركة ، ومن ذلك التعين الذي به المخالفة. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن كل متحيز مركب . أما بيان أن كل مركب فهو ممكن . فلأن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى حيزه ، وحيزه غيره ، فكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى غيره ، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره ، فهو ممكن لذاته ، ينتج أن كل مركب ممكن لذاته . فقد بان : أن كل جسم مركب ، وينتج أن كل جسم ممكن لذاته، فيثبت : أنه لا شيء من واجب الوجود ممكن لذاته. فيثبت : أن كل جسم ممكن لذاته ، ينتج من واجب الوجود لذاته بجسم . وهو المطلوب
The third: Every thing occupying space (mutahayyiz). For it participates with other spatial things in being spatial, and differs from them by its specific determination (ta‘ayyun). That by which it participates is other than that by which it differs. Therefore, every individual of spatial things must be composed of the generality of spatiality (tahayyuz), by which it participates, and of that determination by which it differs. Thus by these three aspects it is established that every spatial thing is composite. As for the demonstration that every composite is possible (mumkin): because every composite is dependent on its space, and its space is other than it, so every composite is dependent on another, and everything dependent on another is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Hence every composite is possible in itself. It has become clear: that every body (jism) is composite, and it follows that every body is possible in itself. Thus it is established: that nothing of the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) is possible in itself. Thus it is established: that every body is possible in itself, and it follows that the Necessary Being in itself is not a body. That is what was required (al-matlab).
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a complete proof (the third of a series) demonstrating that the Necessary Being cannot be a body. The author first defines a 'spatial thing' (mutahayyiz) as anything occupying space. He argues that every spatial thing is composite because it shares a common property (spatiality) with other spatial things but differs from them by a specific determination (ta‘ayyun). Since the common element and the specific element are distinct, the thing is composed of both. This argument uses the principle of composition from universal and particular. Then the author proves that every composite is possible (contingent): a composite depends on its parts, especially its space, which is external to it; therefore it is dependent on another, and dependence on another entails possibility (contingency) in itself. Consequently, every body (which is spatial) is composite and thus possible. Since the Necessary Being is by definition not possible, it cannot be a body. The conclusion follows logically: the Necessary Being is not a body.

Key Terms
متحيز
تعين
مركب
ممكن
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition alone directly implies contingency without considering dependence on parts.
Correction: The argument shows composition leads to contingency through dependence on the space/parts which are other than the composite.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why is every spatial thing (mutahayyiz) considered composite?
A) Because it is dependent on its place.
B) Because it has both a common element (spatiality) and a specific determination.
C) Because it is possible.
D) Because it is created.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be a body because bodies are composite and thus contingent, while the Necessary Being is necessarily existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God is not a body, He is not confined to any direction or place. The Logical Proof of Essential Similarity
Author enumerates and refutes four logical possibilities to prove essential similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reasoning establishing that if God were a body, He would share the same complete essence as all other bodies.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would be identical in essence to all bodies.

This passage presents the core of the second proof: a detailed logical analysis showing that assuming God is a body leads to the conclusion that He is identical in essence to all bodies. The author systematically examines four possible relationships between the common property (spatiality) and any supposed differentiating property, eliminating three of them as absurd, leaving only the conclusion of absolute similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'الحجة الثانية' to 'في تمام الماهية والذات' once. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
This is a dense logical text; take your time.
2
Identify the four possibilities
The author lists four possible relationships between the common property (spatiality) and the differentiating property. List them in your own words.
3
Understand the refutation of the first three
For each of the first three possibilities, the author gives a reason why they are impossible. Try to paraphrase each refutation and see why it fails.
The refutation of the third possibility involves a subtle point about spatial subsistence.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that spatiality is the essence and difference is an attribute, implying all spatial things share the same essence. Confirm this conclusion.
This conclusion is the basis for the next part of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان متحيزاً لكان مثلاً لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية وهذا محال فذاك محال . بيان الأول : أنه لو كان متحيزاً ، لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً . ثم بعد هذا لا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه يخالف سائر الأجسام في شيء من مقومات ماهيته ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، والأول باطل ، فيبقى الثاني . وإنما قلنا : إن الأول باطل ، لأنه إذا كان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً ، ومخالفاً لها في شيء من مقومات تلك الماهية . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فكان عموم كونه متحيزاً مغايراً لتلك الخصوصية التي وقعت بها المخالفة . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذان الأمران إما أن يكون كل واحد منهما صفة للآخر وإما أن لا يكون كل واحد منهما صفة للآخر . وإما أن يكون ما به المخالفة موصوفاً ، والمتحيز الذي به المشاركة صفة . وإما أن يكون بالعكس منه ، وهو أن يكون المتحيز موصوفاً ، وما به المخالفة يكون صفة . والأقسام الثلاثة الأولى باطلة ، فبقي الرابع. وذلك يفيد القول بأن الأجسام متماثلة في تمام الماهية . وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل ، لأن ذلك يقتضي : أن يكون كل واحد منها ذاتا مستقلة بنفسها ، ومع ذلك فيكون صفة مفتقرة إلى غيرها ، وذلك باطل . وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الثاني باطل ، لأن على هذا التقدير يكون كل واحد منها ذاتاً مستقلة بنفسها ، ولا يكون لواحد منها تعلق بالآخر . وكلا منهما ليس إلا في الذات الواحدة. وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الثالث باطل لأنا إذا فرضنا أن ما به المخالفة هو الذات، وما به المشاركة - وهو التحيز - هو الصفة . فنقول : إن الذي به المخالفة إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة ، وإما أن لا يكون فإن كان الأول فهو جسم متحيز فيلزم أن يكون جزء ماهية الجسم جسماً، وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني امتنع حصول المتحيز فيه . لأن ذلك الشيء لا حصول له في شيء من الأحياز، والمتحيز واجب الحصول في الحيز ، وحصول ما يكون واجب الحصول في الحيز ، في شيء ، يكون ممتنع الحصول في الحيز. وذلك من محالات العقول فيثبت بما ذكرنا : فساد الأقسام الثلاثة . فلم يبق إلا الرابع، وهو أن يكون ما به المشاركة وهو المتحيز ذاتا وما به المخالفة صفة . فإذا كان المفهوم من المتحيز مفهوماً واحداً . فحينئذ تكون المتحيزات متماثلة في تمام الماهية والذات. فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان متحيزاً لكان مثلاً لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية والذات . وإنما قلنا : إن ذلك محال . لوجوه : الأول : إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والتوابع . فإما أن تكون جميع الأجسام غنية عن الفاعل ، وإما أن تكون جميعها محتاجة إلى الفاعل [ والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن العالم محدث محتاج إلى الفاعل ] فيتعين الثاني. فيثبت : أن كل متحيز فهو محتاج إلى الفاعل
The second proof: If He were a spatial thing (mutahayyiz), then He would be similar (mithl) to other spatial things in complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya), and this is impossible (muhal), so that [assumption] is impossible. The explanation of the first: If He were spatial, He would be equal to other spatial things in being spatial. Then after this, it is inevitable that either it is said that He differs from other bodies in something of the constituents of His essence, or that He does not do so. The first is false, so the second remains. We say that the first is false because if He is equal to other spatial things in being spatial, and differs from them in something of the constituents of that essence, and that by which participation occurs is other than that by which difference occurs, then the generality of His being spatial is distinct from that particularity by which difference occurs. Having established this, we say: These two matters are either each of them an attribute (sifa) of the other, or neither of them is an attribute of the other, or that by which difference occurs is the described (mawsuf) and the spatial thing by which participation occurs is the attribute, or the opposite — namely, that the spatial thing is the described and that by which difference occurs is the attribute. The first three divisions are false, so the fourth remains. And that yields the statement that bodies are similar in complete essence. We say the first division is false because it requires that each of them be an independent essence (dhat mustaqilla bi-nafsiha) and yet be an attribute dependent on another, and that is false. We say the second division is false because on this supposition each of them is an independent essence, and no one of them has connection with the other, and both of them are only in one essence. We say the third division is false because if we assume that that by which difference occurs is the essence, and that by which participation occurs — i.e., spatiality (tahayyuz) — is the attribute, then we say: That by which difference occurs either is particular to space and direction (jiha), or it is not. If the first, then it is a spatial body, and it would follow that a part of the essence of body is a body, which is impossible. If the second, it is impossible for the spatial thing to subsist in it, because that thing has no subsistence in any space, while the spatial thing necessarily subsists in space, and the subsistence of that which necessarily subsists in space in something that cannot subsist in space is among intellectual impossibilities. Thus by what we have mentioned the falsity of the three divisions is established. Nothing remains except the fourth, which is that that by which participation occurs — i.e., the spatial thing — is the essence, and that by which difference occurs is an attribute. So when the concept of the spatial thing is one concept, then spatial things are similar in complete essence and essence (al-dhat). Thus by what we have mentioned it is established that if He were spatial, He would be similar to other spatial things in complete essence and essence. We say that this is impossible for reasons. The first: Things that are similar in complete essence must be equal in their necessary properties (lawazim) and concomitants. So either all bodies are independent of the agent (al-fa‘il) or all are in need of the agent. The first is false because we have demonstrated that the world is originated (muhdath) and in need of the agent, so the second is determined. Thus it is established that every spatial thing is in need of the agent.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof (the second proof) that the Necessary Being cannot be a body. The author begins by stating that if God were a body, He would be similar to all other bodies in their complete essence, which is absurd. To demonstrate this, he first argues that if God were a spatial thing, He would be equal to all spatial things in the property of being spatial. Then there are two possibilities: either He differs from other bodies in some constitutive element of His essence, or He does not. The first is false because it would imply composition of a common property (spatiality) and a specific difference, so He would be composite, contradicting His being necessary. The second possibility remains: He does not differ in any constitutive element, meaning He shares the entire essence with other bodies. The author then analyzes the relationship between the common element (spatiality) and the specific element (the supposed difference) in terms of attribute and essence. He enumerates four logical possibilities: (1) each is an attribute of the other, (2) neither is an attribute of the other, (3) the specific difference is the essence and spatiality is the attribute, (4) spatiality is the essence and difference is the attribute. The first three are refuted: (1) would require each to be an independent essence yet also an attribute, contradictory; (2) would make them unrelated and thus both would have to be in a single essence, which is absurd; (3) if the specific difference is the essence, it either is itself spatial (making it a body, leading to a vicious regress) or not spatial, in which case a spatial thing cannot subsist in it, which is impossible. Therefore only (4) remains: spatiality is the essence, and the specific difference is an attribute. This means that the concept of a spatial thing is one and the same for all spatial things, so they are all identical in complete essence. The author then argues that this conclusion is impossible because if all bodies are identical in essence, they must share all necessary properties. Either all bodies are independent of a creator or all are dependent. Since we have proven elsewhere that the world (including bodies) is originated and needs a creator, it follows that all bodies need a creator. But this would imply that the Necessary Being, if He were a body, would also need a creator, which is impossible. Hence the assumption that God is a body is false.

Key Terms

تماثل
تمام الماهية
ذات
صفة
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the third possibility (difference as essence, spatiality as attribute) is plausible if the differentiating property is non-spatial.
Correction: The author shows that a non-spatial differentiator cannot support a spatial thing, making the third possibility impossible.

Checkpoint
In the fourfold division, which relationship is established as correct?
A) Spatiality is an attribute and difference is the essence.
B) Both are independent essences.
C) Spatiality is the essence and difference is an attribute.
D) Each is an attribute of the other.

Key Takeaway
If God were a body, He would share the same complete essence as all other bodies, which is the foundation for the next step of the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself picturing Allah in your mind as having a form, even vaguely? What does this proof demand you do with that image?
?
If the Creator cannot be like any created thing, what habits in your worship or daily life reflect that you truly believe this?
?
This proof uses reason to protect Allah's transcendence. Are you willing to submit your imagination to logic when it comes to Allah? What part of your concept of God must you now correct?

Allah's Existence
intermediate

The Impossibility of Essential Similarity
Author deduces that essential similarity would force all bodies to need a creator, proving God cannot be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conclusion that all bodies are identical in essence leads to the impossibility of God being a body, because all bodies would then need a creator.

Core Idea
If all bodies share the same essence, they must all need a creator—impossible for God.

Having established that if God were a body He would be identical in essence to all bodies, the author now argues that this essential similarity leads to an absurd consequence. If all bodies are identical in essence, they must share all necessary properties—including dependence on an agent. Since the world is originated and needs an agent, all bodies would need one, implying God would need a creator, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the proof
Read from 'وإنما قلنا: إن ذلك محال' to the end. Understand the claim: essential similarity implies equal necessary properties.
2
Identify the two options
The author gives two possibilities: either all bodies are independent of an agent or all are dependent. Which one is chosen and why?
3
Understand the deduction
Since we know the world is originated and needs an agent, all bodies must need an agent. This would include God if He were a body, which is impossible.
4
Confirm the overall proof
Reflect on how this completes the second proof: the assumption that God is a body leads to a contradiction.
