The Four Divisions and Proof of Bodily Similarity in Essence
Refutation of three false divisions of body composition and proof of essential similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all bodies must be identical in essence (mahiyya) based on a logical division of the relationship between essence and attribute.

Core Idea
All bodies share the same essence; only their attributes differ.

In this passage, the author presents a rigorous logical division (taqsīm) to determine the correct relationship between a body's essence (dhāt) and its attributes. He considers four possibilities, systematically eliminates three, and establishes the fourth. This argument is foundational for later discussions on the nature of created beings and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the repetitive structure. The author states the conclusion first, then proves it by refuting three alternatives.
The Arabic uses logical terms like 'إنما قلنا' (we say only) to introduce refutations.
2
Identify the four divisions
The passage considers four possible arrangements of essence and attribute. Try to list them before reading the commentary.
The first three are explicitly rejected; only the fourth is accepted.
3
Follow the refutation of each false division
The author gives a reason for each rejection. Understand why each is impossible.
Pay attention to the reasoning for the third division, which is the longest.
4
Understand the conclusion
The fourth division leads to the conclusion that all bodies are identical in their essence (tamām al-māhiyya).
This conclusion is crucial for theology.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why some might think bodies differ in essence. This passage shows that difference is only in attributes, not essence.
This relates to the doctrine of tashbīh (anthropomorphism) or the nature of created beings.

والأقسام الثلاثة الأولى باطلة، فبقي الرابع. وذلك يفيد القول بأن الأجسام متماثلة في تمام الماهية. وإنما قلنا: إن القسم الأول باطل، لأن ذلك يقتضي: أن يكون كل واحد منها ذاتا مستقلة بنفسها، ومع ذلك فيكون صفة مفتقرة إلى غيرها، وذلك باطل. وإنما قلنا: إن القسم الثاني باطل، لأن على هذا التقدير يكون كل واحد منها ذاتاً مستقلة بنفسها، ولا يكون لواحد منها تعلق بالآخر، وكلا منهما ليس إلا في الذات الواحدة. وإنما قلنا: إن القسم الثالث باطل، لأنا إذا فرضنا أن ما به المخالفة هو الذات، وما به المشاركة – وهو التحيز – هو الصفة، فنقول: إن الذي به المخالفة إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة، وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان الأول فهو جسم متحيز فيلزم أن يكون جزء ماهية الجسم جسماً، وهو محال. وإن كان الثاني امتنع حصول المتحيز فيه، لأن ذلك الشيء لا حصول له في شيء من الأحياز، والمتحيز واجب الحصول في الحيز، وحصول ما يكون واجب الحصول في الحيز في شيء يكون ممتنع الحصول في الحيز. وذلك من محالات العقول. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: فساد الأقسام الثلاثة. فلم يبق إلا الرابع، وهو أن يكون ما به المشاركة وهو المتحيز ذاتا وما به المخالفة صفة. فإذا كان المفهوم من المتحيز مفهوماً واحداً، فحينئذ تكون المتحيزات متماثلة في تمام الماهية والذات. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أنه لو كان متحيزاً لكان مثلاً لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية والذات.
The first three divisions are false, so the fourth remains. And that implies the statement that bodies are similar in the entirety of essence. We say that the first division is false because it requires that each one of them be an independent essence by itself, and yet be an attribute dependent upon another – that is false. And we say that the second division is false because according to this estimation, each one of them would be an independent essence by itself, and none of them would have connection with the other, but each of them is only in one essence. And we say that the third division is false because if we suppose that that by which difference occurs is the essence, and that by which sharing occurs – which is spatial occupation – is the attribute, then we say: that by which difference occurs either is specific to location and direction, or it is not. If it is the first, then it is a spatial body, so a part of the quiddity of a body would be a body, which is impossible. If it is the second, then the occurrence of the spatial entity in it is impossible, because that thing has no occurrence in any location, and the spatial entity necessarily occurs in a location, and the occurrence of that which necessarily occurs in a location within something that cannot occur in a location is among the impossibilities of the intellects. So by what we have mentioned, the invalidity of the three divisions is established. So nothing remains except the fourth, which is that that by which sharing occurs – namely the spatial entity – is the essence, and that by which difference occurs is the attribute. So if the concept of the spatial entity is one concept, then the spatial entities are similar in the entirety of essence and self. So by what we have mentioned, it is established that if something were spatial, it would be similar to all other spatial entities in the entirety of essence and self.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a logical division (taqsīm) of the possible relationships between essence (dhāt) and attribute (ṣifa) in bodies that share spatial occupation (taḥayyuz). The author identifies four divisions, rejects the first three, and affirms the fourth, concluding that all bodies are identical in their essence (mahiyya) and differ only in attributes. The first division would make each body an independent essence yet also an attribute dependent on another, which is contradictory. The second division makes each body an independent essence with no connection to others, but then they could not constitute a single composite body. The third division makes the differing factor the essence and the shared factor (spatiality) an attribute; this leads to absurdities: if the differing factor is itself spatial, it becomes a body composing a body; if it is non-spatial, it cannot receive a spatial attribute. Therefore, only the fourth division remains: the shared spatiality is the essence, and the differing qualities are attributes. Hence, all spatial entities (bodies) share the same essence and are identical in their quiddity. The passage ends by stating that if something were spatial (like a body), it would necessarily be similar to all other bodies in essence.

Key Terms

الماهية
المتحيز
الذات
الصفة
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think bodies differ in their essential nature (e.g., fire and water have different essences).
Correction: The argument shows that all spatial entities (bodies) share the same essence (spatial occupation); differences are only in attributes like hot/cold, color, etc.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion the author draws from the four divisions?
A) All bodies differ in essence.
B) All bodies are identical in essence.
C) Some bodies are similar, others differ.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
All bodies are identical in their essence (mahiyya); differences are only in attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as occupying a place—like a throne in the sky—even though you know He is beyond space? How does this proof challenge that subtle anthropomorphism in your heart? What will you do to uproot it?
?
If all bodies are essentially the same and differ only in attributes, then your body, the bodies of the rich, and the bodies of the poor share one essence. Does that thought humble your attachment to your physical appearance or possessions? Why or why not?
?
This proof rests on logical division, not mere tradition. Have you ever relied on blind imitation (taqlīd) for your belief about Allah's nature? Are you willing to examine your beliefs with the same rigor this scholar demands? What is stopping you?

The First Proof: Similarity and Need for a Creator
First proof that God cannot be a body, based on similarity and need for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that God cannot be a body, which argues that all bodies share the same essence and therefore must all be in need of a creator, so the Creator cannot be a body.

Core Idea
If all bodies are identical in essence, they cannot vary in needing a Creator.

This lesson introduces the first of five proofs. The proof begins from the premise that all bodies are identical in their essence, established earlier. From this, it deduces that all bodies must be either all independent of a creator or all in need. Since we know the world is created, all bodies are in need, so God, who is the Creator of all, cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: premise, disjunction, rejection, conclusion.
2
Identify the two options presented
The proof presents two possibilities: all bodies independent of a creator or all dependent. Understand why these are the only options given the premise of identical essence.
3
See why independence is rejected
The first option is false because we have already demonstrated that the world is originated and requires a creator.
This relies on an earlier proof of the world's origination.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, all bodies are in need of a creator. Since God is the creator of all, He cannot be a body (spatial entity).
The conclusion is that the Creator is not spatial (mutahayyiz).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا: إن ذلك محال. لوجوه: الأول: إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والتوابع. فإما أن تكون جميع الأجسام غنية عن الفاعل، وإما أن تكون جميعها محتاجة إلى الفاعل، والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن العالم محدث محتاج إلى الفاعل، فيتعين الثاني. فيثبت: أن كل متحيز فهو محتاج إلى الفاعل، فخالق الكل يمتنع أن يكون متحيزاً. الثاني: إن اختصاص ذلك الجسم بالعلم والقدرة والإلهية إما أن يكون من الواجبات، أو من الجائزات. والأول باطل، وإلا لزم أن تكون كل الأجسام موصوفة بتلك الصفات على سبيل الوجوب، لما أنه ثبت: أن الأفراد الداخلة تحت النوع يجب كونها متساوية في جميع اللوازم، والثاني باطل. وإلا لزم أن لا يحصل في ذلك الجسم المعين: هذه الصفات. إلا بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص. فإن كان ذلك الجاعل جسماً، عاد الكلام فيه. ولزم إما التسلل وإما الدور. وإن لم يكن جسماً فهو المطلوب. والثالث: إن الأجسام لما كانت متماثلة، فلو فرضنا بعضها قديماً، وبعضها محدثاً لزم المحال. ذلك لأن كل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله. فيلزم جواز أن ينقلب القديم محدثاً، وأن ينقلب المحدث قديماً. وذلك محال، معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة. والرابع: إنه كما صح التفرق والتمزق على سائر الأجسام، وجب أن يصحا على ذلك الجسم، وكما صحت الزيادة والنقصان، والعفونة والفساد على سائر الأجسام، وجب أن يصح كل ذلك عليه. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل محال. والخامس: إن الأجزاء المفترضة في ذلك المجموع تكون متساوية في تمام الماهية. ولا شك أن بعض تلك الأجزاء وقع في العمق، وبعضها في السطح، وكل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله، فالذي وقع في العمق يمكن أن يقع في السطح وبالعكس. وإن كان الأمر كذلك، كان وقوع كل جزء على الوجه الذي وقع عليه، لا بد وأن يكون بتخصيص مخصص، ويجعل جاعل. وذلك على إله العالم: محال.
And we say: that is impossible. For proofs: The first: Similar things in the entirety of essence must be equal in necessary concomitants and dependencies. So either all bodies are independent of the agent, or all are in need of the agent. The first is false because we have demonstrated that the world is originated and in need of the agent, so the second is determined. Thus it is established: every spatial entity is in need of the agent, so the Creator of all cannot be spatial. The second: The specification of that body with knowledge, power, and divinity is either among the necessary things or among the possible things. The first is false, otherwise it would necessitate that all bodies be described with those attributes necessarily, since it has been established that individuals falling under a species must be equal in all necessary concomitants. And the second is false, otherwise it would necessitate that these attributes not occur in that specific body except by the making of a maker and the specification of a specifier. If that maker is a body, then the argument recurs in him, and either infinite regress or circularity would follow. And if he is not a body, then that is the desired conclusion. The third: Since bodies are similar, if we suppose some of them eternal and some originated, the impossible would follow. That is because whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its similar. So it would necessitate that the eternal could become originated, and the originated could become eternal. That is impossible, known to be impossible by intuition. The fourth: Just as division and disintegration are valid for other bodies, they must be valid for that body; and just as increase and decrease, decay and corruption are valid for other bodies, all of that must be valid for it. And it is known that that is false and impossible. The fifth: The parts assumed in that aggregate are equal in the entirety of essence. There is no doubt that some of those parts occur in the depth and some on the surface, and whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its similar, so that which occurs in the depth can occur on the surface and vice versa. And if it is so, the occurrence of each part in the manner in which it occurred must be due to the specification of a specifier and the making of a maker. And that, regarding the God of the universe, is impossible.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs (wujūh) that God cannot be a body. The proofs rely on the earlier conclusion that all bodies are identical in essence (tamāthul). The first proof argues that if all bodies are identical in essence, they must share all necessary properties; since we know the world is originated and needs a creator, all bodies must be originated and in need, so God, who is the Creator of all, cannot be a body. The second proof addresses the unique attributes of God (knowledge, power, divinity): if God were a body, those attributes would either be necessary (making all bodies divine) or contingent (requiring a cause); the cause would either be a body (leading to regress) or not a body (proving God is not a body). The third proof argues that if some bodies (like God) were eternal and others originated, then because of similarity, the eternal could become originated and vice versa, which is absurd. The fourth proof notes that bodies undergo division, decay, etc.; if God were a body, He would undergo these, which is impossible for God. The fifth proof considers a hypothetical composite body; its parts are identical in essence, so any arrangement must be due to a specifying maker; applying this to God leads to an impossible regress. Thus, God cannot be a body.

التوابع
الفاعل
المتحيز

Checkpoint
What is the first conclusion drawn from the similarity of bodies?
A) All bodies are independent of a creator.
B) All bodies are in need of a creator.
C) Some bodies are independent, some are dependent.
D) The creator is a body.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that because all bodies are similar, they must all be dependent, so God, who is independent, cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever picture Allah as being confined to the heavens or a specific direction, like a throne? How does this proof challenge that mental image? What will you do this week to replace that image with the understanding that He is not a spatial being?
?
The proof reveals that every body, including you, is completely dependent on Allah. In what areas of your life do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge your need for Him in that area?
?
If Allah were a body, He would be dependent like all bodies. Does your heart truly accept that He is independent and beyond any likeness to creation? What habit can you adopt to remind yourself of His transcendence daily?

The Second Proof: Divine Attributes Cannot Be Contingent
Second proof against God being a body, focusing on the attributes of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God cannot be a body, which argues that if God were a body, His attributes would be either necessary (making all bodies divine) or contingent (requiring a cause beyond another body), leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If God were a body, His unique attributes would force an absurd choice.

This lesson examines the second proof. It considers the hypothetical scenario of God being a body and possessing attributes like knowledge, power, and divinity. The proof shows that these attributes cannot be necessary (otherwise all bodies would share them) nor contingent (as that would require a cause, which cannot be a body without regress). Hence, the only consistent conclusion is that God is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the dilemma presented: necessary vs contingent.
2
Understand the necessary option
If the attributes were necessary for that body, then by similarity all bodies would have them, which is false.
This uses the same principle of similarity.
3
Understand the contingent option
If the attributes were contingent, they would require a cause. That cause cannot be a body (else regress) nor non-body (which proves God is not a body).
4
Recognize the regress/loop
If the cause is a body, the argument would repeat (tasalsul) or become circular (dawr).
5
Conclude
The only consistent option is that the cause is not a body, so God is not a body.

And we say: that is impossible. For proofs: The first: Similar things in the entirety of essence must be equal in necessary concomitants and dependencies. So either all bodies are independent of the agent, or all are in need of the agent. The first is false because we have demonstrated that the world is originated and in need of the agent, so the second is determined. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes cannot be contingent, this means your very qualities of life, knowledge, and power are entirely dependent on Him. Do you feel independent in your daily actions, or do you acknowledge that every breath is a gift? What would change if you saw yourself as completely reliant on Allah at every moment?
?
The proof shows that an infinite chain of causes is impossible. Consider the web of things you trust in—your job, your health, your family. Where have you placed your ultimate trust? How can you transfer that trust directly to the Creator, the only self-sufficient being?
?
You might intellectually reject that God is a body, but do you ever imagine Him in physical terms during prayer or contemplation? What concrete mental discipline can you adopt to shatter those images and affirm His transcendence?

The Third Proof: Eternal and Originated Cannot Coexist
Third proof that God cannot be a body, based on impossibility of mixing eternal and originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that God cannot be a body, which argues that if some bodies were eternal (like God) and others originated, then by similarity the eternal could become originated, which is absurd.

Core Idea
Similar bodies cannot differ in being eternal or originated.

This lesson covers the third proof. It uses the principle that whatever is true of one thing is true of its similar. If some bodies were eternal and others originated, then each could change status, which is impossible. Hence, no body can be eternal, so God cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the use of the principle of similarity.
2
Understand the supposition
Suppose some bodies are eternal (qadīm) and some originated (muḥdath).
3
Apply the similarity principle
Since bodies are similar, what is valid for one is valid for its similar. So the eternal could become originated and vice versa.
4
Recognize the impossibility
This consequence is impossible by intuition (badīha). Therefore, the supposition is false.
5
Conclude
No body can be eternal. God is eternal, so God is not a body.

وإنما قلنا: إن ذلك محال. لوجوه: الأول: إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والتوابع. فإما أن تكون جميع الأجسام غنية عن الفاعل، وإما أن تكون جميعها محتاجة إلى الفاعل، والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن العالم محدث محتاج إلى الفاعل، فيتعين الثاني. فيثبت: أن كل متحيز فهو محتاج إلى الفاعل، فخالق الكل يمتنع أن يكون متحيزاً. الثاني: إن اختصاص ذلك الجسم بالعلم والقدرة والإلهية إما أن يكون من الواجبات، أو من الجائزات. والأول باطل، وإلا لزم أن تكون كل الأجسام موصوفة بتلك الصفات على سبيل الوجوب، لما أنه ثبت: أن الأفراد الداخلة تحت النوع يجب كونها متساوية في جميع اللوازم، والثاني باطل. وإلا لزم أن لا يحصل في ذلك الجسم المعين: هذه الصفات. إلا بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص. فإن كان ذلك الجاعل جسماً، عاد الكلام فيه. ولزم إما التسلل وإما الدور. وإن لم يكن جسماً فهو المطلوب. والثالث: إن الأجسام لما كانت متماثلة، فلو فرضنا بعضها قديماً، وبعضها محدثاً لزم المحال. ذلك لأن كل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله. فيلزم جواز أن ينقلب القديم محدثاً، وأن ينقلب المحدث قديماً. وذلك محال، معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة. والرابع: إنه كما صح التفرق والتمزق على سائر الأجسام، وجب أن يصحا على ذلك الجسم، وكما صحت الزيادة والنقصان، والعفونة والفساد على سائر الأجسام، وجب أن يصح كل ذلك عليه. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل محال. والخامس: إن الأجزاء المفترضة في ذلك المجموع تكون متساوية في تمام الماهية. ولا شك أن بعض تلك الأجزاء وقع في العمق، وبعضها في السطح، وكل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله، فالذي وقع في العمق يمكن أن يقع في السطح وبالعكس. وإن كان الأمر كذلك، كان وقوع كل جزء على الوجه الذي وقع عليه، لا بد وأن يكون بتخصيص مخصص، ويجعل جاعل. وذلك على إله العالم: محال.
And we say: that is impossible. For proofs: The first: Similar things in the entirety of essence must be equal in necessary concomitants and dependencies. So either all bodies are independent of the agent, or all are in need of the agent. The first is false because we have demonstrated that the world is originated and in need of the agent, so the second is determined. Thus it is established: every spatial entity is in need of the agent, so the Creator of all cannot be spatial. The second: The specification of that body with knowledge, power, and divinity is either among the necessary things or among the possible things. The first is false, otherwise it would necessitate that all bodies be described with those attributes necessarily, since it has been established that individuals falling under a species must be equal in all necessary concomitants. And the second is false, otherwise it would necessitate that these attributes not occur in that specific body except by the making of a maker and the specification of a specifier. If that maker is a body, then the argument recurs in him, and either infinite regress or circularity would follow. And if he is not a body, then that is the desired conclusion. The third: Since bodies are similar, if we suppose some of them eternal and some originated, the impossible would follow. That is because whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its similar. So it would necessitate that the eternal could become originated, and the originated could become eternal. That is impossible, known to be impossible by intuition. The fourth: Just as division and disintegration are valid for other bodies, they must be valid for that body; and just as increase and decrease, decay and corruption are valid for other bodies, all of that must be valid for it. And it is known that that is false and impossible. The fifth: The parts assumed in that aggregate are equal in the entirety of essence. There is no doubt that some of those parts occur in the depth and some on the surface, and whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its similar, so that which occurs in the depth can occur on the surface and vice versa. And if it is so, the occurrence of each part in the manner in which it occurred must be due to the specification of a specifier and the making of a maker. And that, regarding the God of the universe, is impossible.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs (wujūh) that God cannot be a body. The proofs rely on the earlier conclusion that all bodies are identical in essence (tamāthul). The first proof argues that if all bodies are identical in essence, they must share all necessary properties; since we know the world is originated and needs a creator, all bodies must be originated and in need, so God, who is the Creator of all, cannot be a body. The second proof addresses the unique attributes of God (knowledge, power, divinity): if God were a body, those attributes would either be necessary (making all bodies divine) or contingent (requiring a cause); the cause would either be a body (leading to regress) or not a body (proving God is not a body). The third proof argues that if some bodies (like God) were eternal and others originated, then because of similarity, the eternal could become originated and vice versa, which is absurd. The fourth proof notes that bodies undergo division, decay, etc.; if God were a body, He would undergo these, which is impossible for God. The fifth proof considers a hypothetical composite body; its parts are identical in essence, so any arrangement must be due to a specifying maker; applying this to God leads to an impossible regress. Thus, God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
القديم
المحدث
البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the third proof say would happen if some bodies were eternal and others originated?
A) The eternal could become originated.
B) The originated could become eternal.
C) Both A and B.
D) Nothing.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that if God were a body, His eternality could become temporality, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not a body, then He is not limited by time, space, or physical need. How does this truth change the way you pray to Him today? Will you approach Him with greater awe and intimacy, knowing He is beyond your physical imagination?
?
The proof says the eternal cannot become originated. Think about an area of your life where you have acted as if God's attributes could change—perhaps doubting His mercy or power in a trial. Will you repent and affirm His unchangeable perfection right now?
?
The argument uses similarity of bodies. Are you guilty of assuming similarity between God and His creation in your thoughts (e.g., imagining God as a powerful man on a throne)? Commit to studying His attributes as revealed in the Quran and Sunnah, and reject any anthropomorphic imagination.

The Fourth Proof: Bodies Are Subject to Change and Decay
Fourth proof that God cannot be a body, based on properties of bodies like division and decay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that God cannot be a body, which argues that all bodies undergo division, increase, decay, etc., so if God were a body, He would undergo these, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Bodies change and decay; God cannot.

This lesson presents the fourth proof. It notes that all bodies are subject to division, increase, decrease, decay, and corruption. Since God is perfect and changeless, He cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the list of properties that apply to all bodies.
2
List the properties of bodies
The proof mentions division (tafarruq), disintegration (tamazzuq), increase (ziyāda), decrease (nuqsān), decay ('ufūna), and corruption (fasād).
3
Apply similarity principle
Since all bodies are similar, these properties must apply to any body, including a hypothetical divine body.
4
Recognize impossibility for God
These properties are impossible for God, who is perfect. Therefore, God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your body every day, but it is decaying. How does the truth that God is not subject to decay change the way you view your own mortality and what you anchor your hope in? What one step can you take today to shift your trust from what decays to the One who never changes?
?
When you feel your faith 'increase' or 'decrease', do you sometimes measure God by those fluctuations? This lesson proves God is beyond increase and decrease. How will you remind yourself this week that your changing emotions do not change God’s perfection?
?
If you treat God as though He must act like a created being (e.g., needing to 'fit' into your expectations), you are committing the same error as the anthropomorphists. Will you repent of reducing God to your standards? What specific expectation will you surrender today?

The Fifth Proof: Parts and Arrangement Require a Specifier
Fifth proof that God cannot be a body, based on parts requiring a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that God cannot be a body, which argues that if God were a composite body, its parts would be identical in essence, so their specific arrangement would require a specifying agent, leading to regress.

Core Idea
A composite body's arrangement needs a maker; God cannot be caused.

This lesson covers the fifth proof. It considers a hypothetical divine body composed of parts. Since the parts are identical, their specific positions (e.g., depth vs. surface) cannot be inherent; they must be determined by a specifier. That specifier cannot be a body without regress, so God is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Understand the concept of parts in an aggregate.
2
Note the identity of parts
All parts are identical in essence. By similarity, any part could be in any position.
3
Recognize the need for a specifier
The actual arrangement (some in depth, some on surface) must be due to a specifying maker (jā'īl).
4
Examine the specifier
If the specifier is a body, the argument repeats (tasalsul) or becomes circular. If not a body, then God is not a body.
5
Conclude
Therefore, God cannot be a composite body.

Source Text

What does the fifth proof conclude about the arrangement of parts in a hypothetical divine body?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not composed of parts and has no need of a specifier, what does that mean for how you view your own self-sufficiency? Are you recognizing that every aspect of your life—your health, talents, and circumstances—is a gift from the One who needs no cause, or do you act as if you are the specifier of your own success? Today, take a moment to list three things you are grateful for, and consciously attribute them to God’s direct specification.
?
The proof shows that identical parts require an external cause to differ. In your daily choices, do you fall into the illusion that your actions are self-determined without recognizing that your very ability to choose is specified by God? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that your decisions are contingent upon His will? Choose one decision today—prayer, work, or speech—and mentally pause before it to acknowledge God as the Specifier of that moment.
?
This passage guards against conceiving God as a body. Yet, how often do you inadvertently imagine God in human-like terms? Are you willing to challenge every mental image you have of God to ensure it aligns with His absolute transcendence? Commit to studying more about God's attributes and correct any anthropomorphic thoughts that arise today.

The Third Proof: Finitude Implies Possibility
Proves that if God were a body, He would be finite and thus possible, contradicting necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God's necessary existence entails His incorporeality, because every finite thing is possible and the Necessary Existence cannot be possible.

Core Idea
God's necessary existence is incompatible with being a finite body.

This lesson presents a classic kalam argument for God's transcendence beyond physicality. The proof uses two simple premises: every body is finite, and every finite thing is contingent. Since God is necessarily existent, He cannot be a body. The argument is concise but powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument is structured as a conditional followed by two premises.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the statement 'every body is finite' and 'everything finite is possible'. Understand why each is claimed to be true.
The proof references earlier arguments for finitude of dimensions and the necessary knowledge of possibility.
3
Trace the conclusion
See how the premises combine: bodies are finite → possible, but Necessary Existence is not possible → no body is Necessary Existence.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important for establishing God's transcendence: it rules out any form of corporeality.
This connects to the broader Islamic theological commitment to tanzih (transcendence).

الحجة الثالثة : لو كان متحيزاً لكان متناهياً ، وكل متناه ممكن . وواجب الوجود ليس بممكن ، فالمتحيز لا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته . أما بيان أن كل متحيز فهو متناه ، فللدلائل الدالة على تناهي الأبعاد ، وأما أن كل متناه ممكن ، فلان كل مقدار فإنه يمكن فرض كونه أزيد منه قدراً ، وأنقص منه قدراً . والعلم بثبوت هذا الإمكان ضروري - فيثبت : أن كل متحيز ممكن ، ويثبت أن واجب الوجود ليس بممكن ، ينتج : فلا شيء من المتحيزات بواجب الوجود ، وينعكس فلا شيء من واجب الوجود بمتحيز
The third proof: If He were a body, He would be finite, and everything finite is possible. And the Necessary Existence is not possible. Therefore a body cannot be Necessary Existence in itself. As for the demonstration that every body is finite, there are proofs demonstrating the finitude of dimensions. And as for that everything finite is possible, it is because every quantity can be conceived as greater or lesser in measure. Knowledge of the existence of this possibility is necessary. So it is established that every body is possible, and it is established that the Necessary Existence is not possible. It follows that none of the bodies is Necessary Existence, and conversely, none of the Necessary Existence is a body.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This is another independent proof (called the third argument, though earlier there was also a 'third'—the numbering is separate). It uses two premises: (1) every body (mutaḥayyiz) is finite (mutanāhī) because dimensions are finite; (2) every finite thing is possible (mumkin) because you can conceive a larger or smaller version of it. The Necessary Existence (wājib al-wujūd) is not possible; it is necessarily existent. Therefore, no body can be the Necessary Existence, and conversely, the Necessary Existence cannot be a body. This is a classic kalam argument for God's incorporeality based on finitude and contingency.

Key Terms
متحيز (body)
متناهي (finite)
ممكن (possible)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existence)
أبعاد (dimensions)
مقدار (quantity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'possible' means 'non‑existent'; here it means 'contingent'—could have been otherwise.
Correction: In this context, 'possible' (mumkin) means contingent: something that could exist or not exist. It does not mean non‑existent.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof consider every finite thing to be possible?
A) Because it is created.
B) Because it can be imagined as larger or smaller.
C) Because it is a body.
D) Because it is necessarily existent.

Key Takeaway
This argument shows that God's necessary existence logically excludes His being a body, because bodies are finite and contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if you imagine God as a body, you are making Him finite and contingent. Have you ever caught yourself picturing God in a way that limits Him? What is the first step to purifying your concept of Allah from such mental images?
?
The argument relies on the fact that every quantity can be conceived as greater or lesser. Is there an area of your life where you treat Allah’s attributes as something you can measure or compare? How can you train yourself to affirm His absolute perfection without comparison?
?
If you truly internalize that Allah is not a body, how should that change the way you approach your salah and du‘a? Do you sometimes feel distant from Allah because you imagine Him as 'somewhere'? What concrete change can you make today to worship Him as the One beyond place and time?

The Fifth Argument: Existence Necessitates Spatial Relation
Presents the fifth argument for divine corporealism based on existence implying adjacency or separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and fallacy of the anthropomorphist's fifth argument that existence itself forces a spatial relation between God and the world.

Core Idea
The anthropomorphist wrongly argues that existence alone necessitates spatial relation.

This passage summarizes a debate between an anthropomorphist and a monotheist. The anthropomorphist claims that any two existing things must be either adjacent or separate, and since God exists, He must relate to the world spatially. But is existence really the cause of spatial relation? Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument. Notice the debate frame: a long debate summarized with additions.
The footnote marker (١) indicates a variant reading for the definition of adjacency.
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify the chain: (1) any two existents are adjacent or separate; (2) why is this true in the witnessed world? Because of substance, accident, or something common? (3) They eliminate all except existence. (4) Therefore, existence is the cause, and since God exists, He must be related spatially.
Pay attention to the terms 'mujanaba' (adjacency) and 'mubayana' (separation).
3
Identify the key terms
Review the key Arabic terms: مجانبة, مباينة, جوهر, عرض, وجود, حدوث, جهة. Understand how each is used in the argument.
4
Reflect on the fallacy
Consider why the argument fails. The source's common misunderstanding says it erroneously universalizes a contingent feature. Think about whether existence itself truly causes spatial relation.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : إني رأيت مناظرة طويلة ، جرت بين مشبه وموحد ، فلخصت تلك المناظرة وضممت إليها زوائد كثيرة. وهي هذه : قالوا : لا شك أن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما مجانبا للآخر أو مباينا عنه . والمراد من المجانبة كون كل واحد منهما بجنب الآخر ، أي أن (١) يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر . وهذا هو المراد من لفظ المجانبة ، فليكن المراد معلوما منه ، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كون كل موجودين في الشاهد كذلك إما أن يكون بخصوص كونه جوهرا ، أو بخصوص كونه عرضا ، أو لأمر مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض ، وذلك المشترك إما الوجود أو الحدوث والكل باطل سوى الوجود ، فوجب أن تكون العلة لذلك الحكم هي الوجود فقط ، والباري تعالى موجود فوجب الجزم بأنه تعالى لابد وأن يكون إما مجانبا للعالم ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة .
The fifth doubt: I saw a long debate between an anthropomorphist and a monotheist. I summarized that debate and added many additions to it. It is this: They said: There is no doubt that for any two existents, one must be adjacent to the other or separate from it. The meaning of adjacency is that each one is alongside the other, i.e., that one is not flowing into the other. This is the intended meaning of the term 'adjacency', so let the meaning be known from it. If this is established, we say: That every two existents in the witnessed world are thus is either due to the specific fact of being a substance, or due to the specific fact of being an accident, or due to something common between substance and accident. That common thing is either existence or temporality. All are false except existence. So the cause for this judgment must be existence alone. And the Creator, exalted, exists. So it is necessary to decisively assert that He, exalted, must either be adjacent to the world or separate from it in direction.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for divine corporealism, framed as a summary of a debate between an anthropomorphist (mushabbih) and a monotheist (muwahhid). The argument claims that any two existing things must relate spatially: either one is adjacent to the other (side by side) or separate (distinct in direction). Adjacency is defined as one not flowing into the other. Then it asks: why is this true for all existents in the witnessed world? Is it because they are substances, or accidents, or due to something common like existence itself or temporality? It eliminates substance, accident, and temporality, leaving existence as the cause. Since God exists, the same spatial relation must apply to Him and the world: He is either adjacent or separate. Thus God must be in a spatial relation.

Key Terms
مجانبة
مباينة
جوهر
عرض
وجود
حدوث
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence itself necessitates spatial relation between any two existents.
Correction: Spatial relation is a contingent property of physical bodies, not a necessary attribute of existence. God's existence is not like that of contingent beings.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about God's relation to the world?
A) God is identical with the world.
B) God must be either adjacent to the world or separate from it in direction.
C) God is neither adjacent nor separate.
D) God is entirely beyond spatial relations.

Key Takeaway
The anthropomorphist's fifth argument fails because spatial relation is a contingent feature of contingent beings, not a necessary attribute of existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your mind, do you unconsciously place Him in a direction or location? What does this passage expose about the subtle anthropomorphism in your heart? Will you actively train your mind to reject spatial categories for Him?

The anthropomorphist in this argument clings to logical reasoning to defend his view. Are you more attached to your own understanding of God than to what revelation and sound reason truly demand? Will you submit to the truth even if it overturns your assumptions?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is not adjacent to the world nor separate from it spatially, how would it transform your sense of His closeness in prayer and your awe of His absolute transcendence? What concrete step will you take today to deepen this conviction?

The Sixth Objection: Could Huduth Be the Cause?
Defends huduth as the cause and rebuts two counter-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against the exclusive dichotomy, including the objector's defense of huduth as a possible cause and the rebuttal to the claim that ignorance of the cause implies ignorance of the effect.

Core Idea
Ignorance of a cause does not require ignorance of its effect.

In this sixth objection, the objector concedes the exclusive dichotomy between huduth (temporality) and wujud (existence) but asks why huduth cannot be the cause of the division into adjacent and separate. He then rebuts two counter-arguments: one about huduth's composition from existence and non-existence, and another about knowledge of cause and effect. The passage provides key insights into causal reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from objection to two rebuttals.
The passage is incomplete; the second rebuttal cuts off.
2
Identify the main objection
The objector concedes the exclusive dichotomy but asks why huduth cannot be the cause. Understand his starting point.
3
Analyse the first counter-argument and rebuttal
The first counter-argument says huduth is composed of non-existence and existence, so attributing one to the other is problematic. The objector rebuts by distinguishing existential versus privative attributes.
4
Analyse the second counter-argument and rebuttal
The second counter-argument claims that ignorance of huduth would lead to ignorance of the judgment. The objector rejects this with examples from health/disease and materialists/world.
This is the core idea: ignorance of cause does not necessitate ignorance of effect.
5
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off during the second rebuttal. Consider what might follow based on the logic so far.
The full argument likely continues on the next page.

السؤال السادس: سلمنا الحصر، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لقبول هذه القسمة هو الحدوث؟ قوله: الحدوث ماهية مركبة من العدم والوجود وقلنا الممكن هو الذي تكون ماهيته قابلة للعدم والوجود، وكون الشيء بحيث ينقسم إلى المجانب والمباين معناه قبول الانقسام إلى هذين القسمين فالقابلية إن كانت صفة وجودية كانت كذلك في الموضعين، وإن كانت صفة عدمية فهي كذلك في الموضعين، فتعليل أحدهما بالآخر يقتضي إما تعليل وجود بوجود، أو تعليل عدم بعدم، ولا امتناع في واحد من هذين الأمرين. أما قوله ثانيا: لو كان المقتضى لهذا الحكم هو الحدوث لكان الجهل بحدوث الشيء يوجب الجهل بهذا الحكم، قلنا: لم قلتم: إن الجهل بالمؤثر يوجب الجهل بالأثر؟ ألا ترى أن جهل الناس بموجبات الصحة والمرض، لا يوجب جهلهم بحصول الصحة والمرض. وجهل الدهريين بكونه تعالى قادرا على الخلق والتكوين، لا يوجب جهلهم بوجود هذا العالم؟ وأيضا: إن كان الجهل بالمؤثر يوجب الجهل بالأثر، لكان العلم بالمؤثر، أولى أن يوجب العلم بالأثر فعلى هذا التقدير لو كان المقتضى للانقسام إلى المجانب وإلى المباين بالجهة
The sixth objection: We concede the exclusive dichotomy, so why may it not be that the requirement for accepting this division is temporality (al-huduth)? [His statement:] 'Temporality is a quiddity composed of non-existence and existence, and we said the possible is that whose quiddity is receptive to non-existence and existence. And a thing's being divisible into adjacent and separate means accepting division into these two categories. So the receptivity, if it is an existential attribute, is similarly in both instances; and if it is a privative attribute, it is similarly in both instances. Therefore, attributing one to the other requires either attributing existence to existence or attributing non-existence to non-existence, and there is no impossibility in either of these two matters. As for his second statement: 'If the requirement for this judgment were temporality, then ignorance of the thing's temporality would necessitate ignorance of this judgment.' We say: Why do you claim that ignorance of the cause necessitates ignorance of the effect? Do you not see that people's ignorance of the causes of health and disease does not necessitate their ignorance of the occurrence of health and disease? And the materialists' ignorance of His, exalted, being powerful over creation and bringing into being does not necessitate their ignorance of the existence of this world? Moreover, if ignorance of the cause necessitated ignorance of the effect, then knowledge of the cause would be more likely to necessitate knowledge of the effect. On this supposition, if the requirement for the division into adjacent and separate in direction were...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection. The objector concedes the exclusive dichotomy (between huduth and wujud) but asks why huduth cannot be the cause. He then responds to a counter-argument that identifies huduth as a quiddity composed of non-existence and existence, arguing that attributing one to the other is not impossible. Finally, he rebuts a second counter-argument: that ignorance of the cause (huduth) would lead to ignorance of the judgment. He gives examples to show that ignorance of the cause does not entail ignorance of the effect, and that if it did, knowledge of the cause would lead to knowledge, which is not always the case. The passage ends abruptly, possibly continuing on the next page.

Correction: The objector shows this is false: people can know effects without knowing their causes, as with health/disease and the existence of the world.

The Seventh Objection: Existence and Its Application to God
Objection to applying existence as determinant to God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection against applying existence as a determinant to God, and the Karramiyya position on God's existence.

Core Idea
The nature of God's existence may differ from contingent existence, affecting theological judgments.

Having conceded that existence (rather than contingency) is the cause, the objector now challenges its universal application. He argues that the judgment only follows if God's existence is comparable to that of contingent beings. This lesson examines this objection and the sectarian nuance introduced by the Karramiyya school.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the objector's challenge.
2
Identify the assumption
Notice that the objection hinges on whether God's existence equals contingent existence.
The Karramiyya view is mentioned.
3
Understand the Karramiyya position
Consider how the Karramiyya deny that God's existence is an attribute subsisting in His essence, thus breaking the analogy.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this objection challenges the application of rational judgments to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السابع : سلمنا أن المؤثر في هذا الحكم [ ليس] هو الحدوث. وأنه هو الوجود . لكن لم قلتم : يلزم حصول هذا الحكم في حق الله تعالى ؟ وبيانه : أن هذا المطلوب إنما يلزم لو كان وجود الله تعالى مساويا لوجود الممكنات . وهذا إنما يصح إن قلنا : إن وجود الله تعالى صفة قائمة بماهيته والكرامية لا يقول أكثرهم بذلك
Seventh question: We concede that the cause of this judgment is not contingency, and that it is existence. But why do you say that this judgment necessarily occurs in the case of God? Its explanation: This required conclusion would only follow if the existence of God were equal to the existence of contingent things. And this is only valid if we say that God's existence is an attribute subsisting in His essence, but most of the Karramiyya do not say that.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This is the seventh objection. Assuming that existence is the cause (not contingency), the objector asks why this would automatically apply to God. He argues that it would apply only if God's existence is similar to that of contingent beings. But the Karramiyya school, in particular, holds that God's existence is not an attribute subsisting in His essence, unlike creatures. Therefore, the judgment may not apply to God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if existence is the cause of a rational judgment, it must apply to God without further consideration.
Correction: The objector points out that the nature of God's existence may be different from that of contingent beings, so the judgment may not follow.

C) Because contingency is not the cause
D) Because the Karramiyya disagree with the conclusion

Key Takeaway
The objector challenges the application of existence as a determinant to God by highlighting the difference between God's existence and contingent existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Decide today to study His attributes as He reveals them, not as you imagine them.
?
The objector concedes that existence is the cause of judgment, yet argues it does not apply to God because His existence is different. Are you quick to apply human standards to God in your life? For example, do you assume He must act justly according to your definition of justice? Ask yourself: have I ever rejected a divine command because it didn't fit my sense of fairness? The false claim that infinite space is God
Author reports and begins refuting the claim that infinite space is God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that some use to identify the infinite spatial expanse with God, and the initial steps of the author's refutation.

Core Idea
Necessity of existence does not prove divinity.

This passage reports a striking claim: the vast, infinite space itself is God. The author presents the reasoning behind this view before beginning to dismantle it. As you read, pay attention to how the proponents use self-evident intuitions about existence, dependency, and directionality. The author will show that even if the expanse is necessary in itself, that does not make it divine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the first line: some people say that the infinite spatial expanse (al-faḍā') is Allah. This is the view the author will refute.
The claim is not the author's own belief.
2
Trace the proponents' reasoning
They argue from primary intuition (badīha): all existents are either the expanse or occur in it (as body or accident). Bodies and accidents depend on essences, which depend on the expanse, but the expanse itself is self-subsistent. Also, the intellect cannot conceive its non-existence because directionality would still exist.
The argument aims to show the expanse is necessary (wājib).
3
See the author's counter
The author begins by noting that if non-existence of the expanse leads to a contradiction, it only proves the expanse is necessary in itself, not that it is God. The refutation continues beyond this fragment.
The key point: necessity does not equal divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن من الناس من قال : إن هذا الفضاء الذي لا نهاية له : هو الله سبحانه . قالوا : والسبب في اعتقاد هذا المذهب : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن الموجود ، إما هذا الفضاء ، وإما شيء يحصل في هذا الفضاء . إما حصولا على سبيل الاستقلال وهو الجسم ، أو على سبيل التبعية وهو العرض . قالوا : والصفات والأعراض محتاجة إلى الذوات التي هي الجواهر والأجسام . وهذه الذوات محتاجة في وجودها إلى هذا القضاء ، وأما هذا الفضاء فإنه غير محتاج إلى شيء آخر ، لأن الفضاء شيء قائم بنفسه ، فلا حاجة به إلى فضاء آخر . وأيضا : العقل يأبى تصور عدم هذا الفضاء ، لأن بتقدير أن يرتفع هذا الفضاء فهل يتميز ههنا الجانب الذي هو قدامي عن الجانب الذي هو خلفي ، أولا يتميز ؟ فإن لم يتميز ، فهذا مدفوع في بديهة العقل ، [ وإن تميز أحد الجانبين عن الآخر ، فذلك الفضاء موجود لأنا لا نعني بهذا الفضاء إلا الأمر الذي يتميز فيه جهة عن جهة ] وجانب عن جانب . وعلى هذا التقدير : الفضاء شيء يلزم من فرض عدمه فرض وجوده ، وهذا محال . والمفضي إلى المحال محال . فيثبت : أن فرض عدم هذا الفضاء ممتنع لذاته [ فيثبت بما ذكر : أن هذا
Know that among people are those who say that this infinite spatial expanse (al-faḍā') is Allah, glorified be He. They said: The reason for holding this doctrine is that the primary intuition (badīha) of the intellect judges that existent things are either this spatial expanse or something that occurs in this spatial expanse, either occurring independently, which is the body (jism), or dependently, which is the accident (ʿaraḍ). They said: Attributes and accidents are in need of essences (dhāt), which are the substances (jawāhir) and bodies. And these essences are in need in their existence of this spatial expanse. But this spatial expanse is not in need of anything else, because the spatial expanse is something subsistent by itself (qā'im bi-nafsih), so it has no need of another expanse. Also: the intellect refuses to conceive the non-existence of this spatial expanse, because if we suppose that this spatial expanse is removed, would there be a distinction here between the front side and the back side, or not? If it is not distinguished, then this is rejected by primary intuition. [And if one side is distinguished from another, then that spatial expanse exists, for we mean by this spatial expanse only that thing in which a direction is distinguished from a direction] and a side from a side. On this supposition: the spatial expanse is something whose non-existence being supposed necessitates its existence, and this is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. So it is established that the supposition of the non-existence of this spatial expanse is impossible in itself. [So it is established by what was mentioned that this]
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the claim that the infinite spatial expanse (al-faḍā') is God. The author first reports the proponents' reasoning: primary intuition (badīha) shows that all existents are either the expanse itself or something occurring in it — either independently (body) or dependently (accident). Accidents and attributes require substances, and substances require the spatial expanse, but the expanse itself is self-subsistent and needs nothing else. Additionally, they argue that the intellect cannot conceive the non-existence of this expanse, because if it were removed, there would be no distinction between directions (e.g., front and back). If no distinction, that contradicts intuition; if distinction remains, then the expanse still exists (since the expanse is defined by distinctions). Therefore, the non-existence of the expanse leads to a contradiction, so it is necessary. The author then refutes by showing that if its non-existence leads to a contradiction, that only proves it is necessary (wājib), but not that it is God. The argument as presented is incomplete (the page cuts off), but the author is establishing that the spatial expanse is a necessary existent (wājib al-wujūd) in their view, then likely will point out that this does not prove divinity.

Key Terms
الفضاء (al-faḍā')
بديهة العقل (badīhat al-ʿaql)
جسم (jism)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
جوهر (jawhar)
قائم بنفسه (qā'im bi-nafsih)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is proving that the infinite spatial expanse is God.
Correction: The author is presenting the proponents' argument in order to refute it. He does not endorse this view.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of those who say the infinite spatial expanse is God?
A) The spatial expanse is an accident that depends on bodies.
B) The spatial expanse is self-subsistent and necessary, and all other existents depend on it.
C) The spatial expanse is created and finite.
D) The spatial expanse is a body that occupies a direction.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the spatial expanse's existence does not prove it is God; the author refutes this identification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proponents based their argument on what seemed self-evident to their intellect. In your own life, what beliefs have you held as 'self-evident' that later turned out to be false? How do you guard against being misled by your own reasoning?
?
The author refutes the idea that necessity equals divinity. What in your life have you treated as 'necessary' or indispensable—perhaps your job, relationships, or health—that you have unknowingly put in a place that only Allah deserves? What would it look like to genuinely uproot that?
?
If the intellect can lead to such a profound error as mistaking space for God, how confident are you in your own rational conclusions about faith? When was the last time you honestly questioned whether your understanding of God is shaped more by cultural assumptions than by revelation?

Second Argument: Impossibility of Conceiving the Non-Existence of the Void
Author gives the second argument based on the impossibility of conceiving the non-existence of the void, leading to its necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the void's non-existence is inconceivable, leading to the conclusion that the void is necessary of existence and therefore God.

Core Idea
The void's non-existence is self-contradictory, proving its necessary existence.

In this passage, the author presents a second, more rigorous argument for the divinity of the void. The argument is a proof by impossibility: trying to conceive the void's non-existence leads to an immediate contradiction. Let's examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: a dilemma with two horns.
The passage contains bracketed parts; consider them part of the argument.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument asks: if we suppose the void removed, do directions become distinguishable or not? Both options lead to a contradiction.
The first horn: if directions are indistinguishable, that contradicts primary intuition. The second horn: if directions are distinguishable, the void still exists.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the supposition of non-existence leads to impossibility, the non-existence itself is impossible. Therefore the void is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself, and this is precisely what is meant by God.
The author equates the void with the necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا : العقل يأبى تصور عدم هذا الفضاء ، لأن بتقدير أن يرتفع هذا الفضاء فهل يتميز ههنا الجانب الذي هو قدامي عن الجانب الذي هو خلفي ، أولا يتميز ؟ فإن لم يتميز ، فهذا مدفوع في بديهة العقل ، [ وإن تميز أحد الجانبين عن الآخر ، فذلك الفضاء موجود لأنا لا نعني بهذا الفضاء إلا الأمر الذي يتميز فيه جهة عن جهة (١)] وجانب عن جانب . وعلى هذا التقدير : الفضاء شيء يلزم من فرض عدمه فرض وجوده ، وهذا محال . والمفضي إلى المحال محال فيثبت : أن فرض عدم هذا الفضاء ممتنع لذاته [ فيثبت بما ذكر : أن هذا الفضاء واجب الوجود لذاته، ويثبت : أن ما سواء من الذوات والصفات مفتقر إليه (١)] ويثبت : أنه غني عن كل ما سواه ولا معنى للإله الواجب الوجود لذاته ، إلا ذلك . فيثبت : أن إله العالم ليس إلا هذا الفضاء الذي لا نهاية له
Also: the intellect refuses to conceive the non-existence of this void, because if this void is supposed removed, then does a forward side become distinguished from a backward side here, or not? If not distinguished, then that is repudiated by primary rational intuition. [If one side is distinguished from another, then that void exists, for we mean by this void nothing but that in which one direction is distinguished from another (1)] and side from side. On this supposition, the void is something whose non-existence necessitates its existence, and this is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. So it is established that the supposition of the non-existence of this void is intrinsically impossible. [Thus it is established by what was mentioned that this void is necessary of existence by itself, and it is established that everything else of essences and attributes is dependent upon it (1)] and it is established that it is independent of everything else, and there is no meaning of 'the god, necessary of existence by itself' except that. So it is established that the god of the world is nothing but this infinite void.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more rigorous argument for the divinity of the void. The argument is a proof by impossibility: if we try to suppose the void non-existent, we immediately face a dilemma. Either after removing the void, different directions (forward/backward) are still distinguishable, which means the void still exists because directionality defines the void; or they are not distinguishable, which contradicts the primary intuition that such a state is impossible. Therefore, the non-existence of the void leads to a contradiction. Since the supposition of non-existence is impossible, the void must be necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself. The passage concludes that this necessary existence is precisely what is meant by God, and thus the void is God.

Key Terms
العقل
بديهة العقل
فرض عدم
ممتنع لذاته
واجب الوجود لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this is a valid proof for the existence of God.
Correction: A Sunni theologian would argue that the void is a mental abstraction, not a real entity; the proof assumes the void as a real existent, which is disputed.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about the non-existence of the void?
A) It is possible but improbable.
B) It is intrinsically impossible.
C) It is impossible only if directions remain.
D) It is possible if the void is not God.

Key Takeaway
The void's non-existence is self-contradictory, so the void must be necessary of existence and is therefore God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading this passage, pause and ask yourself: does your heart recognize that calling the void 'God' is not the same as the God to whom you pray? What exactly is missing?
?
This argument says that the void's non-existence is impossible. But you know that Allah alone is necessary. When you read the words 'there is no meaning of god except that', did you feel a resistance? That resistance is the fitrah. What is your fitrah telling you about the difference between the void and Allah?
?
If you truly believed the void was God, would you still turn to it in worship? Would you ask it for guidance? Why does that feel wrong? Use the lesson to articulate the flaw.

Refutation of the Claim That Space Is the Necessary Existent
A logical refutation showing that space is contingent, not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the claim that space is the necessary existent, through a disjunctive argument and a critique of the opponent's syllogism.

Core Idea
Space cannot be God because it is divisible, composite, and contingent — and no contingent is necessary.

In this passage, the author presents a decisive logical refutation of the idea that space (al-fada') is the necessary existent. He begins by stating that the claim is false, then constructs a disjunctive argument: if space is non-existent, it cannot be divine; if existent, it is subject to measurement and division, which implies composition and contingency. The argument is sharpened with a formal syllogism and concluded with a rebuttal of the opponents' attempt to argue from shared attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Let the author's argument flow without analysis on the first read.
Notice the use of logical terminology.
2
Identify the initial disjunction
Find where the author splits the possibilities for space: either pure nothingness or existent. Understand that this covers all logical possibilities.
The word 'إما' signals the disjunction.
3
Trace the chain from existence to contingency
Follow the reasoning: if existent, space is measurable and divisible; whatever is divisible is composite; every composite is contingent; and nothing contingent is necessary. This leads to the conclusion that space is not necessary.
The chain is a series of conditional statements.
4
Understand the formal syllogism
The author summarizes the argument in a categorical syllogism: 'No space is necessary existent' and its converse 'No necessary existent is space'. Recognize this as a decisive proof (burhan qati').
This is a standard logical form.
5
Analyze the rebuttal of the opponent's argument
The opponent uses shared attributes (independence, nearness, infinity) to claim space is God. The author refutes this by showing their syllogism is invalid (two affirmative premises in the second figure) because different essences can share attributes.
The key is that shared accidents do not prove identity of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا القول عندنا باطل . لأن هذا الفضاء إما أن يكون محضا ، وإما أن يكون موجودا ، فإن كان عدما محضا ، امتنع القول بكونه إلها للعالم ، وإن كان موجودا فلا شك أنه قابل للمساحة والتقدير والتبعيض . فإن الخلاء الذي بين جداري الصفة أصغر من الخلاء الذي بين جداري البلد ، وما كان كذلك كان قابلا للقسمة والتجزئة . وما كان كذلك كان مركبا وكل مركب ممكن ، ولا شيء من الممكن بواجب ، ينتج : فلا شيء من الفضاء بواجب الوجود لذاته. وينعكس فلا شيء من الواجب الوجود لذاته بفضاء . وهذا برهان قاطع في إثبات المطلوب . وأما قولهم : بأن هذا الفضاء غني عن الغير، وقريب ، وغير متناهي ، والإله غني عن الغير، وقريب وغير متناهي. فكل ذلك تمسك بقياس من موجبتين في الشكل الثاني ، وأنه غير منتج ، لما ثبت أنه لا يمتنع في العقل اشتراك الماهيات المختلفة في بعض اللوازم . [ والله أعلم ]
Know that this statement is false according to us. Because this space is either pure nothingness or it is existent. If it is pure nothingness, it is impossible to say that it is the god of the world. And if it is existent, there is no doubt that it is susceptible to measurement and estimation and division. For the void between the two walls of the courtyard is smaller than the void between the two walls of the town. And whatever is such is susceptible to division and partitioning. And whatever is such is composite, and every composite is contingent, and nothing contingent is necessary. The conclusion follows: No space is necessary existent in itself. And conversely, no necessary existent in itself is space. And this is a decisive proof in establishing what is required. As for their statement that this space is independent of others, and near, and infinite, and the God is independent, near, and infinite, all that is clinging to a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure, and it is invalid, because it is established that it is not impossible in the intellect for different essences to share some concomitants. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage contains the refutation of the claim that space is God. The author begins 'Wa'lam anna hadha al-qawl 'indana batil' — know that this statement is false. He then presents a disjunctive argument: space is either non-existent (ma'dam) or existent (mawjud). If non-existent, it cannot be god. If existent, it is subject to measurement (masaha), estimation (taqdir), and division (tab'id). The evidence for this is that the void between two walls can be larger or smaller, showing it is divisible. Anything divisible is composite (murakkab), and every composite is contingent (mumkin). And nothing contingent is necessary. So a syllogism is constructed: No space is necessary existent. The converse is also true: No necessary existent is space. He calls this a decisive proof (burhan qati'). Then he addresses the opponents' argument from shared attributes (independence, nearness, infinity). He criticizes their syllogism as being a qiyas min mawjibatayn fi al-shakl al-thani (a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure), which is invalid (ghayr muntij). The reason is that different essences can share some accidental concomitants (lawazim). So the argument is not logically sound. The passage ends with 'Wa Allah a'lam' (And God knows best).

Key Terms
محض (mahd: pure, absolute nothingness)
موجود (mawjud: existent)
قابل للمساحة (qabil lil-masaha: susceptible to measurement)
مركب (murakkab: composite)
ممكن (mumkin: contingent)
برهان قاطع (burhan qati': decisive proof)
قياس من موجبتين في الشكل الثاني (qiyas min mawjibatayn fi al-shakl al-thani: syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure)
ملزومات (lawazim: concomitants)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of space being necessary is based solely on physical measurements of voids.
Correction: The refutation is a logical argument: if space exists, it must be divisible (since voids can be larger or smaller), which implies composition and hence contingency, which cannot be attributed to the necessary existent.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical form of the refutation against space being necessary?
A) An argument from shared attributes.
B) A syllogism showing that if space exists, it is divisible, hence composite, hence contingent, and thus not necessary.
C) A proof from scripture.
D) An argument that space is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of space being the necessary existent is demonstrated through its divisibility leading to contingency, and the opponent's claim from shared attributes is invalid because different essences can share accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author proves that space cannot be necessary because it is divisible and composite. In your own life, what 'spaces'—concepts, possessions, relationships, or habits—do you unconsciously treat as if they are independent and necessary, forgetting that everything besides Allah is contingent and dependent? Will you identify one such thing today and consciously remind yourself of its contingency in your next prayer?
?
The opponents mistakenly thought that because space shares attributes with God (independence, nearness, infinity), it must be God. Are there any attributes of Allah that you inadvertently project onto created beings or things, perhaps by relying on them in ways that belong only to Him? How will you actively distinguish between the Creator and creation in your heart starting now?
?
The author calls his argument a 'decisive proof.' Yet many people hold false beliefs despite clear reasoning. When you encounter a truth that challenges a comfortable assumption—like the total contingency of everything you rely on—do you submit to the proof, or do you rationalize to protect your convenience? What is one belief about yourself or the world that you know is logically false but still hold onto, and will you uproot it today?

Why Space Cannot Be God
The author refutes the argument that space is divine and gives a decisive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why space cannot be the Necessary Being and how the author uses a rational proof (burhan qati') to refute this claim.

Core Idea
Space is composite and contingent, so it cannot be the Necessary Being.

In this passage, the author addresses a claim that identifies space (al-fada') with God. He presents a decisive proof that dismantles this idea by analyzing the nature of space. You will see how he uses a simple disjunction and logical steps to show why space cannot be the necessary existent, and why the opponents' parallel attributes argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full argument. Notice the author's confident tone when he says 'Know that this statement is false according to us.'
2
Identify the disjunction and proof
Find where the author splits the possibilities (non-existence vs existence). Trace the reasoning: if space exists, it is divisible, thus composite, thus contingent, thus not necessary.
The contrapositive also shows the Necessary Being cannot be space.
3
Understand the logical fallacy
The opponents argue that space shares attributes with God (independence, nearness, infinity). The author dismisses this as an invalid second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises. Different essences can share attributes.
This is a key logical move—attributes alone do not prove identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير يكون غنيا عن المكان والحيز ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن هذا الفضاء ليس حاصلا في فضاء آخر ، بل هو شيء قائم بنفسه ، غني عن كل ما سواه فهذا حكاية شبهات هؤلاء الأقوام . واعلم أن هذا القول عندنا باطل . لأن هذا الفضاء إما أن يكون محضا ، وإما أن يكون موجودا ، فإن كان عدما محضا ، امتنع القول بكونه إلها للعالم ، وإن كان موجودا فلا شك أنه قابل للمساحة والتقدير والتبعيض . فإن الخلاء الذي بين جداري الصفة أصغر من الخلاء الذي بين جداري البلد ، وما كان كذلك كان قابلا للقسمة والتجزئة . وما كان كذلك كان مركبا وكل مركب ممكن ، ولا شيء من الممكن بواجب ، ينتج : فلا شيء من الفضاء بواجب الوجود لذاته. وينعكس فلا شيء من الواجب الوجود لذاته بفضاء . وهذا برهان قاطع في إثبات المطلوب . وأما قولهم : بأن هذا الفضاء غني عن الغير، وقريب ، وغير متناهي ، والإله غني عن الغير، وقريب وغير متناهي. فكل ذلك تمسك بقياس من موجبتين في الشكل الثاني ، وأنه غير منتج ، لما ثبت أنه لا يمتنع في العقل اشتراك الماهيات المختلفة في بعض اللوازم . والله أعلم
The estimation [that space] is independent of place and direction, because it is known necessarily: that this space is not occurring in another space, but it is a thing subsistent by itself, independent of everything else. This is the narration of the doubts of these people. Know that this statement is false according to us. Because this space is either pure non-existence or existent. If it is pure non-existence, it is impossible to say it is a god for the world; and if it is existent, there is no doubt that it is susceptible to area, measurement, and division. For the void between the two walls of the room is smaller than the void between the two walls of the city, and whatever is like that is capable of division and partition. And whatever is like that is composite, and every composite is contingent, and no contingent is necessary, so it follows: no space is necessary of existence by itself. And by contrapositive, no necessary of existence by itself is space. This is a decisive proof in establishing what is sought. As for their statement: that this space is independent of others, and near, and infinite, and the god is independent of others, and near, and infinite – all of that is adherence to a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure, and it is not productive, because it is established that it is not impossible in the intellect for different essences to share some concomitants. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that identifies space (al-fada') with God. The author first narrates the claim: space is independent of place and direction, self-subsistent, and independent of everything else. He then declares this claim false. He presents a disjunction: space is either pure non-existence or existent. If non-existence, it cannot be a god. If existent, it is subject to measurement and division, as the void between two walls is smaller than that between city walls; thus it is composite, and every composite is contingent (mumkin), while the necessary existent (wajib al-wujud) cannot be contingent. Therefore space cannot be the necessary existent. The contrapositive also holds: the necessary existent cannot be space. This is presented as a decisive proof (burhan qati'). Then he addresses the opponents' analogy: they claim space is independent, near, and infinite, and God also has these attributes. The author dismisses this as a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure, which is invalid because different essences can share some attributes without being identical.

واجب الوجود
ممكن
مركب
شكل ثاني
موجبتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sharing attributes like independence and infinity implies identity between space and God.
Correction: The author shows that this reasoning is an invalid syllogism (second figure with two affirmative premises) and that different essences can share attributes without being the same.

Key Takeaway
Space cannot be God because it is composite and contingent, while the Necessary Being must be simple and necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
