?
The author calls the opponents' reasoning invalid. When have you accepted a weak argument for God's existence because it felt comfortable or convenient? Are you willing to reject even your own cherished notions if they do not stand up to decisive proof?
?
You are composite—body and soul, parts and pieces. How does your own contingency shape your daily reliance on the One who is truly Self-Sufficient? Are you living as if you are independent when you are not? What would change if you truly internalized that you are possible, not necessary?

Refuting the Dependence of God's Eternity on Time
Author refutes the claim that God's eternal duration requires a pre-existing time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's eternal duration does not depend on an external temporal framework, and how the author refutes the objection that it does.

Core Idea
God's eternal duration is intrinsic and does not rely on any time external to Him.

In this passage, the author tackles a crucial objection: if God is eternal, doesn't that imply a pre-existing time in which He exists? The objector argues that perpetual duration is inconceivable without a separate, continuous time, which would make God dependent on something else. The author responds by analyzing the nature of time itself, showing that perpetuity can be understood without such a dependence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the privative interpretation
The author first explains the privative sense of God's infinity in perpetuity: we can imagine any number of years and reason judges God existed before that, and this judgment is eternal. This sets up the background for the objection.
Privative here means that the infinity is understood as a negation of a limit, not a positive quality.
2
Grasp the objection
The objector argues that such perpetual duration can only be conceived if we assume a perpetual time or duration. If God is necessarily perpetual, but perpetuity requires time, then God's essence depends on something else, making Him contingent—a contradiction.
The objection aims to show that divine eternity implies a dependency on time.
3
Follow the author's analysis of time
The author refutes by analyzing whether time itself is perpetual or not. If time is perpetual, its perpetuity cannot owe to another time (otherwise infinite regress or simultaneous existence of all times, both impossible). Hence perpetuity is conceivable without another time. If time is not perpetual, then God's perpetuity is not conditioned by it.
The key move: the perpetuity of a thing does not depend on assuming another time.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since in both cases God's perpetuity does not depend on an external time, the objection fails. God's eternity is intrinsic and does not require a separate temporal framework.
The author thus defends the coherence of divine eternity without temporal dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كونه تعالى غير متناهي بحسب الدوام والبقاء ، فإن تفسيره هو الوجه الثاني ، وهو أنا إذا استحضرنا في أوهامنا ، وخيالاتنا أيا من السنين ؟ كم سنينا من الأعداء ؟ فإنا لا ننتهي إلى طرف وحد إلا ويحكم العقل بأنه تعالى كان موجودا قبل ذلك ، وهذا الحكم باقي أبد الآباد ، فإن قالوا : فهذا الدوام لا يمكن تعقله إلا مع فرض الزمان الدائم ، والمدة المستمرة ، وإذا كان الله تعالى واجب الدوام ، وثبت أن حقيقة الدوام لا تعقل إلا مع المدة والزمان ، فحينئذ يلزم افتقار ذات الله تعالى في الدوام والبقاء ، إلى شي غيره ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته ، ممكنا لذاته . هذا خلف . ونقول : دوام الشيء لا يتوقف على اعتبار حال الزمان والمدة ، لأن ذلك الزمان إما أن يكون دائما أو لا يكون ، فإن كان دائما فدوامه ليس لأجل دوام زمان آخر ، والالزم أن يكون كل زمان مظروفا لزمان آخر ، إلى غير النهاية [ أو كلها يكون موجودا دفعة. وذلك محال . وإذا كان دوام الزمان(۱) ] ليس لأجل زمان آخر ، فقد عقلنا معنى الدوام من غير اعتبار زمان آخر ، فليعقل مثله في حق واجب الوجود لذاته ، وأما إن قلنا : إن الزمان غير دائم مع أن واجب الوجود لذاته ، فحينئذ لا يكون دوامه مشروطا بشيء آخر ، وذلك يوجب سقوط دائم السؤال المذكور
As for His being infinite with respect to perpetuity and everlastingness, its interpretation is the second aspect, which is that when we bring to mind in our imaginations and fancies any of the years... how many years from the enemies? We do not reach a limit except that reason judges that He the Exalted existed before that, and this judgment remains forever and ever. If they say: This perpetuity cannot be conceived except with the assumption of perpetual time and continuous duration. And if Allah the Exalted is necessarily perpetual, and it is established that the reality of perpetuity is not conceived except with duration and time, then it would entail that the essence of Allah the Exalted, in perpetuity and everlastingness, is in need of something else, and that which is in need of another is possible by essence, so it would entail that the Necessary by essence is possible by essence. This is a contradiction. And we say: The perpetuity of a thing does not depend on considering the state of time and duration, because that time is either perpetual or not. If it is perpetual, its perpetuity is not due to the perpetuity of another time; then it would be necessary that every time is a container for another time, ad infinitum [or all of them exist simultaneously, and that is impossible. And if the perpetuity of time] is not due to another time, then we have conceived the meaning of perpetuity without considering another time. Let the like be conceived in the case of the Necessary Existence by essence. And if we say that time is not perpetual while the Necessary Existence by essence is, then His perpetuity is not conditioned by anything else, and that causes the fall of the perpetual mentioned question.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning God's infinity in terms of perpetuity (eternal duration). The author explains the privative sense: we can imagine any number of years and never reach a beginning because reason judges God existed before that ­— a judgment that holds forever. The objector argues that such perpetual duration can only be understood if there is a perpetual time or continuous duration, implying God's eternality depends on something else (time), which would make God contingent. The author refutes this by analyzing the nature of time. He argues that the perpetuity of a thing does not depend on assuming time; time itself is either perpetual or not. If time is perpetual, its perpetuity does not come from another time (otherwise infinite regress of containers or simultaneous existence of all times, both impossible). Therefore, perpetuity can be understood without another time. If time is not perpetual while God is, then God's perpetuity is not conditioned by anything. Thus, the objection fails. The author thus defends that God's eternal duration is intrinsic and does not require an external temporal framework.

Key Terms
دوام
بقاء
زمان
أبد
واجب الدوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's eternal duration requires a pre-existing, independent time in which He exists.
Correction: The author shows that perpetuity can be conceived without assuming another time. If time is perpetual, its perpetuity is not due to another time, and if time is not perpetual, God's perpetuity is not conditioned by it. Thus, God's eternity is intrinsic.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection the author addresses regarding God's perpetual existence?
A) That God's essence is limited by space.
B) That eternal duration cannot be conceived without assuming a perpetual time, making God dependent on it.
C) That God's attributes are infinite.
D) That God's power is limited to finite things.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal duration is intrinsic, not dependent on any external time, and the objection that it requires a separate time fails under logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s eternity does not depend on time, what does that mean for your own sense of security in a time-bound world? Does your trust in God truly rest on Him being beyond the changes of time, or do you still subconsciously measure His faithfulness by created timelines?
?
The author argues that perpetuity can be conceived without a container. When you think of God’s existence, do you still imagine Him inside a framework of past and future, or can you truly submit to the fact that He simply is, without any external measure? What would need to change in your daily prayers for this reality to sink in?
?
The objection assumes that needing something else makes a thing contingent. In your life, what aspects of your faith or reliance on God have you unconsciously made contingent on worldly things like time, reputation, or comfort? Will you today identify one such dependency and deliberately place it solely in God’s intrinsic eternity?

Establishing the Necessary Existent from Contingency
Author presents the cosmological argument for the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the contingent world must terminate in a self-necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Contingency proves that a Necessary Being must exist.

Before discussing the levels of knowledge, the author presents the foundational proof that every contingent being requires a cause, and that an infinite regress is impossible. This leads to the affirmation of a Necessary Existent. This passage introduces the first step in the epistemological framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand contingency
Recognize that the world is possible (contingent), meaning it could have been otherwise. This is the premise.
Contingency is established earlier in kalam.
2
Trace the causal chain
Every possible thing needs a cause. If causes went on infinitely, nothing would exist. So there must be a first cause that is necessary in itself.
This is the argument against infinite regress.
3
Identify the conclusion
The author concludes that possibles must end in an existent that is necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا علمنا أن العالم ممكن ، علمنا أن كل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب ، وعلمنا أن الدور والتسلسل باطلان ، فحينئذ نعلم أن هذه الممكنات يجب انتهاؤها إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . فههنا المعلوم : إثبات موجود ، يستند غيره إليه ولا يستند هو إلى غيره ، والمعلوم ههنا أمور ثلاثة : أحدها : الوجود المطلق . والثاني : وجوب استناد غيره إليه ، وهذا نفس الإضافة . والثالث : امتناع استناده إلى غيره وهذا سلب . فيثبت أن المعلوم ههنا وجود مقيد ، يفيد إضافة ويفيد سلبا ، وكل ذلك مغاير للحقيقة المخصوصة التي لواجب الوجود . فيثبت : أن الحاصل عندنا من معرفة الله تعالى ليس إلا المرتبة الأولى وأما المرتبة الثانية فهل هي حاصلة لأحد من الخلق ؟ وبتقدير أن لا تكون حاصلة لأحد ، فهل هي ممكنة الحصول للملائكة فقط ، أو لهم وللأنبياء ، أو ممكنة الحصول للكل؟ وهذه المباحث لا بد فيها من البحث. أما المرتبة الثالثة فهل هي ممكنة الحصول لأحد من الخلق أم لا ؟ هذا هو المراد من قولنا : إن رؤية الله تعالى هل هي ممكنة الحصول أم لا ؟ فهذا تمام البحث عن قولنا : إنه هل تصح رؤية الله تعالى أم لا ؟
If you know this, we say: When we know that the world is possible, we know that every possible must have a cause, and we know that cycle and infinite regress are false, then we know that these possibles must terminate in an existent necessary in itself. So here the known is: establishing an existent, others depend on it and it does not depend on others. The known here are three matters: first: absolute existence. Second: the necessity of others depending on it, and this is the relation itself. Third: the impossibility of its depending on others, and this is a negation. So it is established that the known here is a qualified existence, conveying relation and conveying negation, and all of that is other than the specific reality of the Necessary Existent. So it is established that what we obtain of knowledge of God is only the first level. As for the second level, is it attained by any creature? And if it is not attained by anyone, is it possible of attainment only for angels, or for them and prophets, or possible for all? These investigations must be examined. As for the third level, is it possible of attainment for any creature or not? This is the meaning of our saying: Is vision of God possible or not? So this completes the investigation of our saying: Is it valid to see God or not?
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the three levels of knowledge (al-maratib al-thalath) as applied to knowledge of God. The author first presents the rational proof for the existence of a Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) from the contingency of the world. From this proof, three aspects are derived: absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq), the necessity of dependence of other existents on Him (which is a relation), and the impossibility of His depending on others (which is a negation). These aspects are not the specific essence of God but are relational or negative determinations. The author then states that what we actually possess is only the first level—knowledge that God exists. The second level (a more direct cognitive relation) and the third level (which is vision, ru'ya) are questioned: can they be attained by creatures, and if so, by whom? The discussion of ru'ya is identified as the third level. This passage sets the epistemological framework for the subsequent arguments on the possibility of seeing God.

Key Terms
العالم
ممكن
سبب
دور
تسلسل
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world could be self-sufficient without a Creator.
Correction: The argument shows that contingency necessitates an external cause, and infinite regress is impossible, so a Necessary Existent must exist.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the contingency of the world?
A) That the world is eternal
B) That the world must terminate in a Necessary Existent
C) That the world has no cause
D) That infinite regress is possible

Key Takeaway
Contingency leads inexorably to the affirmation of a self-necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your existence depends on a Necessary Being, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What specific change would you make today to acknowledge your dependence?
?
The argument states that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. In your own life, where do you avoid ultimate accountability by making endless excuses? Will you stop postponing and confront the Necessary End?
?
Since every contingent needs a cause, do you honestly recognize your own contingency? In what specific ways do you deny your need for the Creator, and how would your day look different if you fully embraced that dependence?

Three Aspects of the First Level of Knowledge
Author enumerates three derived aspects and declares they are not the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational aspects derived from the Necessary Existent: absolute existence, relation of dependence, and negation of dependence.

Core Idea
Our knowledge of God is limited to these three relational aspects, not His essence.

After proving the Necessary Existent, the author identifies three 'known things' (ma'lumat) that we derive from the proof: absolute existence, the relation of others depending on Him, and the negation of His depending on others. These constitute the first level of knowledge. This passage explains that these aspects are not the specific reality of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three matters
The author lists three: 1) absolute existence, 2) the necessity of others depending on Him (relation), 3) the impossibility of His depending on others (negation).
These are logical categories.
2
Understand their nature
These are relational or negative determinations, not the specific essence (haqiqa) of God. They are attributes we conceive from the proof.
3
Grasp the limitation
The author states that what we actually possess from knowledge of God is only this first level. We do not know His essence directly.

Source Text

The Second and Third Levels: Vision of God (Ru'ya)
Author poses questions about the second and third levels, equating the third with ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the questions regarding the possibility of attaining the second and third levels of knowledge, especially the vision of God (ru'ya).

Core Idea
The second and third levels of knowledge are not automatically attained; their possibility is debated.

Having established that we only possess the first level, the author poses critical questions about the higher levels. Can any creature attain the second level (a deeper cognitive relation)? If so, is it limited to angels and prophets? And the third level, which is vision (ru'ya), is it possible at all? This passage sets the agenda for the discussion on seeing God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second level question
The author asks whether the second level is attained by any creature, and if not, whether it is possible for angels only or for prophets too.
The second level is not explicitly defined but seems to be a more direct cognitive relation.
2
Understand the third level
The third level is identified with ru'ya (vision of God). The author asks whether it is possible for any creature.
This is the famous debate on seeing God.
3
Recognize the purpose
The whole preceding discussion builds up to this question: 'Is it valid to see God or not?'

Do We Know Allah's Essence? The First Proof
The author enumerates the four categories of knowledge about Allah and argues they do not amount to knowledge of His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to the author, our knowledge of Allah is limited to four categories and does not encompass His essence.

Core Idea
We know Allah's attributes, not His essence.

This lesson introduces a crucial theological question: can we know Allah's very essence in this life? The author begins by denying that we can, and presents his first proof, grounding it in the types of knowledge we can have of Allah. Pay attention to the four categories he lists and why they fall short of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's clear structure: question, answer, proof, premises.
2
Identify the four categories
Look for the four things the author says we can know: existence, modality, relations, and negations. Highlight or note them.
These are technical terms in Islamic theology.
3
Understand why they don't give essence
The author argues that knowledge of these categories is not knowledge of the essence itself, nor does it lead to it. Ask yourself why that is so.
The essence is the specific reality that makes Allah who He is, beyond attributes.
4
Reflect on the premise
The author begins to explain the first premise about why we only know these four things. Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence, indicating a continuation in the next lesson.
This sets up a deeper discussion of each category.

Chapter Eleven: On whether in this life we know the essence of Allah Almighty by reality or by illusion? That is, that specific reality. And assuming that we know it, can that knowledge be attained by any of the creatures, or by all of them, or not? We say: As for the first inquiry – whether in this worldly life we know that specific reality – we say: That knowledge does not occur. This is indicated by several proofs. The first proof: What is known to us of the Truth, Exalted be He, is either existence, or the modality (kayfiyya) of that existence, or relations (idafat), or negations (sulub). Knowledge of these known things is not the same as knowledge of Allah's specific essence, nor do these pieces of knowledge necessitate knowledge of that specific essence. So it must be said: We do not know that specific reality. In establishing this proof we need premises. The first premise: An explanation that we know of Allah only these four things. The first: When we know that the world is originated (muhdath) and every originated has an originator, here we have known the existence of Allah. As for the second – knowledge of the modality of that existence – it is from two aspects: The first: We know that He is necessary of existence by essence (wajib al-wujud li-dhatih), which is an expression of knowing that that existence is necessarily established for Him, for what He is, and that is an expression of knowing a quality (sifa) of that existence. The second: We know that He is eternal (qadim), everlasting (azali), perpetual (baqi), sempiternal (sarmadi), which is an expression of that existence being perpetual, free from non-existence in the past and in the future, and its substance reduces to knowledge of a quality of that existence. Qualities...

Commentary
This passage introduces Chapter 11 and poses the question: Do we know Allah's specific essence (al-dhat al-khassah) in this life, and if so, is that knowledge possible for any or all creatures? The author immediately answers the first part: such knowledge does not occur. He then presents the first proof. The proof states that what we know of Allah is limited to four categories: existence (al-wujud), modality of existence (kayfiyyat al-wujud), relations (al-idafat), and negations (al-sulub). None of these constitute knowledge of the essence itself, nor do they lead to it. Therefore, we do not know His specific reality. To elaborate, the author provides a first premise, explaining that we only know these four things. He details the first two: existence (known through the argument from contingency) and modality of existence (which includes knowing that He is necessary of existence and that He is eternal, everlasting, etc.). The passage ends mid-sentence listing 'qualities' (sifat), suggesting more is to come. The argument is that all we know are attributes, not the essence itself.

Conclusion: Denial of Knowledge of Essence Leads to Contradiction
The author concludes that denying knowledge of God's essence leads to contradiction in all cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying knowledge of God's essence leads to contradiction regardless of how the subject is qualified.

Core Idea
Denying knowledge of the essence leads to contradiction in all possible interpretations.

The author completes the first proof by showing that any attempt to claim that the divine essence is unknown results in logical contradiction. Whether the subject is taken as the essence itself or as qualified by known attributes, the claim either judges the unknown (impossible) or contradicts itself. This passage demonstrates the inevitability of contradiction, thereby establishing that the essence must be known in some way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the logical structure and the two cases discussed.
Pay attention to how the author sets up the two possibilities.
2
Identify the two cases
The author considers two ways the subject of the proposition could be understood: as the essence itself, or as the essence qualified by known attributes or states.
3
Understand the contradiction in each case
In the first case, judging the unknown is impossible. In the second case, judging the known to be unknown is a direct contradiction.
Notice that both cases lead to contradiction.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the claim 'that reality is unknown' necessitates contradiction in all estimations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معلومة، وحينئذ يلزم التناقض. وأما إن قيل: إن موضوع هذه القضية هو تلك الحقيقة بحسب بعض صفاتها المعلومة، أو بحسب بعض أحوالها المعلومة، فحينئذ قد حكم على الأمر المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم بأنه غير معلوم، وذلك أيضا يوجب التناقض. فقد ظهر بما ذكرنا: أن قولنا: إن تلك الحقيقة غير معلومة يوجب التناقض على جميع التقديرات.
Known, and then contradiction follows. And if it is said: The subject of this proposition is that reality according to some of its known attributes, or according to some of its known states, then one has judged the known thing, insofar as it is known, to be unknown, and that also necessitates contradiction. So it has become clear from what we mentioned that our statement 'that reality is unknown' necessitates contradiction in all estimations.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion of a proof (likely the first proof) that the statement 'the essence of God is unknown' leads to contradiction. The author considers two ways the subject of the proposition might be understood: either the essence is taken in itself, or it is taken with respect to some known attributes or states. In both cases, the proposition either judges the unknown (which is impossible) or judges the known to be unknown (a contradiction). Thus, all possible interpretations lead to contradiction, proving that the essence must in some sense be known.

The Second Proof: Knowing the Essence is Necessary
Second proof: every assent requires conceptualization of subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the logical principle that every assent requires prior conceptualization to prove that God's essence must be known.

Core Idea
Without prior knowledge of the subject, no attribute can be affirmed.

This lesson presents the second proof from the author’s argument for the necessity of conceptualizing the divine essence. The author begins with a fundamental logical principle: every assent (tasdiq) is preceded by conceptualization (tasawwur) of the subject and predicate. He then applies this principle to the affirmation of divine attributes, showing that regardless of how we frame the subject, we end up requiring that the essence be known.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical starting point
Read the first sentence: every assent requires prior conceptualization of subject and predicate. This is the key premise. Ask yourself: what does this mean for affirming attributes?
This principle is foundational in Islamic logic and theology.
2
Explore the two possibilities for the subject
The author presents two options: either the subject is the essence itself (from where it is), or it is the essence qualified by other attributes. Trace the reasoning for each branch carefully.
Notice that the first branch leads directly to the conclusion that the essence must be known.
3
See how infinite regress is avoided
For the second branch, grounding attributes in further attributes would lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. The only way to avoid this is to eventually land on an attribute affirmed for the essence itself, again requiring the essence to be known.
The argument elegantly uses the impossibility of infinite regress as a logical tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن كل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور الموضوع والمحمول فإذا أثبتنا الصفات لتلك الذات، فالذي يجعل موضوعا لهذه الصفات، إما أن يكون هو تلك الذات من حيث هي هي، وإما أن يكون هو تلك الذات بحسب صفات أخرى، فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يكون الموضوع لتلك المحمولات، التي هي الصفات، ليس إلا تلك الذات المخصوصة من حيث هي هي أن تكون معلومة، وإن كان الثاني وهو أن موضوع هذه الصفات تلك الذات بحسب صفات أخرى، فحينئذ ينتقل الكلام إلى كيفية استناد تلك الصفات. فإن أسندناها إلى صفات أخرى، لزم التسلسل وهو محال، أو الانتهاء إلى صفة نثبتها لتلك الذات من حيث هي هي، وحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الذات معلومة متصورة من حيث إن موضوع القضية يجب كونه معلوما.
The second proof: Every assent is preceded by conceptualization of subject and predicate. So when we affirm attributes for that essence, that which is made subject for these attributes is either that essence in itself, or that essence according to other attributes. If the first, then the subject for those predicates, which are the attributes, is nothing but that specific essence in itself, and it must be known. If the second, i.e., the subject of these attributes is that essence according to other attributes, then the discussion shifts to how those attributes are grounded. If we ground them in other attributes, an infinite regress follows, which is impossible; or we end at an attribute that we affirm for that essence in itself, and then it follows that that essence is known and conceptualized, because the subject of a proposition must be known.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that God's essence must be knowable. The author begins with a logical principle: every assent (tasdiq) is preceded by a conceptualization (tasawwur) of the subject and predicate. When we affirm attributes for God, the subject of those attributes must be known. There are two possibilities: either the subject is the essence itself, in which case the essence must be known; or the subject is the essence qualified by other attributes. In the second case, we must examine the grounding of those attributes. If we ground them in further attributes, there is an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, we must eventually reach an attribute that is affirmed for the essence in itself, which again requires that the essence be known. Thus, in either case, the essence must be known and conceptualized.

Misconception: One might think that the subject of attributes could be unknown, or that we can affirm attributes without knowing the essence.
Correction: The author shows that logical necessity requires the subject to be known; otherwise, infinite regress or impossibility follows.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that affirming divine attributes logically requires prior knowledge of the essence, on pain of infinite regress.

Third Proof: Knowledge of Attributes Implies Knowledge of Essence
Sanusi enumerates three possible cognitive relations to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that knowing God's attributes as subsisting in His essence necessarily entails knowledge of the essence itself.

Core Idea
Knowing an attribute as an accident of a substance requires knowing the substance.

This lesson presents the third proof from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyah. The author systematically considers three ways we might know something of God: knowing the essence itself, knowing an attribute divorced from the essence, or knowing an attribute as attached to the essence. He argues that only the first and third actually give us knowledge of the essence, while the second is insufficient. The crucial insight is that to know an attribute as belonging to something, you must know both the thing and the attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then read the English translation. Note the three divisions the author mentions.
The proof is structured as a disjunction of three cases.
2
Understand the three cases
Identify each case: 1) knowing the essence itself, 2) knowing the attribute independently, 3) knowing the attribute as subsisting in the essence. Notice which case the author rejects and why.
Case 2 is rejected because knowing 'knowledge in itself' does not give you knowledge of God being knowing.
3
Grasp the key logical premise
The author states that knowledge of the occurrence of something in something else is conditioned by knowledge of each. This is the heart of the proof. Reflect on why this is true. If you know a attribute exists in a subject, you must have some concept of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إنا نعلم من الله تعالى شيئا. وذلك المعلوم إما الذات من حيث هي هي، أو الصفة من حيث هي هي، لا بحسب استنادها إلى تلك الذات، أو بحسب استنادها إلى تلك الصفة فإن كان الأول لزم منه كوننا عالمين بتلك الذات، وإن كان الثاني، وهو أن نعلم تلك الصفات، لا من حيث كونها مستندة إلى تلك الذات، فهذا أيضا باطل. لأنا إذا علمنا العلم من حيث هو هو، فهذا مغاير لما إذا علمنا أن الله تعالى موصوف بالعلم. وأما القسم الثالث وهو أنا نعلم تلك الصفات من حيث إنها قائمة بتلك الذات فهذا العلم بمعرفة تلك الذات، لأن العلم بحصول شيء لشيء آخر، مشروط بعلم كل واحد من الشيئين. وهذا يقتضي كوننا عالمين بتلك الذات المخصوصة.
The third proof: We know something of God the Exalted. That known thing is either the essence in itself, or the attribute in itself, not with respect to its grounding in that essence, or with respect to its grounding in that attribute. If the first, then it follows that we are knowers of that essence. If the second, i.e., we know those attributes, not with respect to their being grounded in that essence, then this is also invalid. For when we know knowledge in itself, this is different from when we know that God the Exalted is described by knowledge. As for the third division, i.e., we know those attributes in terms of their subsistence in that essence, then this knowledge is knowledge of that essence, because knowledge of the occurrence of something in something else is conditioned by knowledge of each of the two things. And this requires that we be knowers of that specific essence.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the knowability of God's essence. The author argues that we know something of God. That known thing can be one of three: either the essence itself, an attribute considered independently of the essence, or an attribute considered as subsisting in the essence. If we know the essence itself, the point is proven. If we know an attribute independently (e.g., knowledge in itself), that does not give us knowledge of God as described by that attribute, so it is insufficient. If we know the attribute as subsisting in the essence, then we must know both the attribute and the essence, because knowledge of an accident in a substance requires knowledge of both. Therefore, in the third case, we necessarily know the essence. Thus, the only viable option is that we know the essence.

صفة
استناد
قيام
علم
مشروط

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing an attribute of God (e.g., His power) is sufficient to know God without needing to know His essence.
Correction: The author argues that to know an attribute as belonging to God, you must already have some knowledge of God's essence, because an accident cannot be known without its substance.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge of the attribute alone.
B) Knowledge of the essence alone.
C) Knowledge of both the attribute and the essence.
D) Knowledge of neither.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a divine attribute as inhering in God necessarily includes knowledge of God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sanusi argues that knowing an attribute as subsisting in God requires knowing the essence. Do you truly know God's attributes (His mercy, justice, knowledge) as realities that belong to Him, or do you merely think of them as abstract concepts? How can you deepen your knowledge of God so that each attribute leads you to a more profound awareness of His very being?
?
If knowledge of God's attributes necessarily includes knowledge of His essence, then why do so many people claim to believe in God's attributes while living as if they do not know Him? What does your own understanding of God's attributes reveal about whether you truly know His essence?
?
The third division requires that you know both the attribute and the subject. When you reflect on an attribute like 'All-Knowing', do you stop at the concept, or do you let it push you to recognize the One who knows you completely? What would change in your daily life if every attribute of God you contemplated brought you face to face with His essence?

The Trilemma of Knowing God's Essence
Sanusi presents a trilemma on knowledge of God's essence, eliminating two options to prove we know the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof from our knowledge of God as a self-subsistent essence, and why alternative interpretations lead to impossible consequences.

Core Idea
Knowing God as an essence means knowing His entire essence, or absurdity follows.

Sanusi presents a powerful trilemma. We know God is a self-subsistent essence. But what exactly do we know? The object of that knowledge must be either the whole essence, a part, or something external. By eliminating the latter two, we prove that we know the entire essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice the three conditional sentences starting with 'In kan'.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage lists three things that the knowledge could be about: the entire essence, a part of its quiddity, or something external to it. Underline each one.
The first is the claim, the second and third are eliminated.
3
Understand why the second and third are impossible
The second option leads to composition, which implies contingency (impossible for God). The third leads to a contradiction where the essence is not itself. Conclude that only the first is coherent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: وهي أنه لا نزاع في أنا نعلم أنه ذات قائمة بالنفس. والمعلوم بهذا العلم إما تمام تلك الذات، وإما جزء من أجزاء ماهية تلك الذات، وإما أمر آخر خارج عن ماهية تلك الذات. فإن كان الأول كان العلم بهذا المعنى علما بتمام الذات، وهو المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني لزم كون تلك الذات مركبة من جزئين أو أكثر، وكل مركب ممكن، فتلك الذات ممكنة. هذا خلف. وإن كان الثالث لزم أن يكون كونه ذاتا، صفة خارجة عن الذات، فيلزم أن يقال: إن الذات ليست عبارة عن الذات، بل عبارة عن الصفة، التي هي أمر مغاير للذات، فيلزم أن تكون الذات ليست ذاتا، بل تكون شيئا غير الذات. وذلك متناقض باطل.
The fourth proof: There is no dispute that we know that He is a self-subsistent essence. And what is known by this knowledge is either the entirety of that essence, or a part of the parts of the quiddity of that essence, or something else external to the quiddity of that essence. If the first, then knowledge in this sense is knowledge of the entirety of the essence, which is what is sought. If the second, then it follows that that essence is composed of two or more parts, and every composite is possible, so that essence is possible. This is a contradiction. If the third, then it follows that His being an essence is an attribute external to the essence, so it must be said that the essence is not identical to the essence, but rather to the attribute, which is something other than the essence, and it follows that the essence is not an essence, but rather something other than the essence. And that is a contradictory absurdity.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the knowability of God's essence. The author starts from the agreed fact that we know God as a self-subsistent essence (dhat qa'im bi al-nafs). The object of this knowledge can be one of three: the entire essence, a part of its quiddity, or something external. If it is the entire essence, then we have knowledge of it—this is what we want to prove. If it is a part, then the essence is composed of parts, making it possible (contingent), which is impossible for God. If it is external, then the attribute 'essence' becomes external to the essence, leading to the absurdity that the essence is not itself. Therefore, the only coherent possibility is that the known object is the entire essence, proving that we know it.

Key Terms
ذات
قائمة بالنفس
ماهية
جزء
مركب
ممكن
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing God as an essence does not entail knowing the full essence; perhaps we only know a part or an attribute.
Correction: Sanusi shows that claiming partial or external knowledge leads to either contingency (impossible for God) or a contradiction where the essence is not itself. Therefore, the only coherent option is that we know the entire essence.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, if the object of our knowledge of God as an essence were a part of the quiddity, what would follow?
A) The essence would be simple and necessary.
B) The essence would be composed of parts and hence possible (contingent).
C) The essence would be identical to that part.
D) The knowledge would be of something external.

Key Takeaway
Our knowledge of God as a self-subsistent essence must be knowledge of the entire essence; otherwise, absurdities follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowing God as a self-subsistent essence means knowing His entire essence, then what excuse do you have for only seeking Him in times of need? Which attribute of His essence will you intentionally meditate on today, and how will you act on that knowledge?
?
The proof shows that any partial knowledge of God leads to absurd conclusions. Are there areas in your life where you are content with a half-hearted knowledge of God—only His mercy, only His power—while ignoring His justice or holiness? What is one step you will take this week to seek a more complete knowledge of Him?
?
If every composite is possible (contingent), then only God is necessary. Does your life reflect that reliance? What created thing—a relationship, a possession, a status—are you treating as necessary alongside God? Name it, and commit to a concrete change in how you depend on it starting today.

Razi's Conclusion: The Limits of Human Knowledge
Razi concludes his discussion on divine knowledge, affirming human limitation and leaving the angelic case open.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's conclusion that direct knowledge of God's essence is unattainable for humans, his uncertainty about angels, and the role of divine illumination.

Core Idea
Human reason stops; only divine effusion brings true knowledge.

This final passage from Razi's chapter on the knowledge of God's essence serves as both a summary and a reflection. After arguing for the impossibility of humans attaining direct knowledge of God's essence, Razi raises a further question about angels. He leaves it open, and then reminds us that when theological investigation reaches its limit, the only recourse is to turn to God for illumination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow from conclusion to question to reflection.
2
Identify Razi's two claims
Razi makes a clear claim about humans (knowledge not attained) and an uncertain claim about angels. Note the phrase 'mutawaqqaf fihi'.
3
Notice the shift to reflection
After the doctrinal statements, Razi shifts to a reflective tone. Observe the imagery of 'dazzled intellects' and 'straits'.
This shows the purpose of the passage: not just to state limits but to direct the reader towards God.
4
Reflect on 'Ifada al-ma'arif'
The term 'effusion of true cognitions' is key. Consider what Razi means by true knowledge and its source.
This concept highlights the need for divine grace in theological understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . ثم نقول : هذا جملة الكلام في أن هذا العلم غير حاصل للبشر ، فأما أنه هل هو حاصل للملائكة أم لا ؟ وبتقدير أن يقال : إنه غير حاصل لهم. فهل يمكن حصول هذه المعرفة للملائكة أو للخلق ؟ فهذا متوقف فيه . واعلم : أن المباحث في الإلهيات إذا انتهت إلى هذه المضائق ، فحينئذ تدهش العقول وتقف الأفكار ، وليس بعد ذلك إلا الالتجاء إلى الله تعالى في إفاضة المعارف الحقيقية . وبالله التوفيق .
This is the complete discussion in this chapter. Then we say: This is the sum of the discussion that this knowledge is not attained by humans. As to whether it is attained by angels or not? And if it is said that it is not attained by them, then is it possible for this knowledge to be attained by angels or creation? This is a matter of uncertainty. And know: that when investigations in theology reach these straits, then intellects are dazzled and thoughts stop, and after that there is nothing but taking refuge in God, may He be exalted, for the effusion of true cognitions. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage serves as the conclusion of the chapter on knowledge of God's essence. Razi summarizes that this knowledge (i.e., direct knowledge of God's essence) is not attainable by humans. He then raises a further question: is it attainable by angels? And if not, is it possible in principle? He leaves this as an open question (mutaqawwaf fihi). Finally, he makes a theological reflection: when investigations in theology (ilahiyyat) reach such difficult straits (madayiq), human intellects become dazzled and thoughts halt. The only recourse is to turn to God Himself, seeking the effusion of true knowledge. He ends with the formula 'And with God is success' (wa bi Allah al-tawfiq).

Key Terms
غير حاصل
الملائكة
متوقف فيه
إفاضة المعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is skeptical about all theological knowledge.
Correction: Razi is not skeptical; he affirms the limits of human reason in this specific area and points to divine illumination as the source of true knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the status of knowledge of God's essence for angels?
A) Definitely attained.
B) Definitely not attained.
C) Uncertain.
D) Possible only for some.

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes that direct knowledge of God's essence is unattainable for humans, uncertain for angels, and that true knowledge comes only through divine effusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your intellect reaches its limit in understanding God—whether in theology or in personal struggle—do you habitually turn to Him in desperate plea for illumination, or do you trust your own reasoning? Recall a specific time you felt confused; what did you actually do? Now: will you make a sincere du'a today asking Allah to effuse true knowledge into your heart?
?
Razi teaches that after 'intellects are dazzled and thoughts stop', there is nothing but taking refuge in Allah. Think of an area of your faith where your understanding feels blocked or unsettled. Are you willing to stop wrestling with your own thoughts and instead surrender to God, asking Him to gift you understanding? Commit to a specific action: write down your confusion and then spend 5 minutes in humble supplication, seeking light.

The Third Proof: Temporal Attributes Imply Dependency
Third proof: temporal attributes lead to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third proof that God's attributes must be eternal like His essence.

Core Idea
A temporal attribute would make God dependent, contradicting His necessary existence.

This lesson presents a sophisticated kalam argument that proceeds by considering three possibilities for a conceivable attribute: either the divine essence is sufficient for its existence, sufficient for its non-existence, or sufficient for neither. Each possibility is examined, leading to the critical conclusion that temporal attributes would imply dependency on an external cause, contradicting God's necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the full argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The proof begins by stating three cases for any attribute indicated by the intellect: (1) essence sufficient for its existence, (2) essence sufficient for its non-existence, (3) essence sufficient for neither. Locate these in the text.
These three cases cover all logical possibilities.
3
Trace the dependency chain
Follow the reasoning for the third case: if the essence is not sufficient for either existence or non-existence, then a separate cause is required. This makes the essence dependent on that cause, which in turn makes the essence contingent.
The proof uses the principle: ‘that which is dependent on that which is dependent on a thing must be dependent on that thing.’
4
Grasp the conclusion
The proof concludes that attributing temporal attributes to God leads to impossibility, so His attributes are eternal like His essence.
This is a foundational proof in Sunni kalam for the eternity of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : كل صفة يشير العقل إليها ، فإما أن تكون ذات الله كافية في استلزام حصولها ، أو كانت كافية في استلزام عدمها ، أولا تكون كافية في واحد منهما ، فإن كان الأول لزم دوام وجود تلك الصفة بسبب دوام تلك الذات ، وإن كان الثاني لزم دوام عدم تلك الصفة بسبب دوام تلك الذات وإن كان الثالث فحينئذ لا تكون تلك الذات كافية لا في وجود تلك الصفة ولا في عدمها . معلوم : أن تلك الذات لا تنفك عن وجود تلك الصفة وعن عدمها . ونقول : الذات موقوفة على حصول أحد هذين القسمين ، إما وجود تلك الصفة وإما عدمها ، لكن وجودها وعدمها لما لم يكف فيه تلك الذات ، فلا بد فيه من سبب منفصل فتكون تلك الذات موقوفة على أحد هذين القسمين ، وكل واحد منهما موقوف على سبب منفصل، والموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء ، يجب كونه موقوفا على الشيء ، فيلزم كون ذات الله تعالى موقوفة على سبب منفصل ، والموقوف على الغير ممكن لذاته ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وذلك محال . فيثبت : أن ذات الله تعالى لا تقبل الصفات الحادثة ، وأنه كما أن ذاته دائم لم يزل ولا يزال ، فكذلك صفاته دائمة لم تزل ولا تزال .
The third proof: Every attribute indicated by the intellect is either such that the essence of God is sufficient for necessitating its existence, or sufficient for necessitating its non-existence, or not sufficient for either of them. If the first, then the lastingness of the existence of that attribute follows due to the lastingness of that essence. If the second, then the lastingness of the non-existence of that attribute follows due to the lastingness of that essence. And if the third, then that essence is not sufficient for either the existence of that attribute nor its non-existence. It is known that that essence is not free from the existence of that attribute nor from its non-existence. We say: The essence is dependent on the occurrence of one of these two divisions, either the existence of that attribute or its non-existence. But since for its existence and non-existence that essence is not sufficient, there must be a separate cause for it. Thus that essence is dependent on one of these two divisions, and each one of them is dependent on a separate cause. And that which is dependent on that which is dependent on a thing must be dependent on that thing. Therefore it follows that the essence of God, exalted, is dependent on a separate cause. And that which is dependent on another is contingent in itself. So the Necessary Existent in itself would be contingent in itself, and that is impossible. So it is established that the essence of God, exalted, does not accept temporal attributes, and that just as His essence is perpetual, it has not ceased and will not cease, so also His attributes are perpetual, they have not ceased and will not cease.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a lengthy logical argument. It begins by stating that for any conceivable attribute, the divine essence is either sufficient for its existence, or sufficient for its non-existence, or sufficient for neither. If sufficient for existence, the attribute always exists; if for non-existence, it never exists. If neither, then the attribute's existence or non-existence depends on an external cause. But the essence cannot be free of either existence or non-existence of the attribute; it must be one or the other. Each alternative (existence or non-existence) depends on a separate cause, so the essence itself becomes dependent on that separate cause. Anything dependent on another is contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi), so the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) would be contingent, which is absurd. Therefore, the conclusion is that God's essence does not accept temporal attributes, and His attributes are eternal like His essence.

Key Terms
ذات
سبب منفصل
موقوف
ممكن لذاته
واجب الوجود لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an attribute is temporal, it does not affect God's essence directly, so it is acceptable.
Correction: The proof shows that a temporal attribute would make the essence itself dependent on an external cause, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would result if God's essence is not sufficient for either the existence or non-existence of a temporal attribute?
A) The attribute would be eternal.
B) The essence would be dependent on a separate cause, implying contingency.
C) The attribute would be impossible.
D) The essence would be simple.

Key Takeaway
God's essence does not accept temporal attributes; His attributes are eternal just as His essence is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes were temporal and could change, how would that affect your confidence in His promises and His consistency? Does this proof give you certainty that His mercy, justice, and power are unchanging? How would that change your daily reliance on Him?
?
This proof demands that you accept a logical conclusion even if it challenges common assumptions about God. Are you willing to submit your intellect to this reasoning when it conflicts with your desires or cultural beliefs? In what area of your life do you still resist the full implications of God's eternal nature?
?
The proof shows that God's essence is not dependent on any cause. Do you live as though you believe that, or do you sometimes act as if God needs your worship or support? What would change if you truly internalized that everything besides Him depends on Him alone?

First Impossibility: Eternal Pleasure Leads to Eternal World
Razi presents the first horn of the dilemma and its impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that if God's pleasure were eternal, the object of pleasure would also be eternal, implying the eternity of the world, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Eternal pleasure requires an eternal object, leading to the eternity of the world.

Razi presents the second proof against sensory pleasure in God. He begins by considering the first horn of his dilemma: what if the object of pleasure exists from eternity? This lesson unpacks that impossibility and its implications for the world's createdness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how Razi states the dilemma and then tackles the first possibility.
2
Understand the conditional relationship
Notice the key premise: pleasure depends on the existence of the object of pleasure. If the pleasure is eternal, the object must be eternal.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Razi references a previously refuted position: the eternity of the world. Understand why this is impossible from the Islamic theological perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على امتناع حصول اللذة في حق الله تعالى : أن نقول : لو التذ الله تعالى بشيء ، لكان الملتذ به إما أن يكون حاصلا في الأزل ، وإما أن لا يكون. والقسمان باطلان فالقول بكونه ملتذاً باطل. بيان أنه يمتنع كونه ملتذا في الأزل : ذلك لأن الالتذاذ بالشيء ، مشروط بحصول الملتذ به ، فلو كانت اللذة أزلية لكان المتلذذ به أزليا فيلزم قدم العالم، وقد أبطلناه. وبيان أنه يمتنع القول بأن كونه ملتذا : صفة حادثة : وذلك لأن حصول الالتذاذ في حق من يصح عليه الالتذاذ أمر مطلوب الحصول . فإذا كان الله تعالى عالما في الأزل بأنه يمكنه تحصيل الالتذاذ ، ولا مانع له من تحصيل ذلك الشيء البتة ، وجب أن يحدث ذلك الملتذ به . وهذا محال ، لأنه لا وقت يفرض أن الله تعالى يحدثه فيه ، إلا والداعي إلى إحداثه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك ، وكانت الموانع المانعة عن إحداثه زائلة ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يحدثه قبل ذلك الوقت ، فلزم أن يقال : إنه لا وقت إلا والله تعالى قد أحدث ذلك الملتذ به قبل ذلك الوقت ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لزم أن يقال : إن الله أحدثه في الأزل ، وحينئذ يرجع الكلام إلى القسم الأول ، وهو أن الملتذ به كان حاصلا في الأزل ، ويلزم منه قدم العالم. وقد أبطلناه ، فثبت أن القول بصحة كونه تعالى ملتذا أمر يفضي إلى أحد هذين القسمين، وثبت كونهما باطلين ، فكان القول بجواز اللذة على الله تعالى محالا . هذا هو الكلام في اللذات الحسية
The second proof against the occurrence of pleasure (al-ladhdha) in respect of God Most High: that we say: If God Most High took pleasure in something, then that by which pleasure is obtained (al-multadhdh bihi) would either be existent in pre-eternity (al-azal) or not. Both divisions are false, so the statement that He is one who takes pleasure is false. The explanation that His being one who takes pleasure in pre-eternity is impossible is: that pleasure-taking with something is conditional upon the existence of that by which pleasure is obtained. Thus if the pleasure were eternal, the object of pleasure would be eternal, and the eternity of the world (qidam al-‘alam) would follow, and we have already refuted it. And the explanation that the statement that His being one who takes pleasure is a temporal attribute (sifa haditha) is impossible is: because the occurrence of pleasure-taking in respect of one for whom pleasure-taking is valid is a thing whose occurrence is sought. For if God Most High knew in pre-eternity that He could obtain pleasure, and there was absolutely no obstacle preventing Him from obtaining that thing, then it would be necessary for Him to bring about that object of pleasure. This is impossible, because there is no time that one supposes God Most High to bring it about in, except that the motive for bringing it about existed prior to that, and the obstacles preventing its occurrence were removed, and when it is so, it would be necessary for Him to bring it about before that time. Thus it would necessarily be said: there is no time except that God Most High had brought about that object of pleasure before that time. And when it is so, it would necessarily be said that God brought it about in pre-eternity, and then the discussion returns to the first division, which is that the object of pleasure existed in pre-eternity, and the eternity of the world would follow. And we have refuted it. So it is established that the statement that it is sound for Him Most High to be one who takes pleasure is a matter that leads to one of these two divisions, and both are established as false. So the statement that pleasure is possible for God Most High is impossible. This is the discussion concerning sensory pleasures (al-ladhdhat al-hissiyya).
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against sensory pleasure (al-ladhdha al-hissiyya) in God. The argument is structured as a dilemma: if God were to take pleasure in something, that thing (the object of pleasure) either exists from eternity (azal) or does not. Both possibilities are impossible, so the claim that God experiences pleasure is false. The first part proves the impossibility in pre-eternity: pleasure requires the object of pleasure to be present, so if the pleasure were eternal, the object would also be eternal, implying the eternity of the world (qidam al-‘alam), which has already been refuted. The second part proves the impossibility as a temporal attribute (sifa haditha): if God knows in pre-eternity that He can obtain pleasure without any obstacle, He would necessarily create the object of pleasure. But this leads to a regress: there is no time when the motivation is stronger, so God would have created it before any given time, implying creation in pre-eternity and thus the eternity of the world—also refuted. Hence, both horns lead to impossibilities, so the attribution of sensory pleasure to God is impossible.

Key Terms
الالتذاذ
الملتذ به
الأزل
قدم العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof is circular because it assumes the world is created.
Correction: Razi has already established the createdness of the world elsewhere; this argument builds on that foundation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, if God's pleasure were eternal, what would necessarily follow?
A) The object of pleasure would be temporal.
B) The world would be eternal.
C) God would be temporal.
D) Nothing.

Key Takeaway
The first horn of Razi's dilemma shows that eternal pleasure in God would entail the eternity of the world, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God being 'pleased' with you, do you unconsciously picture Him with human emotions? How does Razi's argument force you to purify your concept of God from such anthropomorphism? Will you commit to always using the Qur'anic term 'rida' (contentment) instead, understanding it as an eternal attribute not dependent on temporal events?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly positive attribute like pleasure cannot be ascribed to God if it leads to logical absurdity. What does this reveal about your own tendency to attribute human qualities to God out of emotional comfort? Will you allow the rigorous tawheed of the scholars to override your sentimental desires?
?
If God does not experience pleasure, how should that change the way you seek His approval? Are you pursuing acts of worship with the correct understanding that Allah's 'pleasure' is not a passing emotion but an eternal will that does not fluctuate? Examine your niyyah: do you seek Allah's pleasure as taught by the salaf, or are you seeking an emotional response from a deity you imagine?

Second Impossibility: Temporal Pleasure Leads to Regress and Eternal Creation
Razi presents the second horn of the dilemma and its impossibility via regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second impossibility: if God's pleasure is a temporal attribute, it would lead to a regress and force creation in pre-eternity, again implying the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Temporal pleasure in God leads to a logical regress, requiring eternal creation.

Razi now considers the second horn of his dilemma: what if the attribute of pleasure is temporal, not eternal? This lesson unpacks that scenario, showing that it leads to an infinite regress and ultimately to the same impossibility as the first horn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: Razi presents the second impossibility and then shows the regress argument.
2
Understand the regress argument
Razi argues that if God can obtain pleasure at some time, He would have had the ability and motive earlier, so He would have done it earlier, and so on, forcing eternal creation.
3
See the return to the first impossibility
Realize that the regress ends up implying that the object of pleasure exists in pre-eternity, which reverts to the first impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على امتناع حصول اللذة في حق الله تعالى : أن نقول : لو التذ الله تعالى بشيء ، لكان الملتذ به إما أن يكون حاصلا في الأزل ، وإما أن لا يكون. والقسمان باطلان فالقول بكونه ملتذاً باطل. بيان أنه يمتنع كونه ملتذا في الأزل : ذلك لأن الالتذاذ بالشيء ، مشروط بحصول الملتذ به ، فلو كانت اللذة أزلية لكان المتلذذ به أزليا فيلزم قدم العالم، وقد أبطلناه. وبيان أنه يمتنع القول بأن كونه ملتذا : صفة حادثة : وذلك لأن حصول الالتذاذ في حق من يصح عليه الالتذاذ أمر مطلوب الحصول . فإذا كان الله تعالى عالما في الأزل بأنه يمكنه تحصيل الالتذاذ ، ولا مانع له من تحصيل ذلك الشيء البتة ، وجب أن يحدث ذلك الملتذ به . وهذا محال ، لأنه لا وقت يفرض أن الله تعالى يحدثه فيه ، إلا والداعي إلى إحداثه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك ، وكانت الموانع المانعة عن إحداثه زائلة ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يحدثه قبل ذلك الوقت ، فلزم أن يقال : إنه لا وقت إلا والله تعالى قد أحدث ذلك الملتذ به قبل ذلك الوقت ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لزم أن يقال : إن الله أحدثه في الأزل ، وحينئذ يرجع الكلام إلى القسم الأول ، وهو أن الملتذ به كان حاصلا في الأزل ، ويلزم منه قدم العالم. وقد أبطلناه ، فثبت أن القول بصحة كونه تعالى ملتذا أمر يفضي إلى أحد هذين القسمين، وثبت كونهما باطلين ، فكان القول بجواز اللذة على الله تعالى محالا . هذا هو الكلام في اللذات الحسية
The second proof against the occurrence of pleasure (al-ladhdha) in respect of God Most High: that we say: If God Most High took pleasure in something, then that by which pleasure is obtained (al-multadhdh bihi) would either be existent in pre-eternity (al-azal) or not. Both divisions are false, so the statement that He is one who takes pleasure is false. The explanation that His being one who takes pleasure in pre-eternity is impossible is: that pleasure-taking with something is conditional upon the existence of that by which pleasure is obtained. Thus if the pleasure were eternal, the object of pleasure would be eternal, and the eternity of the world (qidam al-‘alam) would follow, and we have already refuted it. And the explanation that the statement that His being one who takes pleasure is a temporal attribute (sifa haditha) is impossible is: because the occurrence of pleasure-taking in respect of one for whom pleasure-taking is valid is a thing whose occurrence is sought. For if God Most High knew in pre-eternity that He could obtain pleasure, and there was absolutely no obstacle preventing Him from obtaining that thing, then it would be necessary for Him to bring about that object of pleasure. This is impossible, because there is no time that one supposes God Most High to bring it about in, except that the motive for bringing it about existed prior to that, and the obstacles preventing its occurrence were removed, and when it is so, it would be necessary for Him to bring it about before that time. Thus it would necessarily be said: there is no time except that God Most High had brought about that object of pleasure before that time. And when it is so, it would necessarily be said that God brought it about in pre-eternity, and then the discussion returns to the first division, which is that the object of pleasure existed in pre-eternity, and the eternity of the world would follow. And we have refuted it. So it is established that the statement that it is sound for Him Most High to be one who takes pleasure is a matter that leads to one of these two divisions, and both are established as false. So the statement that pleasure is possible for God Most High is impossible. This is the discussion concerning sensory pleasures (al-ladhdhat al-hissiyya).
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against sensory pleasure (al-ladhdha al-hissiyya) in God. The argument is structured as a dilemma: if God were to take pleasure in something, that thing (the object of pleasure) either exists from eternity (azal) or does not. Both possibilities are impossible, so the claim that God experiences pleasure is false. The first part proves the impossibility in pre-eternity: pleasure requires the object of pleasure to be present, so if the pleasure were eternal, the object would also be eternal, implying the eternity of the world (qidam al-‘alam), which has already been refuted. The second part proves the impossibility as a temporal attribute (sifa haditha): if God knows in pre-eternity that He can obtain pleasure without any obstacle, He would necessarily create the object of pleasure. But this leads to a regress: there is no time when the motivation is stronger, so God would have created it before any given time, implying creation in pre-eternity and thus the eternity of the world—also refuted. Hence, both horns lead to impossibilities, so the attribution of sensory pleasure to God is impossible.

Key Terms
صفة حادثة
الداعي
الموانع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It might be thought that making pleasure a temporal attribute solves the problem.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that temporal pleasure leads to an infinite regress and ultimately eternal creation, which is also impossible.

Checkpoint

C) God would not be able to create.
D) The world would be created at a specific time.

Key Takeaway
The second horn of Razi's dilemma shows that a temporal attribute of pleasure in God leads to an infinite regress and ultimately to the eternity of the world, which is impossible.

The Philosophers' Proof for God's Oneness
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' proof that two necessary existents would be composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the philosophers' proof for God's oneness, which shows that postulating two necessary existents leads to each being composite, contradicting the definition of necessary existence.

Core Idea
The assumption of two necessary beings forces each to be composed of shared and unique aspects.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the first and most significant proof for God's oneness, drawn from the philosophers. He begins by explaining the logical consequence of supposing more than one necessary being: they must share the attribute of necessity but differ in individuation. This analysis reveals a fundamental problem with multiplicity — but that problem will be explored in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothesis
The proof starts by supposing two existents each necessary by essence. Notice that this is a hypothetical scenario to test whether multiplicity is possible.
2
Find the shared and distinct features
According to Al-Razi, the two would share in the concept of essential necessity but must differ by specification (ta'ayyun) and individuation (tashakhkhus). This is the key step.
If they were identical in every respect, they would be one.
3
Understand the conclusion
From this, each being would be composite: composed of the common necessity and the individual specification. The proof concludes that such composition is problematic, but the reasons are given later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن عشر في أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا الواحد. اعلم أن الناس ذكروا أنواعا من الدلائل على وحدانية الله تعالى، ونحن نذكرها على الاستقصاء: أما الحجة الأولى: التي عليها تعويل الفلاسفة فهي أن قالوا: لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته، لكانا متشاركين في المفهوم من الوجوب الذاتي، ولا بد وأن يختلفا بالتعين والتشخص، إذ لو كان كون هذا حاصلا لذاك، لكان هذا عين ذاك، ولكان ذاك عين هذا. وحينئذ يكون الكل شيئا واحدا، فيثبت أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته، لوجب كونها متشاركين في الوجوب الذاتي، وكون كل واحد منهما للآخر في نفسه وتشخصه، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة فيكون كل واحد منها مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره، ومن التعين الذي به يخالف غيره، وهذا محال لوجوه: الأول: إن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره، فكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى غيره.
The Eighteenth Chapter: That the Necessary Existent by its essence is none other than the One. Know that people have mentioned kinds of proofs for the oneness of God, the Exalted, and we will mention them exhaustively. As for the first proof—upon which the philosophers rely—it is that they said: If we suppose two existents each of which is necessary of existence by its essence, they would share in the concept of essential necessity, and they must necessarily differ by specification and individuation. For if the being of this one were identical to that one, then this would be identical to that, and that would be identical to this. Then the whole would be one thing. Thus it is established that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary of existence by its essence, they must necessarily share in essential necessity, and each of them is other than the other in its self and individuation. And that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So each of them is composed of the necessity by which it shares with the other and of the specification by which it differs from the other. And this is impossible for reasons: The first: Every composite is dependent on each one of its parts, and each one of its parts is other than it. So every composite is dependent on something other than it.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the oneness of God and then gives the first reason why the opposite scenario (two necessary existents) is impossible. The author introduces the chapter as being about the oneness of the Necessary Existent. He begins by stating that there are many proofs for God's oneness and he will mention them comprehensively. The first proof is the one relied upon by the philosophers. The proof proceeds by supposing two existents each being necessary by essence. They would share in the concept of essential necessity but must differ by their individual identity (ta'ayyun and tashakhkhus). If they were identical in every respect, they would be the same, so they are not. Thus each would have a common element (essential necessity) and a distinguishing element (individuation). This composition is impossible for several reasons, and the first reason given here is that every composite depends on its parts, and each part is other than the composite itself, so the composite depends on something other than itself. Dependence on another is incompatible with being necessary of existence by essence, because a necessary existent cannot depend on anything else. This argument establishes that only one Necessary Existent is possible.

الوجوب الذاتي
التعين
التشخص
مركب

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof shows that multiplicity among necessary beings forces each to be composite, which contradicts their simplicity.

The First Refutation: Composition Implies Dependence
Al-Razi gives the first reason why composition is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why composition is impossible for the Necessary Existent: because composition implies dependence on parts, and dependence contradicts necessary existence.

Core Idea
A composite depends on its parts; the Necessary Existent cannot depend on anything.

Having shown that two necessary existents would each be composite, Al-Razi now gives the reasons why composition is impossible. The first reason is both simple and profound: every composite depends on its parts, and dependence on something other than itself is incompatible with being necessary by essence. This single argument rules out the possibility of multiple necessary beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
The passage states that composition is impossible for several reasons, and gives the first: every composite depends on its parts.
2
Analyze the dependency
Since each part is other than the composite, the composite is dependent on something other than itself. This is called iftiqar ila ghayrihi.
Note that 'dependency' here means ontological need.
3
Connect to necessary existence
A necessary being by essence cannot depend on anything. Therefore, composition cannot occur, so the hypothesis of two necessary beings fails.
This argument is a cornerstone of Islamic theology's insistence on God's absolute simplicity.
