Proof that the Necessary Being Cannot Be a Species
Sanusi uses a dilemma to disprove the Necessary Being as a species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being cannot be a species with multiple individuals, based on the logical dilemma presented in the text.

Core Idea
A species with multiple individuals would make God either identical to one individual or dependent on another.

Sanusi now tackles the second possibility: that the Necessary Being is a species containing multiple individuals. This might seem plausible for a transcendent being, but the author shows it leads to a contradiction. Using a simple dilemma, he demonstrates that the Necessary Being's uniqueness precludes any kind of multiplicity, even within a species. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
The passage addresses the idea that the Necessary Being is a species (naw') with individuals (ashkhas) under it. This would imply multiple instances of the divine essence.
Remember that a species is a universal that can have many particular instances.
2
Identify the dilemma's first horn
If the determinant of a particular individual is the quiddity itself, then wherever that quiddity is found, the same individual must appear. That would make the Necessary Being only that one individual, not multiple.
This contradicts the assumption of having multiple individuals.
3
Identify the dilemma's second horn
If the determinant is a separate cause, then the individual depends on that cause, making the Necessary Being necessary by another, which is impossible.
The Necessary Being must be independent.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both horns lead to impossibility, so the original claim is false. The Necessary Being cannot be a species with individuals.
This reinforces the uniqueness and simplicity of the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : إن واجب الوجود لذاته نوع تحته أشخاص ، فنقول : المقتضى لذلك الشخص المعين ، إن كان هو تلك الماهية أو شيء من لوازمها وجب أن يقال : إنه أينما حصلت تلك الماهية النوعية ، أن يحصل ذلك الشخص بعينه فكان واجب الوجود هو ذلك الشخص فقط ، وإن كان المقتضى لذلك الشخص سببا منفصلا ، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يوجد ذلك المعين ، إلا بسبب منفصل فيكون الواجب لذاته ، واجبا بغيره . وهو محال . فهذا هو الكلام المستقصى في تقرير هذه الوجوه
As for the second division, which is to say that the Necessary Being by essence is a species under which are individuals, we say: the determinant for that particular individual—if it is that quiddity or something from its concomitants—then it must be said that wherever that specific quiddity obtains, that particular individual would obtain, so the Necessary Being would be only that individual. And if the determinant for that individual is a separate cause, then it would follow that that particular does not exist except by a separate cause, so the necessary by essence would be necessary by another. And that is impossible. So this is the comprehensive discussion in establishing these proofs.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage addresses the second possibility: that the Necessary Being is a species (naw') under which are individuals (ashkhas). The author argues that this is also impossible. He presents a dilemma: the determinant (muqtada) for the particular individual is either the quiddity itself (i.e., the species essence) or something extrinsic. If it is the quiddity itself, then wherever that quiddity is found, the same individual must be found, meaning the Necessary Being is only one individual—this contradicts the assumption of multiple individuals. If the determinant is a separate cause, then the individual would depend on that cause for its existence, making the Necessary Being necessary through another, which is impossible. Thus the Necessary Being cannot be a species with multiple individuals.

Misconception: One might think that God could be a species with multiple individuals, like a universal that has many particular instances.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to an impossibility: either all individuals would be identical, or God would depend on an external cause.

B) The genus or the differentia.
C) The essence or the attribute.
D) The necessary or the possible.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be a species with multiple individuals, because any such scenario leads to a logical contradiction.

The Third Proof: Wujub Cannot Be Positive
Sanusi presents the third argument against wujub being positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why wujub (necessity) cannot be a positive attribute, as demonstrated by Sanusi's third proof.

Core Idea
If wujub were positive, it would require another wujub, leading to infinite regress.

Sanusi now presents the third and most subtle argument against wujub being a positive attribute. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming wujub is positive leads to an impossible choice between essential possibility and infinite regress. This passage shows the logical rigor of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical structure: a conditional followed by two options.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument considers two ways wujub could be related to existence: by essential possibility (imkan bi-dhat) or by necessity (wujub). Find these in the text.
3
Analyze the first possibility
If the attribution is by possibility, then the quiddity of the Necessary Existent would be essentially possible, which contradicts its necessary nature. This branch is impossible.
This relies on the fact that the Necessary Existent's quiddity cannot be possible.
4
Analyze the second possibility
If the attribution is by necessity, then wujub would be an additional attribute over existence, leading to an infinite regress of wujub attributes. This is also impossible.
This shows that any positive attribute added to existence would create a chain.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since both branches are impossible, the original assumption that wujub is positive must be false. Therefore wujub is not a positive attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثابتا لكان مساويا لسائر الموجودات في كونه موجودا ، ومخالفا لها في ماهيته المعينة ، فيكون وجوده غير ماهيته فاتصاف ماهیته بوجوده إن كان على سبيل الإمكان بالذات، وهو محال . وإن كان على سبيل الوجوب ، لزم أن يكون وجوب لوجود زائداً عليه . ولزم التسلسل . وهو محال
The third: If wujub (necessity) were a positive thing, it would be equal to all other existents in being existent, and different from them in its specific quiddity. Thus its existence would be other than its quiddity. So the attribution of its quiddity by its existence, if it is by way of essential possibility (imkan bi-dhat), that is impossible. And if it is by way of necessity (wujub), then it would necessitate that wujub be additional to existence, and regress (tasalsul) would follow, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument refuting the idea that wujub (necessity) is a positive attribute. The author argues by reductio: if wujub were a positive thing (amr thubuti), it would be an accident inhering in the essence of the existent. Since existence is other than quiddity, the attribution of the quiddity by its existence would be either by way of possibility (imkan) or necessity (wujub). If by possibility, the quiddity would be essentially possible, but the quiddity of the Necessary Existent is impossible to be possible. If by necessity, then wujub would be an additional attribute added to existence, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of added attributes. Both options are impossible, so wujub cannot be positive.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الماهية
الإمكان
التسلسل
مفهوم ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wujub (necessity) is a positive attribute that exists in the essence independently.
Correction: This argument proves that if wujub were positive, it would create an infinite regress of added attributes, so it cannot be positive.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about wujub?
A) It is a positive attribute added to existence.
B) It cannot be a positive attribute because it leads to regress.
C) It is identical to existence.
D) It is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
Wujub cannot be a positive attribute because assuming it is leads to either essential possibility (impossible for the Necessary) or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that attributing any additional 'necessity' to Allah beyond His existence leads to absurdity. Examine your own heart: do you sometimes add mental attributes to Allah as if He is composed of parts? Will you repent from such conceptual shirk and affirm His oneness without qualification?
?
You have learned that for the Necessary Existent, existence is not other than quiddity. How does this affect your daily awareness of Allah? When you say 'Allah exists,' do you feel the reality that His existence is His very essence, or do you still think of existence as an added quality? Pray for a heart that truly understands.
?
The argument eliminates infinite regress by showing the necessity of stopping at a simple Necessary Existent. Apply this to your own life: are you chasing infinite regresses in your pursuit of meaning, or have you anchored your life in the Self-Sufficient One? What would it take for you to stop the regress and stand still before Allah?

The Fourth Proof: Wujub bi-dhat Would Be Compositely Possible
Sanusi argues that if wujub bi-dhat were positive, it would be composite and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth argument demonstrates that if wujub bi-dhat were a positive attribute, it would entail composition and possibility, contradicting its necessary nature.

Core Idea
If wujub bi-dhat were positive, it would be composite and thus possible — impossible for the Necessary.

Sanusi presents a sharp logical argument: if the attribute of necessary-by-essence (wujub bi-dhat) were a real positive thing, it would be made of two parts — the common concept of necessity and the specific aspect 'by essence'. Anything composite is dependent on its parts and therefore possible, not necessary. This contradiction shows that wujub bi-dhat must be a negative (i.e., not a positive attribute).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The argument is tightly packed; take your time.
2
Identify the two components
See how wujub is divided into two types (by essence and by another). Recognize that the concept of wujub is common, while being 'by essence' is the distinguishing feature.
This division is key to the composition argument.
3
Understand the composition argument
Realize that if common and distinctive are different, then wujub bi-dhat would be a composite quiddity. Any composite is possible (mumkin) because it depends on its parts.
Composition implies dependence, which implies possibility.
4
Grasp the contradiction
Understand that wujub bi-dhat is inherently opposed to possibility. The assumption that it is positive leads to a contradiction, so the assumption must be false.
This is an indirect proof (burhan al-tamanni).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الوجوب بالذات أمراً ثبوتياً لكان ممكناً لذاته ، وهذا محال ، فذاك محال ، بيان الشرطية : أن الوجوب ينقسم إلى ما يكون وجوبا بالذات، وإلى ما يكون وجوبا بالغير، والمفهوم من الوجوب هو الذي به يشترك هذان القمان ، وكونه بالذات هو الذي به يتميز أحد هذين القسمين عن الثاني ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة ، فكونه وجوبا ، مغاير لكونه بالذات وإذا كان كذلك كان الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة وكل ماهية مركبة فهي ينتج أنه لو كان الوجوب بالذات موجوداً ، لكان ممكنا . وإنما قلنا : إن ذلك محال لأن كونه وجوبا بالذات، ماهية مناقضة للإمكان . فالقول يكون إحدى الماهيتين نفس الثاني ، جمع بين النقيضين وهو محال
The fourth: If necessary-by-essence (wajib bi-dhat) were a positive thing, it would be possible by essence (mumkin bi-dhat), and that is impossible, so that is impossible. Explanation of the conditional: That wujub is divided into that which is wujub by essence and that which is wujub by another. The concept of wujub is that in which these two types share, and being by essence is that by which one of these two divisions is distinguished from the second. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So being wujub is different from being by essence. If so, then necessary-by-essence would be a composite quiddity, and every composite quiddity is [possible]. So it follows that if necessary-by-essence existed, it would be possible. And we say: That is impossible because being necessary-by-essence is a quiddity contradictory to possibility (imkan). To say that one of the two quiddities is the other itself would combine the two contradictories, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against wujub being positive. The author explains the conditional: if wujub bi-dhat were a positive thing, it would be possible by essence. The reasoning is that the concept of 'wujub' is shared between wujub bi-dhat and wujub bi-ghayr (necessity by another), while 'bi-dhat' is the distinguishing factor. Since the common factor (wujub) and the distinguishing factor (bi-dhat) are different, wujub bi-dhat would be a composite quiddity consisting of these two aspects. Any composite quiddity is possible (mumkin) because it depends on its parts. Therefore wujub bi-dhat would be possible. But being necessary-by-essence is inherently opposed to possibility. Thus the assumption that it is positive leads to a contradiction (combining the two quiddities), so it must be negative.

Key Terms
الوجوب بالذات
الماهية المركبة
الإمكان
المشاركة
المباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if wujub bi-dhat is a positive attribute, it would be a simple concept without composition.
Correction: The argument shows that even as a positive attribute, it would be composed of the shared concept of wujub and the specific condition bi-dhat, making it composite and dependent.

Checkpoint
Why does the fourth argument conclude that wujub bi-dhat would be possible?
A) Because it is a simple concept.
B) Because it is composed of the common and distinguishing aspects, making it dependent.
C) Because it has no cause.
D) Because it is identical to existence.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that wujub bi-dhat cannot be a positive attribute because that would entail composition and possibility, contradicting essential necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever tried to 'picture' or 'conceive' Allah's necessity as a positive quality, forgetting that any such conception introduces composition? How would your worship change if you truly accepted that His essence is beyond all conceptual composition?
?
This proof shows that even the most basic theological term (wajib) becomes self-contradictory if treated as a positive attribute. Are you willing to let go of your mental definitions of Allah and instead approach Him with awe and silence? What step can you take today to purify your understanding of tawhid?
?
The argument reveals that any created thing's existence is composite and dependent. If you sincerely grasped that only Allah is absolutely simple and independent, would you still place the same level of trust in people and circumstances? What would it mean for you to rely solely on Him?

The Fifth Proof: Wujub is Negative
Sanusi analyzes the two possible meanings of wujub bi-dhat and concludes it is negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's fifth proof that wujub bi-dhat is a negative concept, not a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Wujub bi-dhat cannot be positive; it is a negative concept of self-sufficiency.

Sanusi concludes his five proofs by examining the two possible meanings of wujub bi-dhat. He shows that the first meaning is clearly negative, and the second, if taken as positive, leads to an infinite regress. This establishes that our concept of necessary existence is negative, not positive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the overall argument.
Pay attention to the two meanings Sanusi lists.
2
Identify the two possible meanings
The author states that wujub bi-dhat can mean either non-need for a cause or self-necessitation. Find these in the text.
3
Understand why the first meaning is negative
The first meaning (non-need) is explicitly called a negative concept. Recognize that absence of need is a negation.
Sanusi says this is known by necessary knowledge.
4
Analyze the regress argument for the second meaning
If self-necessitation is a positive attribute distinct from the essence, it becomes an accident requiring another necessitation, leading to infinite regress. Work through this reasoning.
This refutes the positive interpretation.
5
Conclude that wujub is negative
Since both possibilities lead to a negative concept (first directly, second via regress), the overall conclusion is that wujub bi-dhat is negative.
This is the result of all five proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : وهو أن المعقول من الوجوب بالذات، إما عدم افتقاره إلى سبب منفصل ، وإما كونه مقتضيا لوجود نفسه . ولا يعقل من الوجوب بالذات أمر آخر سوى هذين المفهومين . أما الأول : وهو عدم افتقاره إلى سبب منفصل ، فلا شك أن هذا المفهوم مفهوم سلبي لا ثبوتي. والعلم بأن الأمر كذلك علم ضروري . وأما الثاني : وهو أن كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لوجود نفسها ، فنقول : ذلك الافتقار إن كان أمرا ثبوتيا ، مغايرا لتلك الماهية ، ولذلك الوجوب ، كان ذلك الاقتضاء لاحقا من لواحق تلك الماهية ، وصفة من صفاتها ، فلا تكون قائمة بنفسها ، مستقلة بذاتها، فلا بد لها من اقتضاء آخر ، وذلك يوجب التسلسل . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الخمسة : أن المفهوم من كون الشيء واجبا لذاته، ليس مفهوما ثبوتيا ، بل هو مفهوم سلبي
The fifth: That the intelligible from necessary-by-essence (wajib bi-dhat) is either non-need for a separate cause, or it being self-necessitating. No other meaning is intelligible from necessary-by-essence besides these two concepts. As for the first, which is non-need for a separate cause, there is no doubt that this concept is a negative concept (mafhum salbi), not positive. And knowing this is necessary knowledge (daruri). As for the second, that the quiddity is self-necessitating, we say: That necessity [to exist], if it is a positive thing, other than that quiddity and that wujub, then that necessitation would be an accident (lahiq) of that quiddity, and a description among its descriptions, so it would not be self-subsisting, independent in itself. Then it would need another necessitation, and that leads to regress (tasalsul). So it is established by these five proofs that the concept of a thing being necessary by essence is not a positive concept, but rather a negative concept.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage contains the fifth argument and the conclusion of all five proofs. The author begins by stating that the intelligible meaning of wajib bi-dhat reduces to two possibilities: either the absence of need for an external cause (a negative concept) or the quiddity's self-necessitation of its existence (a positive concept). He immediately notes that the first is clearly negative and known necessarily. For the second, if it is a positive attribute distinct from the quiddity, it would be an accident (lahiq) and not self-subsistent. This accident would itself need another necessitation to exist, leading to an infinite regress. Since both possibilities show that wujub cannot be positive—either it is negative or it leads to regress—the author concludes from all five arguments that the concept of wujub bi-dhat is negative (salbi).

Key Terms
المعقول (intelligible)
الافتقار (need)
الاقتضاء (necessitation)
مفهوم سلبي (negative concept)
مفهوم ثبوتي (positive concept)
التسلسل (regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-necessitation is a positive attribute of God.
Correction: If it were a positive attribute, it would be an accident requiring another necessitation, leading to regress.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what is the first possible meaning of wujub bi-dhat?
A) Self-necessitation
B) Non-need for an external cause
C) A positive attribute
D) An accident of the quiddity

Key Takeaway
Wujub bi-dhat is not a positive attribute but a negative concept denoting independence from a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's necessity is purely negative (non-need), what does that mean for how you view your own need of Him? Are there moments in your life where you act as if He needs your worship or your obedience? How can you genuinely align your heart with the fact that He is completely independent of you?
?
The argument shows that any attempt to add a positive attribute of 'self-necessitation' leads to absurdity. Have you ever tried to 'define' Allah's attributes in a way that implies limitation or composition? How does this lesson guard your heart against such thinking?
?
Realizing that Allah's necessity means He has no need of His creation, how does that challenge your tendency to seek validation from people? Will you today consciously remind yourself in a moment of seeking approval that Allah alone is self-sufficient and your worth comes from Him, not from their opinions?

Objection and Initial Refutation for the Positivity of Wujub
Sanusi presents two objections for wujub being positive and begins their refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the negative view of wujub and the author's initial responses, including the problematic definition of 'emphasis' and the reversal of the opposite argument.

Core Idea
The author shows that the objections to negativity can be turned back on the objector.

This lesson presents a classical objection (la yuqal) against the position that wujub is a negative concept. The objector offers two proofs: first, that wujub is emphasis of existence, which cannot be privative; second, that the opposite of wujub (la-wujub) applies only to pure non-existence, so wujub must be positive. Sanusi begins his refutation by analyzing the meaning of 'emphasis' and then countering the second proof with a parallel argument. However, the text cuts off mid-sentence for the second refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a 'la yuqal' followed by two proofs (al-awwal, al-thani) and then the answer (jawaab).
The text is cut off at the end, so the second refutation is incomplete.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two proofs the objector uses. The first defines wujub as emphasis (ta'kid) of existence. The second uses the opposite (naqid) argument, claiming la-wujub is pure non-existence.
Each proof has its own structure; note the reasoning.
3
Examine the first refutation
Study the author's response to the first proof. He questions the meaning of 'emphasis', showing that if it means need for a cause, it is negative; if it means self-necessitation, it leads to regress; if it means something else, it needs clarification.
This refutation avoids conceding the positivity of wujub.
4
Observe the beginning of the second refutation
Read the start of the author's answer to the second proof. He claims it is countered by a similar argument using 'not possible' and 'possible', but the text ends. Appreciate the dialectical style.
The argument is incomplete, but the strategy is clear: turn the opposite logic against the objector.

It is not said: The proof that it is a positive concept has two aspects: First: That wujub of existence is an emphasis (ta'kid) of existence. So if wujub were a privative qualification, it would necessitate that a thing be emphasized by its own negation and by its opposite, which is impossible. Second: That the opposite (naqid) of wujub is la-wujub (non-necessity). The concept of la-wujub applies either to the impossible (mumtani') or to the particular possible (mumkin khas). As for the impossible, it is pure non-existence. So that which it applies to must be pure non-existence, otherwise a positive attribute would inhere in pure non-existence, which is impossible. As for the particular possible, it is not impossible for it to remain in non-existence, and in that state it is described as non-necessary. Thus the concept of la-wujub applies to pure non-existence in this case too. Therefore it is necessary that the concept of la-wujub here is also pure non-existence. So by this proof, la-wujub is pure non-existence. Then it is necessary that the concept of wujub is pure positivity, because one of the two opposites must be a fixed concept. We say: As for the first aspect, its answer is: We say: What do you mean by saying 'wujub is emphasis of existence'? If you mean that what is necessary by essence is in need of a separate cause for its existence, this is intelligible, but this meaning is a negative concept, and that is clear. If you mean by it that the quiddity necessitates that existence, we have already proven that the necessitation of a thing for another cannot be an additional existent, otherwise regress would follow. And if you mean by this emphasis a third concept, then it must be clarified. As for the second aspect, its answer: That this is countered by our saying: 'It is not possible with particular possibility' is the opposite of our saying 'It is possible with particular possibility', but our saying: 'It'

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (la yuqal) against the conclusion that wujub is negative. The objector gives two proofs that wujub is positive. First proof: wujub is emphasis (ta'kid) of existence; if it were privative, a thing would be emphasized by its own negation, which is contradictory. Second proof: the opposite of wujub (la-wujub) applies to pure non-existence (for the impossible) or to a state of the possible, so la-wujub is pure non-existence; therefore its opposite (wujub) must be pure positivity. The author then begins the answer (jawaab). For the first aspect, he analyzes the meaning of 'emphasis': if it means need for a separate cause, it is negative; if it means self-necessitation, it leads to regress; if something else, it needs clarification. For the second aspect, he begins a counterargument using the pair 'not possible' and 'possible', but the text is cut off. Nonetheless, the structure shows the author refutes both objections.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since the opposite of wujub (la-wujub) is pure non-existence, wujub must be a positive concept.
Correction: The author shows that the argument can be reversed: if la-wujub is pure non-existence, then its opposite (wujub) could be pure positivity, but that does not necessarily prove that wujub is positive; the refutation begins to show that the meaning of 'opposite' can be turned around.

The author demonstrates that defining wujub as 'emphasis' can lead to regress or a negative meaning, and that the opposite argument can be turned around.

Proof that the Concept of Necessary Existence is Privative
Author argues that wujūb bil-dhāt cannot be a positive concept because it negates special possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that the concept of necessary existence by essence (wujūb bi al-dhāt) is privative rather than positive, by analyzing the relationship between possibility and impossibility.

Core Idea
Necessary existence negates a positive concept (special possibility), so it must be privative.

This lesson examines a sophisticated argument by the author to demonstrate that the concept of necessary existence (wujūb bi al-dhāt) cannot be a positive concept as some might think. Instead, because it is defined by negating special possibility—which is a positive concept—the resulting concept of necessary existence is privative. The passage uses careful logical distinctions to avoid contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the four key terms: wujūb bi al-dhāt (necessary existence by essence), imkān khāṣṣ (special possibility), mumtaniʿ (impossible), and the concepts of positive (thubūtī) and privative (ʿadamī). These are the building blocks of the argument.
Special possibility refers to the possibility of an object's existence in itself, not just relative to a cause.
2
Follow the initial contrast
Read the first part where the author contrasts 'not possible with special possibility' (applied to pure non-existence) and 'possible with special possibility' (a positive concept). Note how he establishes that the negation of special possibility yields a privative, not a positive, statement.
The author uses a reductio: if 'not possible with special possibility' were positive, an existent would be true of a non-existent.
3
Trace the core argument
Identify how the author defines necessary existence as the negation of special possibility and of impossibility. Since special possibility is positive, its negation must be privative. This is the heart of the proof.
The parallel argument about impossibility shows that if necessary existence negated only impossibility (a privative), it would be positive, but it negates both.
4
Consider the parallel and conclusion
Examine the author's analogy: if necessary existence as a remover of impossibility would be positive, then as a remover of special possibility it must be privative. Understand why the conclusion follows necessarily.
The argument does not rely on any one premise being false; it shows a logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس بممكن بالإمكان الخاص يصدق على الممتنع الذي هو عدم محض فوجب أن يكون قولنا : إنه ليس بممكن بالإمكان الخاص عدما محضا ، وإلا لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم. وإذا كان كذلك كان قولنا : إنه ممكن بالإمكان الخاص ، مفهوما ثبوتيا. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الوجوب بالذات عبارة عن نفي الإمكان الخاص ومن نفي الامتناع وثبت أن الإمكان الخاص مفهوم ثبوتي ، وأن الامتناع مفهوم عدمي ، فإن لزم من كون الوجوب بالذات رافعا للامتناع ، كونه ثبوتيا لأن رافع العدم ثبوت لزم من كون هذا الوجوب بالذات ، رافعا للامتناع ، كونه ثبوتيا لأن رافع العدم ثبوت لزم من كون هذا الوجوب بالذات رافعا للإمكان الخاص كونه عدميا ، لأن رافع الثبوت عدم ، وإن لم يلزم هذا ، لم يلزم ذاك أيضا . فهذا مجموع المباحث الدالة على أن المفهوم من الوجوب بالذات [ يمتنع كونه ثبوتيا .
It is not possible with special possibility that it applies to the impossible which is pure non-existence. So it must be that our statement: 'It is not possible with special possibility' is pure non-existence, otherwise the existence of the existent with the non-existent would follow. If that is the case, our statement: 'It is possible with special possibility' is a positive concept. And if this is established, we say: Necessary existence by essence (al-wujūb bi al-dhāt) is an expression of the negation of special possibility and of the negation of impossibility. And it is established that special possibility is a positive concept, and that impossibility is a privative concept. For if it follows from necessary existence by essence being a remover of impossibility that it is positive—because the remover of non-existence is positive—then from this necessary existence by essence being a remover of special possibility it follows that it is privative, because the remover of positivity is non-existence. And if this does not follow, that does not follow either. So this is the sum of the discussions indicating that the concept of necessary existence by essence [is impossible to be positive].
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the concept of necessary existence by essence (wujūb bi al-dhāt) cannot be a positive (thubūtī) concept but must be a privative (ʿadamī) one. The author begins by distinguishing two types of impossibility: that which is pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ) and that which is possible in some sense. He argues that 'not possible with special possibility' applies to the impossible which is pure non-existence, making that statement itself a non-existence. Otherwise, an existent would be predicated of a non-existent. Then he notes that 'possible with special possibility' is a positive concept. The key step: necessary existence is defined as the negation of special possibility and the negation of impossibility. Since special possibility is positive, negating it yields a privative concept. The author then draws a parallel: if necessary existence were a remover of impossibility (which is privative), it would be positive, but in fact it is a remover of special possibility (which is positive), so it must be privative. He concludes that the entire discussion demonstrates that the concept of necessary existence cannot be positive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying a concept necessarily makes the denial a positive attribute.
Correction: The nature of what is denied determines the result: negating a positive yields a privative, while negating a privative yields a positive.

Checkpoint

B) Because it negates special possibility, which is positive.
C) Because it is a concept shared by multiple beings.
D) Because it is a simple attribute.

Key Takeaway
The concept of necessary existence is privative because it negates a positive concept (special possibility), not because it negates a privative concept.

The author argues that necessary existence negates special possibility, making it privative. How do you reconcile the privative nature of His existence with the overwhelming positive attributes you affirm about Him?
?
If the concept of necessary existence is privative, then it is not something you can fully grasp as a positive concept. Do you trust that the reality of God's existence is beyond your conceptualization, or do you try to force it into a positive box? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that God's existence is a negation of all limitations?

Privative Concepts and Composition
Argument that sharing privative concept does not cause composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sharing a privative concept like necessary existence does not cause composition in the essence.

Core Idea
Sharing privative concepts does not imply composition.

This passage addresses a potential objection: if necessary existence is a privative concept, wouldn't multiple necessary beings share something and thus be composite? The author shows that privative concepts are different; sharing them doesn't create composition, using the example of simples that share negations without becoming composite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice the terms 'privative' (salbī) and 'simple' (basīṭa).
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that sharing a privative concept does not necessitate composition. Contrast this with a positive attribute which might.
3
Work through the proof
The proof uses the fact that every simple quiddity shares negations with other simples, yet remains simple. If sharing negations caused composition, simples would be composite, which is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Apply the proof to necessary existence: since it is privative, multiple necessary beings sharing it does not make them composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : في بيان أنه لما كان المفهوم من الوجوب بالذات (۱) ] أمرا سلبيا ، لم يلزم منه كون الواجب لذاته مركبا. وتقريره : أنه على هذا التقدير يكون الاشتراك حاصلا في هذا المفهوم السلبي، ويكون الامتياز حاصلا بتمام تلك الحقيقة ، وهذا لا يوجب وقوع التركيب في الحقيقة . والدليل عليه : أنه لا شك في وجود ماهية من الماهيات وتلك الماهيات إما أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة ، فإن كانت بسيطة فقد حصلت الماهية البسيطة ، وإن كانت مركبة فالمركب إنما يتولد من البسائط . فالقول بإثبات الماهية البسيطة أمر لابد من الاعتراف به ، على كل حال ثم إن كل بسيطين ، فإنه لابد وأن يشتركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنها ، فإن كان الاشتراك في السلوب يوجب وقوع التركيب في الماهية ، لزم كون البسيط مركبا ، وذلك محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن المفهوم من الوجوب بالذات مفهوم سلبي ، وثبت أنه متى وقع الاشتراك في المفهومات السلبية ، فإنه لا يلزم وقوع التركيب في الماهية ، فيلزم أن بتقدير وجود أشياء يكون كل واحد منها واجب الوجود لذاته ، فإن لا يلزم وقوع التركيب في ذات كل واحد منها [ وهذا آخر الكلام في تقرير هذا المقام (۱) ]
The second position: an explanation that since the concept of necessary existence by essence is a privative matter, it does not entail that the necessary being by essence is composite. Its exposition: on this assumption, the commonality occurs in this privative concept, and the distinction occurs by the entirety of that reality, and this does not necessitate the occurrence of composition in the reality. The proof for it: there is no doubt about the existence of a quiddity among quiddities, and those quiddities are either simple or composite. If it is simple, then the simple quiddity has occurred; if it is composite, the composite only arises from simples. Therefore, affirming the simple quiddity is something that must be acknowledged in any case. Then, any two simples must share in the negation of everything other than them from them. If sharing in negations necessitates the occurrence of composition in the quiddity, then the simple would be composite, which is absurd. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that the concept of necessary existence by essence is a privative concept, and it is established that whenever sharing occurs in privative concepts, the occurrence of composition in the quiddity does not follow. Therefore, on the supposition of the existence of things each of which is necessary existence by essence, composition does not occur in the essence of each one of them. [This is the end of the discourse in establishing this position.]
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an explanation (bayān) of the second position (al-maqām al-thānī). The author argues that even if the concept of necessary existence by essence is privative (salbī), this does not force the necessary being to be composite (murakkab). The reasoning is that multiple beings can share a privative concept without that sharing causing composition in their essences. The proof: every simple quiddity (māhiyya basīṭa) shares with other simples the negation of everything else, yet that does not make it composite. If sharing in negations caused composition, then every simple would be composite, which is absurd. Therefore, if we suppose multiple necessary beings, they share only the privative concept of necessary existence, and their distinctness comes from the entirety of each reality (tamām tilka al-ḥaqīqa). Hence composition does not occur. The author concludes by stating that this establishes the point.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing any common predicate between multiple beings implies a real universal inhering in them, causing composition in their essences.
Correction: The author shows that privative predicates (like 'not being other') can be shared without causing composition, using the example of simples that all share the negation of everything else yet remain simple.

Key Takeaway
Sharing a privative concept, like necessary existence, does not entail composition in the essence, as shown by the example of simples sharing negations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does this common label cause you to lose your unique self? How does this change the way you view your individual responsibility before God?

If necessary existence is a privation, then your own contingent existence is a positive addition from God. When you look at your life, do you treat your existence as a borrowed gift that defines you, or as a mere negation of non-existence? How would your gratitude and humility change if you truly internalized that your very being is not a possession but a privation of non-being sustained by the Necessary Being?

Determination is Not a Positive Attribute: Two Proofs
Author argues that determination (ta'ayyun) is not a positive attribute, using two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that determination (ta'ayyun) cannot be a positive attribute and how the two proofs demonstrate that.

Core Idea
Determination cannot be a positive attribute; if it were, it would lead to infinite regress and contradiction.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection to his earlier arguments. Even if we grant that necessary existence is a positive matter and that multiple necessary beings are distinct by their determinations, the objector might still ask: why must determination itself be a positive attribute? The author responds with two proofs, showing that treating determination as a positive attribute leads to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the author frames the question: why must determination be a positive attribute? This sets up the need to prove otherwise.
2
Analyze the first proof
The first argument: if determination were positive, each determination would require a further determination of itself, leading to infinite regress. Follow the reasoning step by step.
Notice the use of the concepts of shared quiddity and individuation.
3
Examine the second proof
The second argument: in any multiplicity, there must be a unit. That unit is determined, so its determination would be additional to it, making it not truly one — a contradiction.
The passage cuts off, but the logic is clear.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Both proofs aim to show that determination cannot be a positive attribute, preserving the non-composite nature of the necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن سلمنا أن المفهوم من وجوب الوجود بالذات أمر ثابت ، وسلمنا أن تلك الأشياء متباينة بتعيناتها ، فلم قلتم : إن تعين كل متعين أمر ثابت ، حتى يلزم عليه كون كل واحد من تلك الأشخاص مركبا في ذاته ؟ فنفتقر مهنا إلى بيان أن التعين ليس أمرا ثابتا ثم إلى بيان أنه من كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يلزم وقوع التركيب . أما بيان أن التعين لا يجوز أن يكون أمرا ثابتا ، فيدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول: إن التعين لو كان أمراً ثابتاً، لوجب أن يكون تعين هذا الشخص ، مغايرا لتعين ذلك الشخص الآخر إذ لو كان تعين هذا ، نفس تعين ذاك الآخر ، لكان هذا هو عين ذاك الآخر ، وذلك يمنع من حصول التعدد وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذه التعينات المتعددة تكون متشاركة في الماهية المسماة بالتعين ، ومتباينة بتشخصاتها ، فيلزم أن يكون تعين التعين زائدا عليه . ويفضي إلى التسلسل [ لكن القول بالتسلسل (۲) ) يوجب القول [ بوجود أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها. لأن تعين كل متعين ، علة ، لحصول ذلك المتعين . لكن القول بوجود أسباب ومسببات، لا نهاية لها محال (۳) ] فكان هذا محالا وههنا وجه آخر يدل على امتناع هذا وذاك وهو : إن كل كثرة فإن الواحد فيها موجود ، فلو حصلت هذه الكثرة ، لكان الواحد فيها حاصلا ، لكن أي شيء فرض كونه واحدا ، فذلك الواحد متعين ، فتعينه زائد عليه ، فلا يكون ذلك الواحد واحدا ، وقد فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف فيثبت : أن القول بأن التعين زائد على
The second question: If we grant that the concept of necessary existence by essence is a positive matter, and we grant that those things are distinct by their determinations, then why do you say that the determination of each determined thing is a positive matter, such that it follows that each one of those individuals is composite in its essence? So we need here to show that determination is not a positive matter, and then to show that if that is the case, composition does not follow. As for the demonstration that determination cannot be a positive matter, it is indicated by [several] ways: The first way: If determination were a positive matter, then the determination of this person must be different from the determination of that other person. For if the determination of this were the very determination of that, then this would be the very that, and that prevents multiplicity. And if this is established, we say: These multiple determinations share in the quiddity called determination, and are distinguished by their individuations, so it follows that the determination of the determination is additional to it, and this leads to infinite regress. But [the claim of infinite regress necessitates the claim of] the existence of causes and effects without end. Because the determination of each determined thing is a cause for the occurrence of that determined thing. But the claim of existence of causes and effects without end is impossible. So this was impossible. And here is another way that indicates the impossibility of this and that: Every multiplicity has the one present in it. So if this multiplicity occurs, the one would be present in it. But whatever thing is supposed to be one, that one is determined, so its determination is additional to it, so that one would not be one, and we have supposed it so. This is a contradiction. So it is established that the claim that determination is additional is [impossible].
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (al-su'āl al-thānī). Even granting that necessary existence is positive and that multiple necessary beings are distinct by their determinations (ta'ayyunāt), the author asks why determination itself must be a positive attribute, which would cause composition. He then undertakes to prove that determination is not a positive matter. He gives two proofs. The first proof: If determination were positive, each determination would be distinct, but then all determinations would share the quiddity of determination, requiring a further determination for each, leading to infinite regress. Infinite regress of causes is impossible. The second proof (which is incomplete in the text): In any multiplicity, there is a unit. That unit is determined, so its determination would be additional to it, making it not truly one, contradicting its unity. Thus determination cannot be an additional positive attribute. The passage cuts off at the conclusion that the claim that determination is additional is false.

Key Terms
سؤال ثاني
تعين
ثبوتي
تركيب
تسلسل
كثرة
واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determination is a real positive attribute added to the essence of each thing.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to infinite regress or contradiction, so determination cannot be a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against determination being a positive attribute?
A) It would make all beings identical.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress of determinations.
C) It would cause composition in the essence.
D) It would contradict the unity of God.

Key Takeaway
Determination is not a positive attribute because that would entail infinite regress or contradiction, both of which are impossible.

?

?
The second proof shows that if determination were additional, the one would no longer be one. In your spiritual life, are you trying to 'add' things to your faith that actually fragment your devotion to Allah? Fourth Argument: Determination Implies Compositeness and Possibility
The author argues that additional determination leads to compositeness and possibility, eliminating a Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians argue that if determination were an additional attribute, it would lead to the impossibility of a Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Determination cannot be an additional attribute because it would make God possible.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument against treating determination (ta'ayyun) as an additional concept. If determination were extra, it would be composite, and compositeness leads to possibility, which contradicts the nature of the Necessary Existent. This argument is part of a larger effort to uphold the simplicity and necessity of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once and then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, two branches, and conclusion.
The argument is compact; take your time to follow each condition.
2
Identify the two parts of determination
The argument says determination, if additional, would be composed of two parts. Understand that this is a hypothetical scenario.
The two parts are not specified; they are abstract components.
3
Trace the two branches
Consider each part: if both are necessary, we get multiple necessary existents—contradiction. If one part is possible, the composite is possible, making every determinate thing possible—contradiction.
Both branches lead to impossibility.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the conclusion is impossible, the premise must be false: determination is not an additional concept. This supports the simplicity of God.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إن التعين لو كان مفهوماً زائداً ثابتاً، لكان هذا التعين مركباً من جزئين. وكل واحد من هذين الجزئين، إن كان واجباً لذاته، صار واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، وأنتم إنما تذكرون هذه الدلائل لإبطاله، وإن لم يكن كذلك، فحينئذ يلزم القول يكون أحد هذين الجزئين ممكناً لذاته، وما يكون أحد أجزائه ممكناً لذاته كان ذلك المركب أولى بالإمكان، فيلزم أن يكون كل ذات معينة مشخصة في الوجود أن تكون ممكنة لذاتها . وهذا يقتضي أن لا يحصل في الوجود موجود معين، يكون هو في نفسه واجب الوجود، وذلك محال.
The fourth argument: If determination were an additional fixed concept, then this determination would be composed of two parts. And each of these two parts, if it is necessary in itself, then the Necessary Existent would become more than one, whereas you only mention these proofs to invalidate that. And if it is not so, then it would necessitate that one of these two parts is possible in itself, and whatever has one of its parts possible in itself, that composite is more deserving of possibility. So it would necessitate that every determinate individualized essence in existence is possible in itself. And this entails that there does not occur in existence any determinate existent that is in itself Necessary Existent, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rābi‘). It argues that if determination were an additional fixed concept (mafhūm zā’id thābit), then determination itself would be composite (murakkab), consisting of two parts. Each part is either necessary (wājib) or possible (mumkin). If both parts are necessary, then we would have more than one necessary existent, which is invalid. If a part is possible, then the composite is more deserving of possibility, meaning that every determinate thing would be possible, leaving no room for a necessary existent. Since a necessary existent (e.g., God) exists, determination cannot be an additional concept.

Key Terms

مركب
جزء
واجب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that compositeness does not entail possibility; a composite could still be necessary.
Correction: The argument shows that if any part is possible, the composite as a whole inherits possibility ('more deserving of possibility'). Since the necessary existent cannot be possible, this branch is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, if one part of determination is possible, what follows about the composite?
A) It becomes necessary.
B) It is more deserving of possibility.
C) It becomes impossible.
D) It remains the same.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that determination cannot be an additional attribute, as it would lead to compositeness and thereby to possibility, contradicting the existence of a Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What concrete action will you take today to acknowledge your absolute need for Him?
?
The argument shows that any compositeness in God would make Him possible. Yet we often speak of God’s attributes as if they are separable parts. How can you purify your understanding of Allah’s oneness so that your worship reflects the reality that His essence and attributes are one, not multiple additions?
?
If the Necessary Existent were not one, the entire universe could not exist. Think of one area of your life where you act as if there can be other ultimate authorities besides Allah. What is that area, and how will you demolish that false polytheism (shirk) today?

Third Question: Multiplicity is Inevitable Even on Divine Unity
Opponent's objection: multiplicity necessary even on unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent argues that composition and multiplicity are unavoidable even for a single necessary being, based on the relationship between necessary existence and individuation.

Core Idea
Even one necessary being involves composition of essence and individuation.

In this passage, the opponent responds to the argument for divine unity by turning the tables: if multiplicity is a problem, it applies just as much to a single necessary being. The opponent concedes that necessary existence and individuation are real, but then shows that any existing necessary being must be composed of a universal essence and an individuating attribute, leading to a possible component. This forces a rethink of what it means for something to be necessary of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the opponent's concession and the main claim that multiplicity is necessary even on unity.
The passage contains the first reason in full and the beginning of the second.
2
Identify the structure
The opponent states the objection, then gives two reasons (wajhān). The first reason is fully given; the second is cut off. Focus on the first reason's logical flow.
3
Trace the first reason's argument
Follow the steps: The First Principle is not merely a universal essence; what exists is an individuated essence. Individuation is an attribute, dependent on the described, thus possible in itself. This makes the essence composed, and everything external becomes possible.
Notice the conclusion via contrapositive: only possible things exist externally.
4
Consider the implication
The opponent concludes that the objection against multiplicity applies equally to unity. Reflect on why this is a powerful counterargument.
The second reason would likely reinforce this, but is incomplete here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أن وجوب الوجود، مفهوم ثابت، وأن التعين مفهوم ثابت، إلا أنا نقول : والكثرة التي التزمتموها لازمة على القول بوحدة واجب الوجود ، وإذا كان هذا المحذور لازماً ، سواء قلنا بتوحيد واجب الوجود أم لم نقل به فحينئذ يسقط هذا الاستدلال · وإنما قلنا : إنه لازم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود لوجوه : الأول : إن المبدأ الأول ليس عبارة عن محض وجوب الوجود ، الذي هو ما هية كلية [ لا يمنع نفس مفهومها عن وقوع الشركة فيها . لأن الماهية الكلية ] لا وجود لها في الأعيان البتة بل الموجود في الأعيان ذات معينة موصوفة بكونها واجبة الوجود ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة لواجب الوجود ، والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فذلك التعين ممكن لذاته ، وهو معلول بوجوب الوجود، فيلزم أن تكون الذات المعينة مركبة من جزءين أحدهما ممكن لذاته . والذي يكون جزء ماهيته لذاته، كان هو بالإمكان أولى ، فيلزم أن تكون تلك الذات المعينة ممكنة الوجود لذاتها ، فعلى هذا كل ما هو موجود في الأعيان فهو ممكن لذاته ، فينعكس انعكاس النقيض : إن كل ما لا يكون ممكنا لذاته ، فهو ليس بموجود في الأعيان. فيثبت : أن الإلزام الذي أوردتموه على القول بكثرة واجب الوجود لذاته هو بعينه قائم مع القول بوحدة واجب الوجود لذاته الثاني : وهو إن مدار كلامكم على أنه لو كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد ، لكان كل واحد منهما مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره ومن التعين الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، ثم إن كان الوجوب علة [ لذلك التعين ، فكل
The third question: We concede that necessary existence (wujūb al-wujūd) is a fixed concept, and that individuation (ta'ayyun) is a fixed concept, but we say: the multiplicity (kathra) which you have adhered to is necessary (lāzima) on the statement of the unity of the Necessary Being. And if this unpleasant consequence (maḥdhūr) is necessary, whether we affirm the unity of the Necessary Being or do not affirm it, then this inference (istidlāl) falls. And we only say that it is necessary with the statement of the unity of the Necessary Being for reasons: First: The First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is not merely necessary existence, which is a universal essence (māhiyya kulliyya) [that does not prevent its very concept from being shared in. For the universal essence] has no existence in external entities at all; rather, what exists in external entities is an individuated essence (dhāt mu'ayyana) described as being necessary of existence. So that individuation is additional to the essence of necessary existence. And that individuation must be an attribute (ṣifa). And the attribute is in need of the described (mawṣūf), and that which is in need of another is possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihi). So that individuation is possible in itself, and it is an effect (ma'lūl) of necessary existence. Hence it follows that the individuated essence is composed of two parts, one of which is possible in itself. And that which is part of its essence by itself is more entitled to being possible. So it follows that that individuated essence is possible of existence in itself. Thus, everything that exists in external entities is possible in itself. Then the contrapositive follows: Everything that is not possible in itself does not exist in external entities. So it is established that the objection (ilzām) which you raised against the statement of multiplicity of the Necessary Being in itself is exactly the same as that which stands against the statement of the unity of the Necessary Being in itself. Second: And it is that the pivot of your statement is that if the Necessary Being were more than one, each one of them would be composed of the necessity (wujūb) by which it shares with the other and of the individuation by which it is distinguished from the other. Then, if the necessity is the cause ('illa) of that individuation, then every...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection from the opponent. The opponent concedes that necessity of existence and individuation are real concepts but argues that the accusation of multiplicity (kathra) leveled against those who affirm multiple necessary beings applies equally to those who affirm a single necessary being. If this consequence is unavoidable regardless, then the argument for unity loses its force. The author then gives two reasons (wajhān) to support this claim. The first reason (completed in this portion) argues that the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is not merely a universal concept of necessary existence; rather, what exists externally is an individuated essence (dhāt mu'ayyana) described as necessary. This individuation is an attribute additional to the essence of necessary existence. Since an attribute depends on the described, it is possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihi) and an effect of necessary existence. Therefore, the individuated essence is composed of two parts: one necessary (the necessity) and one possible (the individuation). This implies that everything existing externally is possible in itself, which leads to a contrapositive: whatever is not possible does not exist externally. Hence, the opponent's criticism of multiplicity holds equally for the unity position. The second reason begins but is cut off; it points to the opponent's own argument about composition from shared necessity and distinguishing individuation, and then asks whether necessity is the cause of individuation, but the text breaks off at 'fakull'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only the multiplicity of necessary beings leads to composition, but the objection shows that even a single necessary being has composition of essence and individuation.
Correction: Composition is unavoidable whether one affirms one or many necessary beings, because any existing necessary being requires individuation, which is a dependent attribute and thus possible.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's main claim in the third question?
A) That necessity and individuation are impossible concepts.
B) That the problem of multiplicity applies regardless of affirming one or many necessary beings.
C) That the unity argument is sound despite composition.
D) That individuation is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that composition from necessary existence and individuation is unavoidable even for a single necessary being, thus the problem of multiplicity does not uniquely challenge polytheism.

?

The opponent uses logic to challenge the necessity of the Necessary Being. In moments of doubt, do you turn to rational arguments or to the Qur'an and sunna for reassurance? Write down a specific verse or hadith that strengthens your tawḥīd and recite it daily this week.

The Third Objection: Same Difficulty for Oneness
The opponent's third objection against the proof of divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's objection that the alleged difficulty of multiplicity applies equally to the belief in one Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
The opponent claims the proof for oneness is self-defeating.

Before the author presents his refutation, we must first grasp the opponent's challenge. The opponent grants key concepts but argues that the very problem the author used against multiple necessary existents also arises if there is only one. This sets the stage for the author's detailed response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage containing the opponent's objection. Notice how they concede the concepts of necessity and determination but then argue the same difficulty (mahdhur) arises whether one or many.
The objection is framed as a collapse of the proof for oneness.
2
Identify the conceded points
The opponent grants that 'necessity of existence' and 'determination' are fixed concepts. Understand why these concessions are strategic.
3
Understand the 'same difficulty' argument
The opponent says the multiplicity you claimed follows from many necessary existents also follows from one. Ask yourself: what exactly is this 'difficulty'? The author will later clarify it.
The difficulty likely refers to composition of necessity and determination.
4
Prepare for the refutation
Notice the author begins his reply with 'وإنما قلنا' indicating he will show the difficulty is indeed necessary even with the affirmation of oneness. This lesson is just the setup.
The refutation follows in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أن وجوب الوجود، مفهوم ثابت ، وأن التعين مفهوم ثابت ، إلا أنا نقول : والكثرة التي التزمتموها لازمة على القول بوحدة واجب الوجود ، وإذا كان هذا المحذور لازماً ، سواء قلنا بتوحيد واجب الوجود أم لم نقل به فحينئذ يسقط هذا الاستدلال
وإنما قلنا : إنه لازم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود لوجوه :
الأول : إن المبدأ الأول ليس عبارة عن محض وجوب الوجود ، الذي هو ما هية كلية لا يمنع نفس مفهومها عن وقوع الشركة فيها . لأن الماهية الكلية لا وجود لها في الأعيان البتة بل الموجود في الأعيان ذات معينة موصوفة بكونها واجبة الوجود ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة لواجب الوجود ، والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فذلك التعين ممكن لذاته ، وهو معلول بوجوب الوجود، فيلزم أن تكون الذات المعينة مركبة من جزءين أحدهما ممكن لذاته . والذي يكون جزء ماهيته لذاته، كان هو بالإمكان أولى ، فيلزم أن تكون تلك الذات المعينة ممكنة الوجود لذاتها ، فعلى هذا كل ما هو موجود في الأعيان فهو ممكن لذاته ، فينعكس انعكاس النقيض : إن كل ما لا يكون ممكنا لذاته ، فهو ليس بموجود في الأعيان. فيثبت : أن الإلزام الذي أوردتموه على القول بكثرة واجب الوجود لذاته هو بعينه قائم مع القول بوحدة واجب الوجود لذاته
الثاني : وهو إن مدار كلامكم على أنه لو كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد ، لكان كل واحد منهما مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره ومن التعين الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، ثم إن كان الوجوب علة لذلك التعين ، فكل صفات واجب الوجود هو ذلك التعين ، وإن كان ذلك التعين علة للوجوب يلزم أن يكون وجوب الوجود ، معلول شيء آخر . فنقول : هذا لازم عليكم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود . وذلك لأن الوجوب صفة عارضة لاتصاف الماهية بالوجود وذلك لأن الوجود والإمكان والامتناع جهات ، وهي للرابطة الحاصلة بين الموضوع والمحمول ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان الوجوب على كل التقديرات صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، وكل صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، فهي ممكنة لذاتها معللة بالغير. فيثبت أن هذا المحذور لازم . سواء قلنا : إن واجب الوجود لذاته واحداً ، أو قلنا إنه أكثر من واحد .
الثالث : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن من الماهيات ما يكون مستلزما لذاته ماهية أخرى ، مثل قولنا : إن الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية ، وإن الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية وكون الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية أمر واجب لذاته لا لغيره ، وكون الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية أمر واجب لذاته فهذه الوجوبات الذاتية أشياء كثيرة . فالواجب لذاته أكثر من الواحد .
فإن قالوا : هذه الوجوبات الذاتية ليست ذوات قائمة بأنفسها ، وإنما هي أحوال نسبية اعتبارية ، وكلا منا في أن الذوات القائمة بنفسها ، الواجبة الوجود لذاتها، يجب أن تكون واحدة. فنقول في الجواب : أنتم بنيتم التقسيم الذي ذكر ثموه في توحيد واجب الوجود لذاته على مفهوم الوجوب بالذات . والوجوب بالذات حاصل في هذه الاعتبارات ، لأنها من حيث هي هي ، أمور واجبة لذواتها ، ممتنعة التعيين لما هي هي، فكان هذا وجوبا بالذات ، فكان هذا نقضا على ما ذكرتموه .
The third question: We grant that necessity of existence is a fixed concept, and that determination is a fixed concept, but we say: The multiplicity which you have affirmed is necessary upon the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. And if this difficulty is necessary, whether we affirm the oneness of the Necessary Existent or do not affirm it, then this proof collapses. And we only say: It is necessary with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent for reasons: The first: The First Principle is not mere necessity of existence, which is a universal essence that does not prevent its own concept from the occurrence of sharing in it. For the universal essence has no existence in external entities at all. Rather, what exists in external entities is a determined essence described as being necessary of existence. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute of the Necessary Existent. The attribute is dependent upon the described, and that which is dependent upon another is possible in itself. So that determination is possible in itself, and it is an effect of the necessity of existence. So it follows that the determined essence is composed of two parts, one of which is possible in itself. And that which is part of its essence in itself is more deserving of possibility. So it follows that that determined essence is possible of existence in itself. So on this basis, everything that exists in external entities is possible in itself. So the contrapositive follows: Every thing that is not possible in itself is not existent in external entities. So it is established that the objection which you brought against the statement of multiplicity of the Necessary Existent in itself is exactly the same as that which stands with the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself. The second: It is that your argument is based on the idea that if the Necessary Existent were more than one, each one would be composed of the necessity by which it shares with others and of the determination by which it is distinguished from others. Then if the necessity is the cause of that determination, then all attributes of the Necessary Existent are that determination; and if that determination is the cause of the necessity, then it would follow that the necessity of existence is an effect of something else. So we say: This is necessary upon you with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. That is because necessity is an accidental attribute qualifying the essence with existence, and because existence, possibility, and impossibility are modes, and they pertain to the connection between subject and predicate. And if the matter is so, then necessity in all cases is an attribute external to the essence, and every attribute external to the essence is possible in itself and caused by another. So it is established that this difficulty is necessary, whether we say that the Necessary Existent in itself is one or say that it is more than one. The third: We know by necessity that among essences, some entail by themselves another essence, such as our saying: three entails oddness, and four entails evenness. And the fact that three entails oddness is a thing necessary in itself, not for another, and the fact that four entails evenness is a thing necessary in itself. So these essential necessities are many things. So the Necessary in itself is more than one. If they say: These essential necessities are not essences subsisting by themselves; rather they are relative, consideration-based states. And both of us agree that essences subsisting by themselves that are necessary of existence in themselves must be one. So we say in the answer: You built the division which you mentioned in the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself upon the concept of necessity by essence. And necessity by essence exists in these considerations, because they in themselves are things necessary in themselves, impossible of determination as they are. So this is necessity by essence, and this is a refutation of what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection from the opponent against the proof for the oneness of the Necessary Existent (tawhid) and the author's refutation through three arguments. The opponent begins by conceding that both necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are fixed conceptual realities. They then argue that the multiplicity which the author claims follows from the assumption of multiple Necessary Existents also follows from the assumption of one, so the proof for oneness is invalid. The author responds by stating that this difficulty (mahdhur) is indeed necessary even with the affirmation of oneness, and they explain this through several reasons. The first reason: The First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is not bare necessity of existence, which is a universal essence. A universal essence cannot exist in external entities; rather, what exists externally is a determined essence described as necessary. This determination (ta'ayyun) is additional to the essence of necessity, and it must be an attribute (sifa). Attributes depend on their subject, and anything dependent on another is possible (mumkin) in itself. Thus, the determination is possible and is an effect of necessity, making the determined entity composite from a necessary and a possible part, leading to the conclusion that everything in external existence is possible—thus the problem the opponent raised against multiplicity equally applies to the one. The second reason: The opponent's argument assumes that if there were more than one Necessary, each would be composed of shared necessity and distinguishing determination. The author shows that regardless of which is cause, the necessity is an accidental attribute external to the essence, making it possible and caused. This applies even to the one Necessary, so the difficulty remains whether one or many. The third reason: There are many essential necessities in reality—e.g., the entailment of oddness by three and evenness by four are necessary in themselves. These multiple essential necessities show that there are things necessary in themselves beyond just one. The author then answers an anticipated objection: these are not self-subsisting essences but relational states. The response is that the opponent's own division in proving oneness is based on essential necessity (wujub bi al-dhat), and these considerations do possess essential necessity, thereby refuting the claim of oneness.

What does the opponent concede at the beginning of the third objection?

B) That the proof for oneness is valid.
C) That multiplicity is impossible.
D) That the Necessary Existent must be one.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the same difficulty used against multiple necessary existents also arises for the one, thus undermining the proof for oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent concedes truth but uses it to argue against oneness. Have you ever used partial truths to justify a doubt about your faith? Are you willing to hold onto the full truth even when it is challenged?
?
When you hear an intellectual objection to Tawhid, does your certainty waver, or do you trust that the truth is deeper than the objection? This passage shows the opponent's strategy. How will you prepare yourself to face such objections without losing conviction?
?
The opponent claims the same difficulty arises for oneness. Have you ever feared that your belief in one God is just as problematic as polytheism? How does this lesson remind you to examine the premises of such claims and seek deeper knowledge?
