First Reason: Composition Through Determination
The first reason in refuting the third objection: composition of the determined essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first refutation shows that any existent essence, including the One, is composed of necessity and determination, leading to contingency.

Core Idea
Determination added to necessity makes every existent composite and contingent.

The author presents his first argument in response to the objection. He explains that the First Principle cannot be bare necessity of existence; rather, it is a determined essence. This determination is an attribute that is possible in itself, making the whole composite and contingent. The same reasoning applies whether one or many.

Guided Path
1
Read the first reason passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first reason. Follow the logical steps: the First Principle is not mere universal necessity; determination is added; determination is an attribute; attributes are contingent; so the essence is composite and contingent.
This is a step-by-step argument leading to a contrapositive conclusion.
2
Identify the key concepts
Notice the terms: ماهية كلية (universal essence), ذات معينة (determined essence), صفة (attribute), ممكن (possible), معلول (effect). Understand how they fit together.
3
Follow the conclusion
The argument ends with the contrapositive: everything not possible in itself does not exist in external entities. Thus the difficulty the opponent raised applies to the one as well.
The author uses logical deduction to show the objection boomerangs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أن وجوب الوجود، مفهوم ثابت ، وأن التعين مفهوم ثابت ، إلا أنا نقول : والكثرة التي التزمتموها لازمة على القول بوحدة واجب الوجود ، وإذا كان هذا المحذور لازماً ، سواء قلنا بتوحيد واجب الوجود أم لم نقل به فحينئذ يسقط هذا الاستدلال
وإنما قلنا : إنه لازم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود لوجوه :
الأول : إن المبدأ الأول ليس عبارة عن محض وجوب الوجود ، الذي هو ما هية كلية لا يمنع نفس مفهومها عن وقوع الشركة فيها . لأن الماهية الكلية لا وجود لها في الأعيان البتة بل الموجود في الأعيان ذات معينة موصوفة بكونها واجبة الوجود ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة لواجب الوجود ، والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فذلك التعين ممكن لذاته ، وهو معلول بوجوب الوجود، فيلزم أن تكون الذات المعينة مركبة من جزءين أحدهما ممكن لذاته . والذي يكون جزء ماهيته لذاته، كان هو بالإمكان أولى ، فيلزم أن تكون تلك الذات المعينة ممكنة الوجود لذاتها ، فعلى هذا كل ما هو موجود في الأعيان فهو ممكن لذاته ، فينعكس انعكاس النقيض : إن كل ما لا يكون ممكنا لذاته ، فهو ليس بموجود في الأعيان. فيثبت : أن الإلزام الذي أوردتموه على القول بكثرة واجب الوجود لذاته هو بعينه قائم مع القول بوحدة واجب الوجود لذاته
الثاني : وهو إن مدار كلامكم على أنه لو كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد ، لكان كل واحد منهما مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره ومن التعين الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، ثم إن كان الوجوب علة لذلك التعين ، فكل صفات واجب الوجود هو ذلك التعين ، وإن كان ذلك التعين علة للوجوب يلزم أن يكون وجوب الوجود ، معلول شيء آخر . فنقول : هذا لازم عليكم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود . وذلك لأن الوجوب صفة عارضة لاتصاف الماهية بالوجود وذلك لأن الوجود والإمكان والامتناع جهات ، وهي للرابطة الحاصلة بين الموضوع والمحمول ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان الوجوب على كل التقديرات صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، وكل صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، فهي ممكنة لذاتها معللة بالغير. فيثبت أن هذا المحذور لازم . سواء قلنا : إن واجب الوجود لذاته واحداً ، أو قلنا إنه أكثر من واحد .
الثالث : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن من الماهيات ما يكون مستلزما لذاته ماهية أخرى ، مثل قولنا : إن الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية ، وإن الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية وكون الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية أمر واجب لذاته لا لغيره ، وكون الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية أمر واجب لذاته فهذه الوجوبات الذاتية أشياء كثيرة . فالواجب لذاته أكثر من الواحد .
فإن قالوا : هذه الوجوبات الذاتية ليست ذوات قائمة بأنفسها ، وإنما هي أحوال نسبية اعتبارية ، وكلا منا في أن الذوات القائمة بنفسها ، الواجبة الوجود لذاتها، يجب أن تكون واحدة. فنقول في الجواب : أنتم بنيتم التقسيم الذي ذكر ثموه في توحيد واجب الوجود لذاته على مفهوم الوجوب بالذات . والوجوب بالذات حاصل في هذه الاعتبارات ، لأنها من حيث هي هي ، أمور واجبة لذواتها ، ممتنعة التعيين لما هي هي، فكان هذا وجوبا بالذات ، فكان هذا نقضا على ما ذكرتموه .
The third question: We grant that necessity of existence is a fixed concept, and that determination is a fixed concept, but we say: The multiplicity which you have affirmed is necessary upon the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. And if this difficulty is necessary, whether we affirm the oneness of the Necessary Existent or do not affirm it, then this proof collapses. And we only say: It is necessary with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent for reasons: The first: The First Principle is not mere necessity of existence, which is a universal essence that does not prevent its own concept from the occurrence of sharing in it. For the universal essence has no existence in external entities at all. Rather, what exists in external entities is a determined essence described as being necessary of existence. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute of the Necessary Existent. The attribute is dependent upon the described, and that which is dependent upon another is possible in itself. So that determination is possible in itself, and it is an effect of the necessity of existence. So it follows that the determined essence is composed of two parts, one of which is possible in itself. And that which is part of its essence in itself is more deserving of possibility. So it follows that that determined essence is possible of existence in itself. So on this basis, everything that exists in external entities is possible in itself. So the contrapositive follows: Every thing that is not possible in itself is not existent in external entities. So it is established that the objection which you brought against the statement of multiplicity of the Necessary Existent in itself is exactly the same as that which stands with the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself. The second: It is that your argument is based on the idea that if the Necessary Existent were more than one, each one would be composed of the necessity by which it shares with others and of the determination by which it is distinguished from others. Then if the necessity is the cause of that determination, then all attributes of the Necessary Existent are that determination; and if that determination is the cause of the necessity, then it would follow that the necessity of existence is an effect of something else. So we say: This is necessary upon you with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. That is because necessity is an accidental attribute qualifying the essence with existence, and because existence, possibility, and impossibility are modes, and they pertain to the connection between subject and predicate. And if the matter is so, then necessity in all cases is an attribute external to the essence, and every attribute external to the essence is possible in itself and caused by another. So it is established that this difficulty is necessary, whether we say that the Necessary Existent in itself is one or say that it is more than one. The third: We know by necessity that among essences, some entail by themselves another essence, such as our saying: three entails oddness, and four entails evenness. And the fact that three entails oddness is a thing necessary in itself, not for another, and the fact that four entails evenness is a thing necessary in itself. So these essential necessities are many things. So the Necessary in itself is more than one. If they say: These essential necessities are not essences subsisting by themselves; rather they are relative, consideration-based states. And both of us agree that essences subsisting by themselves that are necessary of existence in themselves must be one. So we say in the answer: You built the division which you mentioned in the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself upon the concept of necessity by essence. And necessity by essence exists in these considerations, because they in themselves are things necessary in themselves, impossible of determination as they are. So this is necessity by essence, and this is a refutation of what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection from the opponent against the proof for the oneness of the Necessary Existent (tawhid) and the author's refutation through three arguments. The opponent begins by conceding that both necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are fixed conceptual realities. The author responds by stating that this difficulty (mahdhur) is indeed necessary even with the affirmation of oneness, and they explain this through several reasons. A universal essence cannot exist in external entities; rather, what exists externally is a determined essence described as necessary. Thus, the determination is possible and is an effect of necessity, making the determined entity composite from a necessary and a possible part, leading to the conclusion that everything in external existence is possible—thus the problem the opponent raised against multiplicity equally applies to the one. The second reason: The opponent's argument assumes that if there were more than one Necessary, each would be composed of shared necessity and distinguishing determination. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The First Principle is simply the concept of necessary existence with no further determination.
Correction: The author argues that a universal concept does not exist externally; all existents are determined, so the First Principle must have determination.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, why is the determination (ta'ayyun) considered possible in itself?
A) Because it is part of the essence of necessity.
B) Because it is an attribute dependent on a subject.
C) Because it is a universal concept.
D) Because it is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The first reason shows that any existent, including the One, is composite and contingent due to determination, so the objection applies to the opponent's view as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that a universal essence has no real existence—only determined essences do. When you affirm 'Allah exists,' do you mean a concept or a real, determined Being? How does shifting from a conceptual belief to affirming a real, determined Existent change the urgency of your worship?
?
The author says determination adds an attribute that makes even the Necessary Existent composite and dependent. Are there ways you have 'determined' Allah in your mind, adding your own attributes to Him? How can you purify your tawhid so that your understanding of Allah is not dependent on your own ideas?
?
If everything that exists externally is possible in itself, then even your own existence is a sign of dependence. Does that thought make you feel a sense of absolute need for Allah? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived as though you are possible and He alone is necessary?

Second Reason: Necessity as Accidental Attribute
The second reason: necessity as an accidental attribute causing contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second reason demonstrates that necessity of existence is an accidental attribute, making the subject contingent regardless of unity or multiplicity.

Core Idea
Necessity is an accidental attribute, not the essence itself, causing the same difficulty.

The author's second argument addresses the opponent's reasoning about necessity and determination. The author argues that in all cases, necessity (wujub) is an attribute external to the essence (kharija 'an al-mahiyya). Since every external attribute is possible and caused, the difficulty remains whether one or many.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reason passage
Read the Arabic passage. The author first summarizes the opponent's argument: if multiple, each composed of shared necessity and distinguishing determination. Then the author shows that this same structure applies to the one.
The key move: 'فنقول: هذا لازم عليكم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود'.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that necessity is an accidental attribute (sifa 'aradiyya) that qualifies an essence with existence. It relates to the connection between subject and predicate (al-rabita). As an external attribute, it is possible and caused.
This is a different line from the first reason, focusing on modality.
3
Conclude the argument
The conclusion is that this difficulty (mahdhur) is necessary whether we affirm one or many. Therefore the opponent's objection does not refute the proof for oneness.
The author turns the tables by showing the opponent's own assumptions lead to the same problem.

The third question: We grant that necessity of existence is a fixed concept, and that determination is a fixed concept, but we say: The multiplicity which you have affirmed is necessary upon the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. And if this difficulty is necessary, whether we affirm the oneness of the Necessary Existent or do not affirm it, then this proof collapses. And we only say: It is necessary with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent for reasons: The first: The First Principle is not mere necessity of existence, which is a universal essence that does not prevent its own concept from the occurrence of sharing in it. For the universal essence has no existence in external entities at all. Rather, what exists in external entities is a determined essence described as being necessary of existence. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute of the Necessary Existent. The attribute is dependent upon the described, and that which is dependent upon another is possible in itself. So that determination is possible in itself, and it is an effect of the necessity of existence. So it follows that the determined essence is composed of two parts, one of which is possible in itself. And that which is part of its essence in itself is more deserving of possibility. So it follows that that determined essence is possible of existence in itself. So on this basis, everything that exists in external entities is possible in itself. So the contrapositive follows: Every thing that is not possible in itself is not existent in external entities. So it is established that the objection which you brought against the statement of multiplicity of the Necessary Existent in itself is exactly the same as that which stands with the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself. The second: It is that your argument is based on the idea that if the Necessary Existent were more than one, each one would be composed of the necessity by which it shares with others and of the determination by which it is distinguished from others. Then if the necessity is the cause of that determination, then all attributes of the Necessary Existent are that determination; and if that determination is the cause of the necessity, then it would follow that the necessity of existence is an effect of something else. So we say: This is necessary upon you with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. That is because necessity is an accidental attribute qualifying the essence with existence, and because existence, possibility, and impossibility are modes, and they pertain to the connection between subject and predicate. And if the matter is so, then necessity in all cases is an attribute external to the essence, and every attribute external to the essence is possible in itself and caused by another. So it is established that this difficulty is necessary, whether we say that the Necessary Existent in itself is one or say that it is more than one. The third: We know by necessity that among essences, some entail by themselves another essence, such as our saying: three entails oddness, and four entails evenness. And the fact that three entails oddness is a thing necessary in itself, not for another, and the fact that four entails evenness is a thing necessary in itself. So these essential necessities are many things. So the Necessary in itself is more than one. If they say: These essential necessities are not essences subsisting by themselves; rather they are relative, consideration-based states. And both of us agree that essences subsisting by themselves that are necessary of existence in themselves must be one. So we say in the answer: You built the division which you mentioned in the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself upon the concept of necessity by essence. And necessity by essence exists in these considerations, because they in themselves are things necessary in themselves, impossible of determination as they are. So this is necessity by essence, and this is a refutation of what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection from the opponent against the proof for the oneness of the Necessary Existent (tawhid) and the author's refutation through three arguments. The opponent begins by conceding that both necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are fixed conceptual realities. They then argue that the multiplicity which the author claims follows from the assumption of multiple Necessary Existents also follows from the assumption of one, so the proof for oneness is invalid. The author responds by stating that this difficulty (mahdhur) is indeed necessary even with the affirmation of oneness, and they explain this through several reasons. The first reason: The First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is not bare necessity of existence, which is a universal essence. A universal essence cannot exist in external entities; rather, what exists externally is a determined essence described as necessary. This determination (ta'ayyun) is additional to the essence of necessity, and it must be an attribute (sifa). Attributes depend on their subject, and anything dependent on another is possible (mumkin) in itself. Thus, the determination is possible and is an effect of necessity, making the determined entity composite from a necessary and a possible part, leading to the conclusion that everything in external existence is possible—thus the problem the opponent raised against multiplicity equally applies to the one. The second reason: The opponent's argument assumes that if there were more than one Necessary, each would be composed of shared necessity and distinguishing determination. The author shows that regardless of which is cause, the necessity is an accidental attribute external to the essence, making it possible and caused. This applies even to the one Necessary, so the difficulty remains whether one or many. The third reason: There are many essential necessities in reality—e.g., the entailment of oddness by three and evenness by four are necessary in themselves. These multiple essential necessities show that there are things necessary in themselves beyond just one. The author then answers an anticipated objection: these are not self-subsisting essences but relational states. The response is that the opponent's own division in proving oneness is based on essential necessity (wujub bi al-dhat), and these considerations do possess essential necessity, thereby refuting the claim of oneness.

Third Reason and Final Response: Multiple Essential Necessities
The third reason: multiple essential necessities and the response to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third reason argues for multiple essential necessities (e.g., three's oddness) and how the author counters the objection that these are not self-subsistent.

Core Idea
Essential necessities are many, refuting the claim that only one Necessary exists.

The final reason introduces a direct counterexample: there are many things that are necessary in themselves, such as the entailment of oddness by three. The opponent may object that these are not self-subsistent essences. The author responds by showing that the opponent's own criterion of necessity by essence applies to these, thus the proof for oneness is undermined.

Guided Path
1
Read the third reason passage
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the appeal to self-evident knowledge: 'إنا نعلم بالضرورة'—we know by necessity that certain entailments are necessary in themselves.
Examples: three entails oddness, four entails evenness.
2
Understand the objection and response
The opponent says these are not self-subsisting essences but relational states. The author counters that the opponent's own division (taqsīm) for proving oneness is based on essential necessity (wujūb bi al-dhāt), and these considerations possess that.
This is a nuanced point: what constitutes 'essence' in the opponent's framework?
3
Reflect on the overall argument
This reason shows that there are multiple things necessary in themselves, so the claim that the Necessary in itself must be one is false. The author uses the opponent's own criterion to generate a counterexample.

السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أن وجوب الوجود، مفهوم ثابت ، وأن التعين مفهوم ثابت ، إلا أنا نقول : والكثرة التي التزمتموها لازمة على القول بوحدة واجب الوجود ، وإذا كان هذا المحذور لازماً ، سواء قلنا بتوحيد واجب الوجود أم لم نقل به فحينئذ يسقط هذا الاستدلال
وإنما قلنا : إنه لازم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود لوجوه :
الأول : إن المبدأ الأول ليس عبارة عن محض وجوب الوجود ، الذي هو ما هية كلية لا يمنع نفس مفهومها عن وقوع الشركة فيها . لأن الماهية الكلية لا وجود لها في الأعيان البتة بل الموجود في الأعيان ذات معينة موصوفة بكونها واجبة الوجود ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة ، فيكون ذلك التعين زائد على ماهية وجوب الوجود ، وذلك التعين لا بد وأن يكون صفة لواجب الوجود ، والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فذلك التعين ممكن لذاته ، وهو معلول بوجوب الوجود، فيلزم أن تكون الذات المعينة مركبة من جزءين أحدهما ممكن لذاته . والذي يكون جزء ماهيته لذاته، كان هو بالإمكان أولى ، فيلزم أن تكون تلك الذات المعينة ممكنة الوجود لذاتها ، فعلى هذا كل ما هو موجود في الأعيان فهو ممكن لذاته ، فينعكس انعكاس النقيض : إن كل ما لا يكون ممكنا لذاته ، فهو ليس بموجود في الأعيان. فيثبت : أن الإلزام الذي أوردتموه على القول بكثرة واجب الوجود لذاته هو بعينه قائم مع القول بوحدة واجب الوجود لذاته
الثاني : وهو إن مدار كلامكم على أنه لو كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد ، لكان كل واحد منهما مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره ومن التعين الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، ثم إن كان الوجوب علة لذلك التعين ، فكل صفات واجب الوجود هو ذلك التعين ، وإن كان ذلك التعين علة للوجوب يلزم أن يكون وجوب الوجود ، معلول شيء آخر . فنقول : هذا لازم عليكم مع القول بتوحيد واجب الوجود . وذلك لأن الوجوب صفة عارضة لاتصاف الماهية بالوجود وذلك لأن الوجود والإمكان والامتناع جهات ، وهي للرابطة الحاصلة بين الموضوع والمحمول ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان الوجوب على كل التقديرات صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، وكل صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، فهي ممكنة لذاتها معللة بالغير. فيثبت أن هذا المحذور لازم . سواء قلنا : إن واجب الوجود لذاته واحداً ، أو قلنا إنه أكثر من واحد .
الثالث : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن من الماهيات ما يكون مستلزما لذاته ماهية أخرى ، مثل قولنا : إن الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية ، وإن الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية وكون الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية أمر واجب لذاته لا لغيره ، وكون الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية أمر واجب لذاته فهذه الوجوبات الذاتية أشياء كثيرة . فالواجب لذاته أكثر من الواحد .
فإن قالوا : هذه الوجوبات الذاتية ليست ذوات قائمة بأنفسها ، وإنما هي أحوال نسبية اعتبارية ، وكلا منا في أن الذوات القائمة بنفسها ، الواجبة الوجود لذاتها، يجب أن تكون واحدة. فنقول في الجواب : أنتم بنيتم التقسيم الذي ذكر ثموه في توحيد واجب الوجود لذاته على مفهوم الوجوب بالذات . والوجوب بالذات حاصل في هذه الاعتبارات ، لأنها من حيث هي هي ، أمور واجبة لذواتها ، ممتنعة التعيين لما هي هي، فكان هذا وجوبا بالذات ، فكان هذا نقضا على ما ذكرتموه .
The third question: We grant that necessity of existence is a fixed concept, and that determination is a fixed concept, but we say: The multiplicity which you have affirmed is necessary upon the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. And if this difficulty is necessary, whether we affirm the oneness of the Necessary Existent or do not affirm it, then this proof collapses. And we only say: It is necessary with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent for reasons: The first: The First Principle is not mere necessity of existence, which is a universal essence that does not prevent its own concept from the occurrence of sharing in it. For the universal essence has no existence in external entities at all. Rather, what exists in external entities is a determined essence described as being necessary of existence. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute. So that determination is additional to the essence of necessity of existence, and that determination must be an attribute of the Necessary Existent. The attribute is dependent upon the described, and that which is dependent upon another is possible in itself. So that determination is possible in itself, and it is an effect of the necessity of existence. So it follows that the determined essence is composed of two parts, one of which is possible in itself. And that which is part of its essence in itself is more deserving of possibility. So it follows that that determined essence is possible of existence in itself. So on this basis, everything that exists in external entities is possible in itself. So the contrapositive follows: Every thing that is not possible in itself is not existent in external entities. So it is established that the objection which you brought against the statement of multiplicity of the Necessary Existent in itself is exactly the same as that which stands with the statement of the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself. The second: It is that your argument is based on the idea that if the Necessary Existent were more than one, each one would be composed of the necessity by which it shares with others and of the determination by which it is distinguished from others. Then if the necessity is the cause of that determination, then all attributes of the Necessary Existent are that determination; and if that determination is the cause of the necessity, then it would follow that the necessity of existence is an effect of something else. So we say: This is necessary upon you with the affirmation of the oneness of the Necessary Existent. That is because necessity is an accidental attribute qualifying the essence with existence, and because existence, possibility, and impossibility are modes, and they pertain to the connection between subject and predicate. And if the matter is so, then necessity in all cases is an attribute external to the essence, and every attribute external to the essence is possible in itself and caused by another. So it is established that this difficulty is necessary, whether we say that the Necessary Existent in itself is one or say that it is more than one. The third: We know by necessity that among essences, some entail by themselves another essence, such as our saying: three entails oddness, and four entails evenness. And the fact that three entails oddness is a thing necessary in itself, not for another, and the fact that four entails evenness is a thing necessary in itself. So these essential necessities are many things. So the Necessary in itself is more than one. If they say: These essential necessities are not essences subsisting by themselves; rather they are relative, consideration-based states. And both of us agree that essences subsisting by themselves that are necessary of existence in themselves must be one. So we say in the answer: You built the division which you mentioned in the oneness of the Necessary Existent in itself upon the concept of necessity by essence. And necessity by essence exists in these considerations, because they in themselves are things necessary in themselves, impossible of determination as they are. So this is necessity by essence, and this is a refutation of what you mentioned.

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection from the opponent against the proof for the oneness of the Necessary Existent (tawhid) and the author's refutation through three arguments. The opponent begins by conceding that both necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are fixed conceptual realities. The author responds by stating that this difficulty (mahdhur) is indeed necessary even with the affirmation of oneness, and they explain this through several reasons. The first reason: The First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is not bare necessity of existence, which is a universal essence. A universal essence cannot exist in external entities; rather, what exists externally is a determined essence described as necessary. This determination (ta'ayyun) is additional to the essence of necessity, and it must be an attribute (sifa). Attributes depend on their subject, and anything dependent on another is possible (mumkin) in itself. Thus, the determination is possible and is an effect of necessity, making the determined entity composite from a necessary and a possible part, leading to the conclusion that everything in external existence is possible—thus the problem the opponent raised against multiplicity equally applies to the one. The second reason: The opponent's argument assumes that if there were more than one Necessary, each would be composed of shared necessity and distinguishing determination. These multiple essential necessities show that there are things necessary in themselves beyond just one. The response is that the opponent's own division in proving oneness is based on essential necessity (wujub bi al-dhat), and these considerations do possess essential necessity, thereby refuting the claim of oneness.

Third Argument: Self-Necessary Implications
Razi presents the third argument that many self-necessary implications exist, so the Necessary Being is not one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third argument for the multiplicity of the Necessary Being based on self-necessary entailments among essences, and how he defends it against the objection that such entailments are merely relational states.

Core Idea
Even relational attributes can be necessary by themselves, challenging the claim that only one self-subsistent being is necessary.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's third argument for the multiplicity of the Necessary Being. He argues that certain essences necessarily entail other attributes (e.g., three entails oddness), and these entailments are necessary by themselves. Since there are many such entailments, there are many things necessary by themselves, contradicting the claim that the Necessary Being is one. We'll also see how Razi responds to a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Get an overall sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the examples and the logical connectors.
2
Identify the core premise
Notice Razi's starting point: we know by necessity that some essences entail another essence. Understand the examples (three entails oddness, four entails evenness) and why he claims these entailments are necessary by themselves.
3
Consider the objection
The opponents argue that such entailments are not real necessities but merely relative, relational states. Understand their reasoning: only self-subsistent entities can be necessary.
This objection strikes at the heart of Razi's argument.
4
Follow Razi's reply
Razi counters by pointing out that the opponents' own criterion for necessity is the concept of 'necessity by itself', which applies to these relational states because they are in themselves necessary and cannot be otherwise.
This is a powerful rebuttal that turns the opponents' framework against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن من الماهيات ما يكون مستلزما لذاته ماهية أخرى ، مثل قولنا : إن الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية ، وإن الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية وكون الثلاثة مستلزمة للفردية أمر واجب لذاته لا لغيره، وكون الأربعة مستلزمة للزوجية أمر واجب لذاته فهذه الوجوبات الذاتية أشياء كثيرة . فالواجب لذاته أكثر من الواحد . فإن قالوا : هذه الوجوبات الذاتية ليست ذوات قائمة بأنفسها ، وإنما هي أحوال نسبية اعتبارية ، وكلا منا في أن الذوات القائمة بنفسها ، الواجبة الوجود لذاتها، يجب أن تكون واحدة. فنقول في الجواب : أنتم بنيتم التقسيم الذي ذكر ثموه في توحيد واجب الوجود لذاته على مفهوم الوجوب بالذات . والوجوب بالذات حاصل في هذه الاعتبارات ، لأنها من حيث هي هي ، أمور واجبة لذواتها ، ممتنعة التعيين لما هي هي، فكان هذا وجوبا بالذات ، فكان هذا نقضا على ما ذكرتموه .
The third: We know by necessity that among essences there are some that necessarily entail another essence, like our statement: three entails oddness, and four entails evenness. The fact that three entails oddness is necessary by itself, not by another, and the fact that four entails evenness is necessary by itself. These self-necessities are many things. So the Necessary by itself is more than one. If they say: these self-necessities are not self-subsistent entities; rather they are relative, relational states. And both of us agree that self-subsistent entities that are necessary in existence by themselves must be one. We say in reply: You based the division you mentioned in the oneness of the Necessary Being by itself on the concept of necessity by itself. And necessity by itself is realized in these considerations, because in themselves they are things necessary by themselves, impossible to specify as they are. So this is necessity by itself, and this invalidates what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the multiplicity of the Necessary Being. The argument begins with an empirical premise: we know necessarily that some essences (ماهيات) necessarily entail another essence. For example, the number three necessarily entails oddness, and four necessarily entails evenness. This entailment (استلزام) is a relation that is necessary by itself, not by another. Since such relations are many, there are many things necessary by themselves, so the Necessary Being by itself is more than one. An objection is then raised: these self-necessities are not self-subsistent entities; they are merely relative, relational states (أحوال نسبية اعتبارية). Both parties agree that self-subsistent entities that are necessary by themselves must be one. Razi replies by pointing out that the opponents' own division in proving the oneness of the Necessary Being is based on the concept of necessity by itself (الوجوب بالذات). This concept is present in these relational considerations, because in themselves they are necessary by themselves and impossible to be otherwise. Hence they possess necessity by itself, and this undermines the opponents' claim that only a single self-subsistent entity can be necessary.

Key Terms

استلزام
ماهية
وجوب بالذات
أحوال نسبية اعتبارية
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only independent substances can be necessary by themselves; relational attributes cannot be necessary.
Correction: Razi argues that relational attributes like entailment can be necessary by themselves because they are in themselves necessary and immutable, even if they are not self-subsistent substances.

Checkpoint
What examples does Razi use to demonstrate self-necessary entailments?
A) Three entails oddness, four entails evenness
B) Humanity entails rationality
C) Fire entails burning
D) Heat entails expansion

Key Takeaway
Razi's third argument shows that many self-necessary entailments exist, implying the Necessary Being is not one, and he defends this by arguing that necessity by itself applies to relational states as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the statement 'three entails oddness,' does it feel necessary in the same way that you believe God's existence is necessary? How does this similarity or difference challenge your understanding of tawhid? What does this force you to re-examine in your own faith?
?
If Razi is right that many things are necessary by themselves, does that weaken the unique necessity of God in your heart? How would you personally respond to someone who uses this argument to question God's oneness? Are you prepared to defend your conviction, or does this argument unsettle you?
?
In your daily life, do you ever treat created things (like your job, health, or relationships) as if they are absolutely necessary, forgetting that only God is truly necessary by Himself? How can this lesson remind you to realign your priorities and rely solely on God?

The Composition of 'Necessary per se'
Razi refutes the premise that every composite is possible, using the composition of 'necessary per se'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the concept of 'necessary by itself' is composite, and how this refutes the premise that every composite is possible.

Core Idea
Even 'necessary by itself' is composite in meaning, so the premise that all composites are possible leads to a contradiction.

This passage presents Razi's fourth argument against the claim that every composite essence cannot be necessary. He targets the very concept of necessity, showing that 'necessary by itself' is a compound of genus and differentia. By analyzing its composition, he exposes a fatal contradiction in the opponent's premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice that Razi begins by stating the opponent's premise.
2
Identify Razi's target premise
The opponent claims that every composite in essence cannot be necessary by itself. Razi will refute this.
3
Understand the composition of necessity
Razi divides necessity into two kinds: by itself and by another. He then argues that 'necessity by itself' is composed of a genus (necessity) and a differentia (being by itself).
This is the key move: the concept itself is composite.
4
Follow the contradiction
If every composite is possible, then 'necessary by itself' would be possible, which is a contradiction. This shows the premise is false.
Razi uses the term 'خلف' (contradiction) to indicate the absurdity.
5
Conclude the argument
Razi concludes that the opponent's premise is invalid. Either the composition of 'necessary by itself' does not entail possibility (so premise fails), or it does entail possibility (leading to a contradiction).
This completes his refutation of the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كلامكم مبنى على أن كل مركب في ماهية ، فإنه يمتنع يكون واجبا لذاته . فنقول : إن هذا باطل لأن الوجوب على قسمين ، منه ما يكون وجوبا بالذات ، ومنه ما يكون وجوبا بالغير، فالوجوب هو القدر المشترك وكونه بالذات هو الذي به يحصل الامتياز ، فالوجوب بالذات ، مركب من الجنس وهو مسمى الوجوب ، ومن المفصل وهو خصوص كونه بالذات ، فيثبت : أن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة من قيدين فإن كان كل مركب ممكناً، لزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات ممكناً بالذات هذا خلف فيكون أحد المعاندين عين الثاني ، وذلك باطل ، وإن لم يلزم فيها يكون متركبا لماهية ، كونه ممكنا . فحينئذ تبطل هذه المقدمة . وهذا تمام الكلام عن الحجة الأولى.
The fourth: Your statement is based on the premise that every composite in essence cannot be necessary by itself. We say: This is false, because necessity is of two kinds: some is necessity by itself, and some is necessity by another. So necessity is the common factor, and its being by itself is that by which distinction occurs. So necessity by itself is composed of the genus, which is named 'necessity', and the differentia, which is the particularity of its being by itself. Thus it is established that necessity by itself is an essence composed of two restrictions. If every composite were possible, then it would follow that necessity by itself would be possible by itself—this is a contradiction, so one of the two contradictories would be the same as the other, and that is false. And if it does not follow that it is possible because it is composed in essence, then this premise is invalidated. This completes the discussion of the first argument.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage contains the fourth argument, which is a refutation of the premise that every composite in essence (مركب في ماهية) cannot be necessary by itself. Razi argues that this premise is false because necessity (الوجوب) is a concept that applies to two categories: necessity by itself (بالذات) and necessity by another (بالغير). The common factor (القدر المشترك) is necessity, and the differentia (المفصل) is the particularity of being by itself. Therefore, 'necessary by itself' is a composite of genus and differentia: the genus is the meaning of necessity, and the differentia is the condition of being by itself. Now, if we accept the opponents' premise that every composite is possible, then 'necessary by itself' would be possible by itself. This leads to a contradiction (خلف), because it would make the necessary possible, merging two opposites. Thus the premise is false. If they object that the composition does not entail possibility, then their premise fails altogether.

Correction: Razi shows that 'necessary by itself' is composite in meaning, containing a generic element (necessity) and a specific element (being by itself).

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that the concept of necessary-by-itself is composite, so the premise that all composites are possible leads to a contradiction, thus it is false.

Necessity as Negative Concept: Refuting Genus/Species Classification
Razi argues that necessity is negative, so not a genus/species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of existence cannot be a genus or species because it is a negative concept, not a positive constituent of essence.

Core Idea
Necessity is a negation, not an essence—so it cannot classify.

This passage presents Razi's second argument against the possibility of multiple necessary beings. Opponents claimed that if there were multiple, necessity would have to be either a genus under which they fall as species or a species under which they fall as individuals. Razi refutes this by showing that necessity is a negative concept (مفهوم سلبي), not a positive attribute that enters into the essence. Therefore, the logical relations of genus and species do not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim followed by Razi's refutation.
The passage is concise; every sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent asserts that if more than one Necessary Being exists, necessity must function as a genus or species. Find this conditional statement.
The opponent is using a standard classification argument.
3
Focus on Razi's condition
Razi says the opponent's argument only works if necessity is a positive concept (ثبوتي) entering the essence. Identify the condition he sets.
This is the key move: the argument hinges on the nature of necessity.
4
Understand the negative concept
Razi states necessity is a negative concept (مفهوم سلبي). Why does this prevent it from being part of the essence? Consider what a genus or species requires.
A genus or species must add something positive to the essence.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since necessity is negative, it cannot be a genus or species. Therefore, the opponent's classification does not apply, and multiple necessary beings remain possible to imagine but not implied by classification.
Razi refers to earlier discussion for the full proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية وهي قولكم : لو كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد ، لكان واجب الوجود ، إما أن يكون جنسا تحته أنواع ، أو نوعا تحته أشخاص . قلنا هذا الكلام إنما يلزم لو ثبت أن وجوب الموجود مفهوم ثبوتي داخل في الماهية ، حتى يقال : إنه بالنسبة إلى الأشياء الداخلة فيه ، يجب أن يكون جنساً بالنسبة إلى الأنواع أو نوعا بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص ، وقد بينا أنه مفهوم سلبي فيمتنع أن يكون داخلا في الماهية ، فيكون كونه مقولا على ما تحته ، ليس على قياس قول الجنس على أنواعه ولا على قياس قول النوع على أشخاصه . وتمام التقرير ما تقدم. والله أعلم.
As for the second argument—your statement: If the Necessary Being were more than one, then the Necessary Being would either be a genus under which there are species, or a species under which there are individuals. We say: This statement only follows if it is established that the necessity of existence is a positive concept entering into the essence, so that it is said that with respect to the things falling under it, it must be a genus for species or a species for individuals. But we have shown that it is a negative concept, so it cannot enter into the essence. Therefore its being predicated of what falls under it is not analogous to the predication of a genus of its species nor to the predication of a species of its individuals. The full explanation has preceded. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument, which is a response to the opponents' claim that if there were more than one Necessary Being, they would have to be either a genus with species or a species with individuals. Razi refutes this by stating that the necessity of existence (وجوب الموجود) is a negative concept (مفهوم سلبي), not a positive one that enters into the essence. Since it does not constitute the essence, it cannot serve as a genus or species. A genus and species are positive, essential properties that classify entities. Because necessity is negative (it denies the possibility of non-existence), it is not part of the essence, and thus the logical relation of genus/species does not apply. Therefore, the opponent's objection is invalid. Razi refers to an earlier explanation for the full treatment.

Refuting the Composition of the Necessary by Essence
The author refutes the claim that the necessary by essence is composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the claim that the Necessary by Essence is composite, and why its conceptual structure leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If the Necessary by Essence were composite, it would be contingent—a direct contradiction.

The author tackles an argument that the Necessary by Essence is composite by analyzing its logical structure. By breaking down the term into its conceptual parts (genus and differentia), he reveals a hidden flaw. This lesson walks through that refutation step by step, showing how the premise leads to an impossible conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then the translation. Notice the argument's structure: definition, composition, conditional, and conclusion.
Focus on the logical terms: 'genus', 'differentia', 'composite', 'possible', 'contradiction'.
2
Identify the conceptual parts
The author states that 'necessary by essence' is composed of a genus (necessity) and a differentia (by essence). Understand that this is a logical composition, not a physical one.
This composition is inherent to the definition, suggesting the concept is not simple.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The premise: if every composite is possible (contingent), then the Necessary by Essence would be possible, contradicting its necessity. The author also considers the alternative branch to fully invalidate the premise.
The contradiction shows the original claim that the Necessary is composite must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكونه بالذات هو الذي به يحصل الامتياز، فالوجوب بالذات مركب من الجنس وهو مسمى الوجوب، ومن المفصل وهو خصوص كونه بالذات، فيثبت أن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة من قيدين فإن كان كل مركب ممكناً، لزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات ممكناً بالذات هذا خلف فيكون أحد المعاندين عين الثاني، وذلك باطل، وإن لم يلزم فيها يكون متركبا لماهية، كونه ممكنا. فحينئذ تبطل هذه المقدمة. وهذا تمام الكلام عن الحجة الأولى
And its being by essence is that by which distinction is obtained. So the necessary by essence is composed of a genus, which is the name 'necessity', and a differentia, which is the particularity of its being by essence. Thus it is established that the necessary by essence is a quiddity composed of two constraints. If every composite is possible, then the necessary by essence would be possible by essence—this is a contradiction—so one of the two opposites would be identical to the other, and that is false. And if it does not follow, then it would be composed of a quiddity, its being possible. So then this premise is invalidated. And this is the completion of the speech concerning the first argument.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a premise in the first argument. The author argues that the concept of 'necessary by essence' is composed of a genus (necessity) and a differentia (by essence). If every composite is contingent (possible), then the necessary by essence would be contingent, which contradicts its necessity. Therefore the premise that the necessary by essence is composite is false. The author concludes the first argument here.

The Negative Nature of Necessary Existence
The author refutes the genus/species argument by asserting necessity is negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of existence is a negative concept and how that refutes the genus/species argument for multiple necessary existents.

Core Idea
Necessity of existence is a negation of dependency, not a positive attribute—so it cannot be a genus or species.

The opponent argues that multiple necessary existents would share a common attribute, making 'necessary existent' a genus or species. The author responds by examining the nature of this attribute, showing it is not positive but negative—thus breaking the logical analogy. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent claims that multiple necessary existents would lead to a genus-species relationship. Note the assumed logic: a shared attribute implies a genus or species.
The opponent uses standard Aristotelian logic.
2
Grasp the positive vs. negative distinction
The author counters that the genus-species analogy only holds if the shared attribute is a positive concept included in the essence. But necessity of existence is negative—it simply negates dependency. Understand this key difference.
A positive concept adds a real attribute; a negative concept denies something.
3
See how this refutes the argument
Because necessity is negative, it cannot be part of the quiddity. Therefore, its predication is not like that of a genus over species. The opponent's argument collapses. Reflect on why this matters for tawhid.
This shows that the oneness of God is rationally defensible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية وهي قولكم: لو كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، لكان واجب الوجود إما أن يكون جنسا تحته أنواع، أو نوعا تحته أشخاص. قلنا هذا الكلام إنما يلزم لو ثبت أن وجوب الموجود مفهوم ثبوتي داخل في الماهية، حتى يقال: إنه بالنسبة إلى الأشياء الداخلة فيه، يجب أن يكون جنساً بالنسبة إلى الأنواع أو نوعا بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص، وقد بينا أنه مفهوم سلبي فيمتنع أن يكون داخلا في الماهية، فيكون كونه مقولا على ما تحته، ليس على قياس قول الجنس على أنواعه ولا على قياس قول النوع على أشخاصه. وتمام التقرير ما تقدم. والله أعلم
As for the second argument, which is your statement: 'If the Necessary Existent were more than one, then the Necessary Existent would either be a genus under which there are species, or a species under which there are individuals.' We say: This statement only follows if it is established that the necessity of existence is a positive concept included in the quiddity, such that it is said to be, in relation to the things included under it, a genus relative to species or a species relative to individuals. And we have shown that it is a negative concept, so it is impossible for it to be included in the quiddity. Thus its being predicated of what is under it is not analogous to the predication of a genus over its species nor to the predication of a species over its individuals. The full explanation is what has preceded. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument that if there were multiple necessary existents, they would have to be either a genus with species or a species with individuals. The author responds that this only applies if 'necessity of existence' is a positive attribute included in the essence (quiddity). But it has already been established that it is a negative concept (the negation of dependency), so it cannot be part of the quiddity. Therefore, its predication is not like genus or species. The argument thus fails.

The Argument from Mutual Hindrance (Daleel al-Tamānu‘)
The author presents the strongest proof for God's oneness: the argument from mutual hindrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that proves God's oneness by demonstrating the impossibility of two gods willing opposite actions.

Core Idea
Two gods willing opposites leads to logical impossibility.

Among the strongest proofs for tawḥīd presented by the theologians is the argument from mutual hindrance. It shows that if two gods with absolute power will opposite things for the same creation, all possible outcomes lead to contradictions. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the argument assumes two gods, each with power over all possible things, and they will opposite actions for the same body. The author lists three possibilities: both wills are fulfilled, only one is fulfilled, or neither is fulfilled.
The three possibilities are exhaustive.
2
Understand why both outcomes cannot happen together
The author argues that if both wills are fulfilled, the same body would be both moving and still simultaneously, which is absurd. This is the first contradiction.
3
Understand why only one outcome cannot happen
If only one will is fulfilled, the other god would be powerless, contradicting the assumption of absolute power. So this possibility is also impossible.
This second possibility is refuted implicitly; the author focuses more on the third.
4
Understand why neither outcome cannot happen
The author provides the most detailed reasoning for this case: the prevention of each intended outcome is caused by the occurrence of the other, leading to a circular causality where prevention necessitates occurrence, which is a contradiction.
This is the key part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في حكاية دلائل المتكلمين على أن الإله واحد. اعلم: أنهم ذكروا أنواعا من الدلائل. أقواها دليل التمانع فقالوا: لو قدرنا إلهين قادرين على جميع الممكنات، ثم أراد أحدهما تحريك جسم، وأراد الآخر تسكينه، فإما أن يحصل المراد معاً، أو يحصل أحدهما دون الآخر. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فكان القول بوجود الإلهين باطلا. أما الحصر فظاهر، وأما أنه يمتنع حصول المرادين معاً، فلأنه يلزم منه كون الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة: متحركا وساكنا معا. وهو محال. وأما أنه يمتنع حصول المرادين معا، فالدليل عليه: أن المانع من حصول مراد هذا، ليس مجرد كون الثاني قادراً، وإنما المانع من حصول مراد الأول هو حصول مراد الثاني، والحكم لا يحصل إلا عند حصول العلة، فأمتناع مراد هذا، معلل بحصول مراد ذاك، وامتناع حصول مراد ذاك معلل بحصول مراد هذا، فلو امتنع المرادان معا، لزم حصول المرادين معا، حتى يكون وجود كل واحد منهما مانعا من حصول الآخر، فيثبت: أن امتناع كل واحد منهما، يوجب حصول كل واحد منهما، وما أفضى نفيه إلى ثبوته كان باطلا فالقول بأمتناع المرادين معا، وجب أن يكون باطلا. واعلم أنه لو ثبت أن الأعراض غير باقية، حصل لنا
The Second Chapter: Narrating the arguments of the theologians that God is One. Know that they mentioned types of proofs. The strongest of them is the proof of mutual hindrance. They said: If we suppose two gods, both powerful over all possible things, then one of them wills the movement of a body and the other wills its stillness, then either both intended outcomes occur together, or one of them occurs without the other, or neither occurs. And the three divisions are false, so the statement of the existence of two gods is false. As for the division, it is apparent. As for the impossibility of both intended outcomes occurring together, it is because it necessitates that one body be at one and the same time moving and still simultaneously, which is absurd. As for the impossibility of both intended outcomes not occurring together, the proof for it is that the preventer of the occurrence of the intended outcome of this one is not merely that the second is powerful; rather, the preventer of the occurrence of the intended outcome of the first is the occurrence of the intended outcome of the second. And the judgment does not occur except when the cause occurs. So the impossibility of this one's intended outcome is caused by the occurrence of that one's intended outcome, and the impossibility of the occurrence of that one's intended outcome is caused by the occurrence of this one's intended outcome. So if both intended outcomes were prevented together, then the occurrence of both intended outcomes would be necessitated, such that the existence of each one of them would be a preventer of the occurrence of the other. Thus it is established that the prevention of each one of them necessitates the occurrence of each one of them. And whatever leads its negation to its affirmation is false. So the statement that both intended outcomes are prevented together must be false. And know that if it is established that accidents are not permanent, we would have obtained...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the famous 'proof of mutual hindrance' (daleel al-tamānu‘) for God's oneness. It begins with a chapter heading and states that the strongest proof is this one. The argument assumes two gods, each with absolute power over all possible things. They will opposite actions for the same body (movement vs. stillness). There are three possibilities: both wills are fulfilled, only one is fulfilled, or neither is fulfilled. The author shows all three are impossible: (1) Both cannot be fulfilled because that would require a body to be both moving and still at once. (2) Only one being fulfilled would mean the other god is unable to realize its will, contradicting its claim to absolute power. (3) Neither being fulfilled leads to a circular causality: the non-fulfillment of one depends on the fulfillment of the other, and vice versa, leading to a contradiction where prevention necessitates occurrence. Thus the assumption of two gods is impossible. The passage ends with a conditional note about the non-permanence of accidents, which seems incomplete in the available text.

Key Terms
دليل التمانع
الإلهين
المراد
المانع
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if the two gods will opposite actions.
Correction: The argument actually considers all three possibilities (both, one, neither) and shows each leads to contradiction, so it covers any scenario of two gods willing anything, even if they will the same thing (but the example uses opposites).

Checkpoint
In the argument from mutual hindrance, what is the problem if neither god's will is fulfilled?
A) Both gods would be weak.
B) It leads to a circular causality where prevention necessitates occurrence.
C) The body would remain still.
D) The argument only considers two possibilities.

Key Takeaway
The argument from mutual hindrance proves that only one God can exist, as any assumption of multiple gods leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the argument proves that only one God can truly act without contradiction, then where in my life have I been acting as if there are multiple 'powers' that control my fate? Do I secretly fear that human approval, financial security, or health can oppose Allah's will? How can I internalize this logical truth so that my heart trusts in Allah alone, even when circumstances seem to contradict His promise?
?
The argument shows that if two gods willed opposite things, neither could act—they would be paralyzed. In my own life, when I try to serve both Allah and my desires, do I not experience a similar spiritual paralysis? Which conflicting 'gods'—status, comfort, relationships—am I unwilling to surrender, and how does this contradiction manifest in my actions and emptiness?
?
The author's proof leaves no room for compromise: either there is one God or logical impossibility. When I examine my own heart, do I implicitly entertain the possibility that there are other sources of ultimate power or benefit alongside Allah? What specific attachments or fears would I have to abandon to live with the certainty that He alone is the Ilāh?

The Proof of Mutual Prevention: Initial Reasoning and First Two Refutations
Sanusi presents the proof structure, enumerates three possibilities, and refutes first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the proof of mutual prevention and why both wills being fulfilled together or both being prevented are impossible.

Core Idea
Two gods with conflicting wills lead to logical contradictions, proving each cannot be divine.

This lesson introduces the famous kalam proof of mutual prevention (dalil al-tamanu') for the oneness of God. The theologians present a scenario of two gods with opposite wills and examine all possible outcomes, showing each leads to an impossibility. By working through these possibilities, you will see how reason alone can establish God's unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the theologians enumerate three possible outcomes when two gods will opposite actions on a single body.
The three possibilities are: both wills fulfilled, neither fulfilled, or only one fulfilled.
2
Understand the first refutation: both wills together
The first possibility is impossible because it would make the body simultaneously moving and still, a direct contradiction. Reflect on why this is inherently absurd.
3
Follow the second refutation: the circularity argument
The second possibility (neither will is obtained) is refuted by showing that the prevention of one will causes the other will to occur, leading to a self-defeating cycle. Trace the logic: if both wills are prevented, each prevention requires the other's will to have occurred, so both wills would have to occur.
This is the key logical move that shows the impossibility of neither will being obtained.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the first two possibilities are impossible, and the third will be refuted later, the existence of two gods is false. Note that this proof relies on pure reason, not revelation.
The third possibility is addressed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في حكاية دلائل المتكلمين على أن الإله واحد اعلم: أنهم ذكروا أنواعا من الدلائل. أقواها دليل التمانع فقالوا: لو قدرنا إلهين قادرين على جميع الممكنات، ثم أراد أحدهما تحريك جسم، وأراد الآخر تسكينه، فإما أن يحصل المراد معاً، أو يحصل أحدهما دون الآخر، أو لا يحصل شيء منهما. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فكان القول بوجود الإلهين باطلا. أما الحصر فظاهر، وأما أنه يمتنع حصول المرادين معاً، فلأنه يلزم منه كون الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة: متحركا وساكنا معا. وهو محال. وأما أنه يمتنع حصول المرادين معا، فالدليل عليه: أن المانع من حصول مراد هذا، ليس مجرد كون الثاني قادراً، وإنما المانع من حصول مراد الأول هو حصول مراد الثاني، والحكم لا يحصل إلا عند حصول العلة، فأمتناع مراد هذا، معلل بحصول مراد ذاك، وامتناع حصول مراد ذاك معلل بحصول مراد هذا، فلو امتنع المرادان معا، لزم حصول المرادين معا، حتى يكون وجود كل واحد منهما مانعا من حصول الآخر، فيثبت: أن امتناع كل واحد منهما، يوجب حصول كل واحد منهما، وما أفضى نفيه إلى ثبوته كان باطلا فالقول بأمتناع المرادين معا، وجب أن يكون باطلا. واعلم أنه لو ثبت أن الأعراض غير باقية، حصل لنا في إبطال هذا القسم دليل آخر. وهو: أنه لو تعذر على كل واحد منهما تحصيل مراده، لزم أن يبقى الجسم خاليا عن الحركة والسكون، وذلك محال. أما إذا قلنا: الأعراض باقية، لم يلزم منه محال البتة، لأنه لما اندفع كل واحد منهما بالآخر، بقي الجسم على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك، فيبقى العرض الذي كان موجودا قبل ذلك. وأما أنه يمتنع حصول أحد المرادين دون الآخر فيدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه لما كان كل واحد منهما قادرا على ما لا نهاية له من الممكنات، امتنع كون أحدهما أقوى قدرة من الثاني، ولما كانا متساويين في كمال الاقتدار، فلو رقع مراد أحدهما دون الثاني، كان هذا قولا برجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح، وأنه باطل. والثاني: إنه إذا حصل مراد أحدهما دون الثاني، فالذي لم يحصل مراده، كان عاجزا، والعاجز لا يكون إلها. فهذا تقرير هذا الدليل.
The second chapter on the narration of the proofs of the theologians that God is one. Know that they mentioned types of proofs. The strongest of them is the proof of mutual prevention (dalil al-tamanu'). They said: If we suppose two gods capable of all possible things, then one of them wills to move a body and the other wills to stop it, then either the object of will is obtained together, or one of them is obtained without the other, or nothing is obtained from them. And the three divisions are false, so the statement of the existence of two gods is false. As for the enumeration, it is apparent. As for the impossibility of the two objects of will being obtained together, it is because it necessitates the single body being moving and still at one moment together, which is impossible. As for the impossibility of the two objects of will not being obtained together, the proof for it is that the preventer of obtaining the object of will of this one is not merely that the second is capable, but rather the preventer of obtaining the object of will of the first is the obtaining of the object of will of the second, and the judgment is not obtained except when the cause is obtained. So the prevention of the object of will of this one is caused by the obtaining of the object of will of that one, and the prevention of obtaining the object of will of that one is caused by the obtaining of the object of will of this one. So if the two objects of will are prevented together, the obtaining of the two objects of will together would be necessary, so that the existence of each one of them would be a preventer of obtaining the other. Thus it is established that the prevention of each one of them necessitates the obtaining of each one of them, and whatever leads its negation to its affirmation is false. So the statement of the prevention of the two objects of will together must be false. And know that if it were established that accidents are not permanent, we would have another proof for the invalidation of this division: namely, that if it were impossible for each one of them to achieve his object of will, the body would have to remain devoid of motion and stillness, and that is impossible. But if we say that accidents are permanent, no impossibility follows at all, because when each one is repelled by the other, the body remains as it was before, so the accident that existed before remains. As for the impossibility of obtaining one of the two objects of will without the other, two aspects indicate it: The first: Since each one of them is capable of infinite possible things, it is impossible for one of them to be stronger in power than the second; and since they are equal in perfection of capability, if the object of will of one of them occurred without the second, this would be a statement of the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator, and that is false. The second: If the object of will of one of them occurs without the second, then the one whose object of will did not occur is incapable, and the incapable cannot be a god. This is the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the famous kalam proof of mutual prevention (dalil al-tamanu') for the oneness of God (tawhid). The author states that the theologians have mentioned various proofs, the strongest of which is this proof. They posit two gods capable of all possible things, then suppose they will opposite actions for a body: one wills motion, the other stillness. This leads to three logical possibilities: (1) both wills are fulfilled together, (2) neither is fulfilled, (3) only one is fulfilled. Each possibility is shown to be impossible, proving that two gods cannot exist. The author then explains each impossibility in detail. For (1), it would make the body simultaneously moving and still, a contradiction. For (2), he gives a subtle argument: the prevention of one will is caused by the other will occurring. If both are prevented, that would require both wills to occur to cause prevention, leading to a self-contradictory circularity. He also adds a note that if accidents are not permanent, then the body would be left without motion or stillness, which is also impossible; but if accidents are permanent, the body remains as before, so no impossibility arises from that angle. For (3), he gives two reasons: first, since both gods are equally omnipotent, there is no basis for one will to prevail over the other without a preponderating factor; second, the god whose will fails would be powerless, and a powerless being cannot be divine. Thus, all three possibilities are impossible, so the existence of two gods is false.

Key Terms
دليل التمانع
إلهين
قادرين
المرادين
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that two gods will always have conflicting wills.
Correction: The proof is a hypothetical; it does not claim actual conflict. It shows that even if conflict could happen, the coexistence of two gods leads to logical contradictions, so it is impossible.

Checkpoint
What are the three logical possibilities when two gods have conflicting wills according to dalil al-tamanu'?
A) Both wills are fulfilled, neither is fulfilled, or only one is fulfilled.
B) Both wills are fulfilled, both are prevented, or the body remains unchanged.
C) Both wills are fulfilled, both are prevented, or the gods cooperate.
D) The gods compromise, or they fight.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual prevention shows that two gods cannot coexist because their hypothetical conflicting wills lead to logical impossibilities for two out of three outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you are convinced that this proof refutes the possibility of two gods, what does that demand from you in terms of your exclusive devotion to the One God? Where in your life are you tempted to serve other 'gods'—money, status, desires—that contradict this truth?
?
This proof hinges on logical consistency. In your own beliefs about God, are you holding any contradictions that you have not resolved? What would it take for you to submit fully to the logical implications of tawhid?
?
If this proof leaves no room for a second deity, then your entire existence must be oriented toward Allah alone. What specific changes in your daily priorities or habits will you make today to reflect that conviction?

The Proof of Mutual Prevention: Accident Argument and the Third Refutation
Sanusi provides an alternative argument using accidents and refutes the third possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional argument regarding the permanence of accidents and why only one will being fulfilled is impossible.

Core Idea
If one god's will fails, he is not omnipotent, so he cannot be God.

This lesson continues the proof of mutual prevention by examining the scenario where neither will is obtained, this time considering the nature of accidents. It then refutes the third possibility where only one will is fulfilled, providing two compelling reasons why this cannot happen. Together with the previous lesson, all three logical possibilities are shown to be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the accident argument
Read the note about accidents: if accidents are not permanent, then if neither will is obtained, the body would be without motion or stillness, which is impossible. But if accidents are permanent, the body remains as before, so no problem arises. Note that this is an additional argument contingent on a specific view of accidents.
This argument is not essential to the main proof; it strengthens it for those who hold accidents are non-permanent.
2
Examine the first aspect for the third possibility
The third possibility (only one will fulfilled) is impossible first because both gods are equally omnipotent. If one will prevailed without a preponderating factor, it would violate the principle that equal powers cannot produce unequal outcomes.
This is a philosophical principle about the equality of agents.
3
Examine the second aspect for the third possibility
Second, if one god's will failed to be fulfilled, that god would be incapable (عاجز) of achieving his will. But incapability contradicts divinity, as God must be omnipotent. Hence the god whose will fails cannot be divine.
This directly ties to the definition of God as all-powerful.
4
Conclude the proof
Since all three possibilities are impossible, the assumption of two gods is false. Thus, God must be one. This completes the proof of mutual prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
