Objection and Response to the Proof of Mutual Prevention
Objection and beginning of response to dalil al-tamanu'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof of mutual prevention assumes a possible conflict of wills and how the response defends that premise.

Core Idea
Two equal gods can will opposites; the proof's premise is defensible.

Sanusi's argument for tawhid using dalil al-tamanu' relies on the possibility that two gods could will opposite things. An objector challenges this very premise, asking for proof that such a conflict is possible. In this passage, we see the beginning of Sanusi's response, which argues that if each god were alone, each could will its respective action, and when together, they either retain that ability—which suffices for the proof—or lose it, leading to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's challenge: they question the foundation of the proof. Note what exactly they are asking for evidence of.
2
Follow the first part of the response
Sanusi begins his answer with a conditional scenario: what each god could do alone vs. together. Understand the two branches: either they retain their ability or not.
3
Examine the first reason against the second branch
The text gives the first reason why losing the ability is invalid: since both are equal in power, there is no reason for one to be prevented while the other is not. This leads to a contradiction.
The passage cuts off here; the full answer continues on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا الدليل بناء على جواز أن يريد أحدهما شيئا، ويريد الثاني ضده. فلم قلتم: إن هذا ممكن؟ وما الدليل عليه؟ لا يقال: الدليل عليه: أنه لو انفرد هذا بالإلهية صح منه إرادة الحركة، ولو انفرد الثاني صح منه إرادة السكون، فإذا اجتمعا، فإما أن يبقيا على ما كانا عليه حال الانفراد، أولا يبقيا على تلك الحالة. فإن بقيا على تلك الحالة فحينئذ يصح من هذا إرادة الحركة، ومن الثاني إرادة السكون حال الاجتماع، وهذا هو المطلوب. وإن لم يكن بقيا حال الاجتماع على ما كانا عليه حال الانفراد، فهذا باطل لوجوه: الأول: إنهما لما كانا متساويين في كمال القدرة والإلهية، لم يكن امتناع هذا عن الإرادة بسبب ذلك، أولى من امتناع ذاك عن الإرادة بسبب هذا فوجب أن يندفع كل واحد منهما بالآخر وذلك محال. لأن الموجب لاندفاع الجانب الأول حصول الجانب الثاني، والموجب لاندفاع الثاني حصول الجانب الأول، فلو اندفع الجانبان معا، لزم الجانبين معا على ما ذكرناه في أول
If it is said: This proof is based on the permissibility that one of them wills something and the second wills its opposite. So why do you say that this is possible? And what is the proof for it? It is said: The proof for it is that if this one were alone in divinity, the willing of motion would be valid from him, and if the second were alone, the willing of stillness would be valid from him. When they come together, then either they remain as they were in the state of solitude, or they do not remain in that state. If they remain in that state, then at that time the willing of motion from this one and the willing of stillness from the second are valid in the state of togetherness, and this is what is required. And if they do not remain in the state of togetherness as they were in the state of solitude, then this is invalid for several reasons. The first: Since they are equal in perfection of power and divinity, the prevention of this one from willing due to that is not more appropriate than the prevention of that one from willing due to this, so it is necessary that each one of them be repelled by the other, and that is impossible. Because the cause for the repulsion of the first side is the obtaining of the second side, and the cause for the repulsion of the second side is the obtaining of the first side. So if both sides were repelled together, both sides would be necessary together, as we mentioned in the beginning...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage records an objection to the proof of mutual prevention and the beginning of its answer. The objector challenges the premise that two gods can will opposite things simultaneously, asking for evidence that this is possible. The author then provides the response: If each god were alone, each could will its opposite action (motion or stillness). When they come together, either they retain this ability or they lose it. If they retain it, then it is possible for them to will opposites, which is sufficient for the proof. If they do not retain it, then that is invalid for several reasons. The first reason given is that since both gods are equal in perfect power and divinity, there is no reason for one to be prevented from willing while the other is not; thus both would be repelled, leading to a contradiction, as explained earlier in the proof. The answer breaks off at the bottom of the page, indicating continuation.

Correction: The response shows that the assumption is defensible: if each god were alone, each could will its own action, and when together, they either retain that ability (making the proof valid) or lose it (leading to a contradiction).

Proof Against Two Gods with Conflicting Wills
Presents a proof against two gods based on equality of power and impossibility of one prevailing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that two equally powerful gods cannot have conflicting wills without leading to logical impossibility.

Core Idea
Two gods with equal power cannot disagree without one being weak.

This lesson presents a sophisticated theological argument that uses logical reasoning to demonstrate the impossibility of two gods with opposing wills. The argument builds on the principle of equal power and the need for a preponderator, showing that any conflict would either leave the universe in an impossible state or imply that one god is incapable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct parts: the initial conditional and the two proofs.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage begins with a conditional: if both gods fail to achieve their desires, the body would be devoid of motion and rest, which is impossible. Then it addresses a response if accidents are permanent. Then it gives two proofs that one desire cannot prevail over the other.
The author uses 'أما' to transition to the proof.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof uses the equality of power. Since both gods are equally capable of infinite possibilities, one cannot be stronger. If one's desire prevailed, it would require a preponderator without cause, which is false.
The term 'المرجح' (preponderator) is key here.
4
Analyze the second proof
The second proof argues that if one god's desire occurs without the other's, the second god is incapable. An incapable being cannot be a god. This directly leads to the conclusion that two gods cannot coexist.
This proof is a direct reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for tawheed. It shows that any plurality in divinity leads to logical contradictions, affirming the necessity of one God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في إبطال هذا القسم دليل آخر . وهو : أنه لو تعذر على كل واحد منهما تحصيل مراده، لزم أن يبقى الجسم خاليا عن الحركة والسكون ، وذلك محال أما إذا قلنا : الأعراض باقية ، لم يلزم منه محال البتة ، لأنه لما اندفع كل واحد منهما بالآخر ، بقي الجسم على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك ، فيبقى العرض الذي كان موجودا قبل ذلك . وأما أنه يمتنع حصول أحد المرادين دون الآخر فيـدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنه لما كان كل واحد منهما قادرا على ما لا نهاية له من الممكنات ، امتنع كون أحدهما أقوى قدرة من الثاني ، ولما كانا متساويين في كمال الاقتدار ، فلو رقع مراد أحدهما دون الثاني، كان هذا قولا برجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وأنه باطل . والثاني : إنه إذا حصل مراد أحدهما دون الثاني ، فالذي لم يحصل مراده، كان عاجزا ، والعاجز لا يكون . فهذا تقرير هذا الدليل
Another proof for the invalidation of this section. It is: If it were impossible for each of them to attain his desire, then the body would necessarily remain devoid of motion and rest, and that is impossible. But if we say that accidents are permanent, no impossibility follows at all, because when each of them is repelled by the other, the body remains as it was before, so the accident that existed before remains. As for the impossibility of one of the two desires occurring without the other, two aspects indicate it: First, since each of them is capable of an infinite number of possibilities, it is impossible for one to be stronger in power than the other, and since they are equal in perfect capability, if the desire of one were to occur without the other, this would be a statement of the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator, and that is false. Second, if the desire of one occurs without the other, then the one whose desire did not occur would be incapable, and the incapable cannot be (a god). This is the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dālīl) against the possibility of two gods coexisting and disagreeing. The author begins by stating that if it were impossible for both gods to achieve their opposite wills (e.g., one wants motion, the other rest), then the body would have to be without both motion and rest, which is impossible (since a body must be in one state). However, if accidents are permanent (as the author holds), then no impossibility follows: the body remains in its prior state. Then the author proves that it is impossible for one god's will to prevail over the other's. He gives two reasons. First: since both gods are equally capable of infinite possibilities, neither can be stronger. If one's will prevailed, that would require a preponderator, but there is none, so it is false. Second: if one's will prevailed, the other would be unable to achieve his will, making him weak, and a weak being cannot be a god. Thus the proof concludes that two gods cannot exist with opposing wills.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only works if one believes accidents are not permanent (i.e., transient).
Correction: The author addresses this by showing that even if accidents are permanent, the proof still holds because the body remains in its prior state.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would happen if both gods failed to achieve their desires?
A) The body would be in two opposite states simultaneously.
B) The body would be free of both motion and rest, which is impossible.
C) The body would remain in its previous state.
D) One god would overcome the other.

Key Takeaway
Two gods with equal power cannot both have their wills fulfilled if they disagree, proving that absolute unity is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your daily life, you often choose between conflicting desires. This proof argues that two equal wills cannot both be fulfilled. Are you trying to serve two masters—your own whims and God's commands? Which desire will you sacrifice today to align with His will alone?
?
The proof uses the concept of 'preponderator'—a cause that tips the scale. In your decisions, do you act without a clear reason, or do you seek a proper motivation? Commit to examining your intentions and ensuring they are for God's sake, not just your own.

Defending Unity: Three Rebuttals Against the Objection of Two Wills
Objection and three-part rebuttal against two wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection to the argument for God's unity based on conflicting wills and the three rational rebuttals that defend the impossibility of two gods willing opposites.

Core Idea
Two equal gods cannot will opposites without contradiction, refuting the objection.

This passage tackles a crucial objection to the argument that two gods would lead to conflict. The objector claims that if each god alone can will something, then together they should be able to will opposites. The author provides three incisive rebuttals and then demolishes the underlying assumption that what holds in solitude holds in conjunction. This lesson examines each point in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the objector's claim and the suggested proof carefully. Understand why someone might think two gods could coexist with opposing wills.
The objector builds on the idea that what is possible in solitude should be possible together.
2
Analyze the Three Rebuttals
Study the first, second, and third reasons given by the author. Each one shows a fatal flaw in the objector's reasoning.
Look for the logical structure: equality of power, eternal nature of will, and absurd consequence of incapability.
3
Understand the Final Refutation
Examine the author's final argument that the principle 'what is valid in solitude remains valid in conjunction' is false. See why the objector's entire position collapses.
This refutation ties together the earlier points and decisively defeats the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الدليل بناء على جواز أن يريد أحدهما شيئا ، ويريد الثاني ضده . فلم قلتم : إن هذا ممكن ؟ وما الدليل عليه ؟ لا يقال : الدليل عليه : أنه لو انفرد هذا بالإلهية صح منه إرادة الحركة ، ولو انفرد الثاني صح منه إرادة السكون ، فإذا اجتمعا ، فإما أن يبقيا على ما كانا عليه حال الانفراد ، أولا يبقيا على تلك الحالة [ فإن بقيا على تلك الحالة ] فحينئذ يصح من هذا إرادة الحركة ، ومن الثاني إرادة السكون حال الاجتماع ، وهذا هو المطلوب وإن لم يكن بقيا حال الاجتماع على ما كانا عليه حال الانفراد ، فهذا باطل لوجوه : الأول : إنهما لما كانا متساويين في كمال القدرة والإلهية ، لم يكن امتناع هذا عن الإرادة بسبب ذلك ، أولى من امتناع ذاك عن الإرادة بسبب هذا فوجب أن يندفع كل واحد منهما بالآخر وذلك محال . لأن الموجب لاندفاع الجانب الأول حصول الجانب الثاني ، والموجب لاندفاع الثاني حصول الجانب الأول ، فلو اندفع الجانبان معا ، لزم الجانبين معا على ما ذكرناه في أول الدليل . وكل شيء يفضى ثبوته إلى نفيه ، أو نفيه إلى ثبوته كان باطلا ، فوجب أن يكون هذا الكلام باطلا الثاني : وهو أن تعلق إرادة كل واحد منهما بذلك الفعل المعين ، حكم ثبت من الأزل ، والأزل لا يجوز زواله ، فوجب أن لا تبطل تلك الإرادة الثالث : إنه لما أراد هذا شيئا ، وأراد الثاني ضده. ثم عجزا عن تحصيل مراديهما ، عند الاجتماع. كان هذا العاجز أقوى ، والضعيف أكمل فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا المقام . لأنا نقول : مدار هذا الكلام على أن كل حكم يصح حصوله عند الانفراد، وجب أن يبقى صحيحاً ممكنا عند الاجتماع. فنقول : وهذا باطل بدليل : أن هذا عند الانفراد قادر على تحصيل مراده ، وذلك عند الانفراد أيضاً قادر على تحصيل مراده ، وأما عند الاجتماع فلم يبقيا على هذه الحالة ، فيثبت أنه لا يلزم من حصول حكم حال الانفراد ، إمكان حصوله حال الاجتماع ، فظهر سقوط هذا الكلام
If it is said: This proof is based on the permissibility that one of them wills something and the second wills its opposite. So why do you say that this is possible? And what is the proof of it? It should not be said: The proof of it is that if this one were alone with divinity, willing motion would be valid from him, and if the second were alone, willing rest would be valid from him. So when they are together, either they remain as they were in the state of solitude, or they do not remain in that state. If they remain in that state, then willing motion from this one and willing rest from the second is valid in the state of conjunction, and that is the desired conclusion. If they do not remain in the state of conjunction as they were in solitude, then this is false for several aspects: First, since they are equal in perfect power and divinity, the prevention of this one from willing due to that is not more appropriate than the prevention of that one from willing due to this, so each must be repelled by the other, and that is impossible. Because the cause of repelling the first side is the occurrence of the second side, and the cause of repelling the second side is the occurrence of the first side, so if both sides are repelled together, it would necessitate both sides together as we mentioned at the beginning of the proof. And everything whose existence leads to its non-existence, or whose non-existence leads to its existence, is false. So this statement must be false. Second, that the attachment of the will of each of them to that specific action is a decree established from eternity, and eternity does not allow its removal, so that will must not be annulled. Third, when this one willed something and the second willed its opposite, then they both became incapable of attaining their desires in the state of conjunction, then this incapable one would be stronger, and the weak one more perfect. This is the completion of the exposition of this station. For we say: The pivot of this statement is that every decree that is valid in the state of solitude must remain valid and possible in the state of conjunction. We say: This is false, indicated by the fact that this one when alone is capable of attaining his desire, and that one when alone is also capable, but in the state of conjunction they do not remain in this state. So it is proven that the possibility of a decree in solitude does not necessitate its possibility in conjunction. Thus this statement collapses.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage handles an objection and its rebuttal. The objector says: Your proof assumes it is possible for one god to will motion and the other to will rest; why is that possible? The objector suggests the proof: if alone, each can will his opposite, so when together they should be able to as well. The author responds that this reasoning is false for three reasons. First, because both are equal in power and divinity, neither has priority; if one's will were to prevail, it would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is absurd. Moreover, if each is repelled by the other, that would require both to be repelled simultaneously, leading to contradiction. Second, each god's will is eternal; eternity cannot cease, so the wills cannot be annulled. Third, if both become incapable when together, that would imply the incapable is stronger and the weak more perfect—an absurdity. The author then refutes the underlying principle that what holds in solitude must hold in conjunction: if alone each is capable, but together they are not, showing the principle false.

الاجتماع
الإرادة
الأزل
الدافع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since each god can will something alone, they can will opposites together without contradiction.
Correction: The author shows that equality of power prevents simultaneous opposite wills, eternity of will prevents annulment, and the conjunction state changes the dynamics, so the principle is false.

Checkpoint

B) Both gods are equal, so neither's will can prevail without a preponderator.
C) The incapable god would be stronger.
D) The body would remain without motion or rest.

Key Takeaway
The objection that two gods can will opposites fails because it assumes a false parity between solitude and conjunction, and leads to absurd consequences.

?
The objector's argument relies on a false assumption about capabilities in isolation vs. together. Where in your own life do you assume that something true in isolation remains true in combination? How might this lead you to faulty reasoning about Allah? Write down one area where you need to correct such thinking.
?
The author shows that two equal gods would mutually repel each other's wills. In your heart, are there any 'partners' you have given to Allah—like status, wealth, or comfort—that effectively 'repel' your devotion to Him? Identify one and resolve to remove it this week.

Fourth Objection and Author's Response
Author presents the fourth objection and begins answering the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the tawhid proof and the author's answer to the first objection, including the argument about combining opposites and the refutation of counter-objections involving knowledge and will.

Core Idea
Even limited-power gods would lead to logical impossibility, confirming the tawhid proof.

In this passage, the author continues defending the proof for God's unity by addressing a refined objection. The objector tries to limit the scope of the earlier argument, but the author shows that the same reasoning applies even if the gods have limited power. The answer then refutes counter-objections based on knowledge of outcomes and motives. Although the passage is cut off, the logical structure is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice that the objector concedes the proof prevents two all-powerful gods but argues it does not prevent two gods with limited power, nor a powerless necessary being.
This objection tries to narrow the scope of the earlier proof.
2
Understand the answer's core argument
The author argues that if each god alone could will a certain action (e.g., motion or rest), then when together they must retain that ability, but that would require combining opposites, which is impossible. Therefore, the existence of two gods leads to impossibility.
The key point: retaining separate abilities leads to contradiction.
3
Examine the first counter-objection refutation
The objector claims that knowledge of which side will occur would prevent conflict. The author responds that knowledge depends on occurrence, which depends on will—reversing this leads to circularity (dawr).
Circularity is a logical fallacy, so the objection fails.
4
Consider the incomplete second counter-objection
The objector argues that knowledge of benefit leads both gods to will the same side. The author begins a reply that seems to involve the role of motive, but the text cuts off.
The incomplete part suggests a further refutation.

السؤال الرابع : هب أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل يمنع من وجود إلهين يكون كل واحد منهما قادرا على [ ما لا نهاية له من المقدورات ، إلا أنه لا يدل على امتناع وجود إلهين يكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على بعض المقدورات (۱) ] ولا يدل أيضاً على وجود موجود آخر واجب الوجود لذاته ، بحيث لا يقدر على شيء البتة ، سواء كان حياً أو ميتاً

والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إنا قد دللنا على أن كل واحد منهما لو انفرد ، فإنه يصح منه القصد إلى تكوين ذلك الضد ، وعند اجتماعهما ، وجب أن يبقيا على ما كانا عليه حال الانفراد . قوله : « ينتقض هذا ، بما أن هذا وحده قادر على خلق الحركة ، وذاك وحده قادر على خلق السكون ، ثم إنهما عند الاجتماع لا يبقيان قادرين على تحصيل هذين المرادين ، قلنا الدليل يقتضي بقاء كل واحد منهما قادراً عند الاجتماع على ما كان قادراً عليه حال الانفراد ، إلا أن ذلك محال ، لما أنه يقتضي الجمع بين الضدين فكان القول بوجود الإلمين مفضياً إلى أحد هذين المحالين ، وهو أن يتعذر عليها حال الاجتماع، ما كان مقدوراً لهما حال الانفراد إلا أن ذلك محال ، لما أنه يقتضي الجمع بين الضدين . ولما كان القول . بوجود الإلهين مفضياً إلى أحد هذين المحالين وجب القطع بأن فرض وجود الألمين محال : وهذا يؤكد الدليل المذكور ولا يوجب الطعن فيه . أما قوله في المعارضة الأولى : ( إن كل واحد منهما عالم بجميع المعلومات ، فيكون كل واحد منهما عالماً بأن أي الطرفين يقع ، وأيهما لا يقع ؟ وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن تريد ، إلا ذلك الواحد ، فيمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما ، قلنا : العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع الذي هو تبع الإرادة الوقوع ، فلو جعلنا إرادة الوقوع تابعة للعلم بالوقوع ، لزم الدور ، وأنه باطل . أما قوله في المعارضة الثانية : ( إن كل واحد منهما يكون عالماً بأن الطرف الراجح في المصلحة أيهما ؟ فوجب كونهما مريدين لذلك الطرف ، قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي
The fourth question: Suppose that the proof you mentioned prevents the existence of two gods each of whom is powerful over [what has no end of possibles, except that it does not indicate the impossibility of the existence of two gods each of whom is powerful over some possibles (1)] and also does not indicate the existence of another existent necessary of existence in itself, such that it is not able to do anything at all, whether it is alive or dead. So this is the summary of speech on the side of the question. And the answer to the first question: We have already argued that if each one of them were alone, then intending the composition of that opposite is valid from him, and when they are together, they must remain as they were in the state of solitude. His statement: 'This is vitiated by the fact that this one alone is able to create motion, and that one alone is able to create rest, then when they are together they do not remain able to achieve these two intentions,' we say: The proof requires that each one of them remains able when together over what he was able over in the state of solitude, except that is impossible because it necessitates combining opposites. So the statement of the existence of two gods leads to one of two impossibilities: either they become incapable when together of what was possible for them when alone, except that is impossible because it necessitates combining opposites. And since the statement of the existence of two gods leads to one of these two impossibilities, it is necessary to judge that the assumption of the existence of two gods is impossible. And this confirms the mentioned proof and does not necessitate impugning it. As for his statement in the first objection: 'Each of them knows all known things, so each of them knows which of the two sides will occur and which will not occur; and if that is so, it is impossible that he intend except that one, so the occurrence of conflict between them is impossible,' we say: Knowledge of occurrence is dependent on occurrence which is dependent on the will of occurrence, so if we made the will of occurrence dependent on knowledge of occurrence, circularity would be necessary, and that is false. As for his statement in the second objection: 'Each of them knows which side is preponderant in benefit, so they must both will that side,' we say: The act is either dependent on the motive
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the earlier proof against the existence of two gods. The objector concedes that the proof prevents two gods each with unlimited power, but argues it does not prevent two gods each with limited power, nor does it prevent a separate necessary existent that is completely powerless. Then the author begins answering the first objection (not the fourth). The answer argues that if each god alone could will a certain opposite (e.g., motion or rest), then when together they must retain that ability, but that would lead to combining opposites which is impossible, so the assumption of two gods is impossible. The author then refutes two further counter-objections: first, that knowledge of which event will occur would prevent conflict; the answer is that knowledge follows occurrence, which follows will—so reversing this leads to circularity. Second, that knowledge of the benefit leads both to will the same side; the answer is cut off, but it begins to say that the act either depends on the motive. The passage is incomplete but illustrates the dialectical method of defending the tawhid proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tawhid proof only works against gods with unlimited power.
Correction: The proof also works against any two independent powerful beings because they would each retain their ability to will opposite things, leading to contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the first objection?
A) Two gods can each will different things without conflict.
B) Two gods together would remain able to will opposites, leading to impossibility.
C) The proof against two gods is invalid.
D) Knowledge prevents conflict between gods.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods does not depend on unlimited power; even limited power leads to logical contradiction when multiple wills are involved.

?

?
The refutation of the sub-objections hinges on avoiding circularity—knowledge dependent on will, then will dependent on knowledge. Is your faith a closed loop of comfortable routines that never demands real submission? Where in your life have you made your own reasoning the final authority instead of the clear command of Allah?

The Fourth Objection and Its Answer: Limited Power Doesn't Help
Sanusi presents an objection and answers it, strengthening the proof of tawheed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof against multiple gods remains valid even if each god's power is limited to some things, and why the attempt to avoid the contradiction fails.

Core Idea
Two gods, even with limited power, cannot avoid impossible conflict.

A clever objection to the earlier proof suggests that it only applies if each god has infinite power over all things. But what if each god is limited to different domains? The answer shows that even then, the contradiction of opposite wills arises. This passage strengthens the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the structure
The passage has two main parts: the objection (question) and the answer. Within the answer, there is a sub-objection and its resolution. Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says the proof only blocks two gods each with infinite power over all things, but not two gods each with power over only some things. Also, it doesn't disprove a necessary being with zero power.
Notice the phrase 'هب أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل' — it starts a hypothetical.
3
Follow the answer's logic
The author argues that if each god were alone, each could intend opposite actions (like creating motion vs. rest). When together, they must retain those capabilities, but that would require combining the opposites — impossible.
The key is that the power to create opposite effects leads to contradiction when both try to exercise it.
4
Examine the sub-objection and its resolution
The sub-objection gives a concrete example: one god alone can create motion, the other rest, but together they cannot both get their way. The reply: retaining capability would force impossible
The text says 'إلا أن ذلك محال، لما أنه يقتضي الجمع بين الضدين'.
5
Conclude with the decisive judgment
Since assuming two gods leads to either loss of power or combining opposites — both impossible — the assumption must be false, confirming the original proof.
The final sentence says 'وهذا يؤكد الدليل المذكور ولا يوجب الطعن فيه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : هب أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل يمنع من وجود إلهين يكون كل واحد منهما قادرا على [ ما لا نهاية له من المقدورات ، إلا أنه لا يدل على امتناع وجود إلهين يكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على بعض المقدورات (۱) ] ولا يدل أيضاً على وجود موجود آخر واجب الوجود لذاته ، بحيث لا يقدر على شيء البتة ، سواء كان حياً أو ميتاً. فهذا جملة الكلام في طرف السؤال. والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إنا قد دللنا على أن كل واحد منهما لو انفرد، فإنه يصح منه القصد إلى تكوين ذلك الضد ، وعند اجتماعهما، وجب أن يبقيا على ما كانا عليه حال الانفراد . قوله : « ينتقض هذا ، بما أن هذا وحده قادر على خلق الحركة ، وذاك وحده قادر على خلق السكون ، ثم إنهما عند الاجتماع لا يبقيان قادرين على تحصيل هذين المرادين » قلنا الدليل يقتضي بقاء كل واحد منهما قادراً عند الاجتماع على ما كان قادراً عليه حال الانفراد، إلا أن ذلك محال، لما أنه يقتضي الجمع بين الضدين فكان القول بوجود الإلهين مفضياً إلى أحد هذين المحالين، وهو أن يتعذر عليها حال الاجتماع، ما كان مقدوراً لهما حال الانفراد إلا أن ذلك محال، لما أنه يقتضي الجمع بين الضدين . ولما كان القول بوجود الإلهين مفضياً إلى أحد هذين المحالين وجب القطع بأن فرض وجود الإلهين محال : وهذا يؤكد الدليل المذكور ولا يوجب الطعن فيه .
The fourth question: Suppose that the proof you have mentioned prevents the existence of two gods each of whom is capable of [what has no end of the things they are capable of, except that it does not indicate the impossibility of the existence of two gods each of whom is capable of some of the things they are capable of (1)] and it also does not indicate the existence of another existent necessary in itself, such that it is not capable of anything at all, whether it is living or dead. So this is the summary of the speech on the side of the question. And the answer to the first question: We have indeed proven that if each of them were alone, then intending to create that opposite would be valid from him, and when they come together, it is necessary that they remain as they were in the state of isolation. His statement: 'This is refuted by the fact that this one alone is capable of creating motion, and that one alone is capable of creating rest, and then when they come together they do not remain capable of obtaining these two desired objects.' We say: The proof requires that each of them remain capable when together of what he was capable of when alone, except that that is impossible because it would require combining two opposites. So the claim of the existence of two gods leads to one of two impossibilities: either that they are prevented when together from what was possible for them when alone, except that that is impossible because it would require combining two opposites. And since the claim of the existence of two gods leads to one of these two impossibilities, it is necessary to decisively judge that the assumption of the existence of two gods is impossible. And this confirms the mentioned proof and does not necessitate criticism of it.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth question and the answer to the first part of it. The questioner objects that the proof against two gods only applies if each god has infinite power over all things, but does it also prevent two gods each with limited power over some things? And does it also prove the existence of a necessary existent that cannot do anything at all? The answer summarizes the speech on the question side. Then the author gives the answer to the first question: we have shown that if each god were alone, each could intend to create the opposite of what the other would create. When they come together, they must remain as they were when alone. An objection is raised: but when alone, one can create motion and the other rest, but together they cannot both obtain their desires. The answer: the proof requires that each remain capable when together of what he was capable when alone, but that is impossible because it would require combining two opposites (e.g., motion and rest in the same object). Thus the assumption of two gods leads to impossibility: either they lose some of their power when together (which implies the impossibility of being gods), or they remain capable but then would have to bring about two opposites simultaneously—also impossible. Hence, the assumption of two gods is necessarily false. This confirms the original proof and does not invalidate it.

Correction: The proof works even with limited power because each god alone can intend an opposite action, and when together they must retain that capability, which leads to the impossible attempt to combine opposites.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods does not depend on infinite power; the contradiction of opposite wills persists regardless of the scope of their power, confirming the original proof of tawheed.

?

The Third Objection: Knowledge Prevents Conflict
He answers the objection that knowledge prevents conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that each god's knowledge prevents conflict by revealing that the objection actually assumes conflict is possible.

Core Idea
An objection that seems to block conflict concedes conflict is possible.

This passage presents a classic dialectical move in kalam. The objector argues that each god's foreknowledge of the futility of opposition prevents any attempt at conflict. The author's response is subtle: he points out that this objection implicitly admits that without that knowledge, conflict would be possible. This shifts the logical burden back onto the objector.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's statement: 'the knowledge of each one... that if he attempted to oppose the second, it would be impossible for him to achieve his desire because of the prevention from opposition.' Notice the assumption that knowledge is the barrier.
The objector uses knowledge as a preventer.
2
Identify the author's reply
The author says: 'this refers to preventing him from undertaking opposition. And in it is an admission that were it not for this preventer, opposition would be possible.'
The author turns the objection into a concession.
3
Reflect on the logical implication
Ask yourself: what does the objection admit? It admits that without knowledge, conflict is possible. So the objector's claim does not show conflict is inherently impossible, only that it is prevented by knowledge.
This strengthens the original argument that conflict is not impossible in itself.

Scope of the Unity Proof
He clarifies that the proof only aims to show the impossibility of two perfectly powerful gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise scope of the proof for divine unity: it disproves two all-powerful gods, not any second being.

Core Idea
The proof targets only two perfectly powerful gods.

When arguing for the oneness of God, one might object that the standard proof only rules out two equally powerful deities, not a lesser second being. In this passage, the author clarifies the exact aim of the proof, ensuring we don't overclaim what it demonstrates.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objection presented at the beginning of the passage. It claims the proof does not rule out two gods with limited power, nor a second being with no power at all.
This objection is hypothetical, raised to test the proof's limits.
2
Identify the author's response
The author responds directly: 'our purpose with this proof is only to show the impossibility of asserting the existence of two gods with complete power.' Notice the word 'only' — this limits the scope.
3
Understand the limitation
The proof does not claim to disprove any second being whatsoever. It is specifically about two all-powerful gods (كاملي القدرة). This is a key precision in theology.
Recognizing the limited scope prevents misapplying the argument.
4
Reflect on the closing phrase
The passage ends with 'والله أعلم' (And God knows best). This reminds us of humility: even when arguing, we acknowledge that ultimate knowledge is with Allah.
This phrase is common in Islamic scholarly writing.

The Second Proof: Impossibility of Two All-Powerful Gods
Second proof based on the assumption of two all-powerful gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second proof for God's oneness, which demonstrates that two all-powerful gods cannot exist because each would have power over the same objects, leading to contradiction.

Core Idea
Two all-powerful gods each controlling all things: a logical impossibility.

The second proof in the matn builds on the concept of absolute power. It argues that if two gods each had power over all possible things, then each would be able to perform the exact same action as the other—a scenario that leads to absurdity. This lesson unpacks the conditional reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the passage presents a conditional statement and begins to explain why its consequence is impossible.
The passage cuts off; focus on the structure we have.
2
Identify the conditional and its consequence
Find the 'if...then...' structure. The conditional supposes two gods, each powerful over all possible things. The consequence is that each is powerful over the very same object of power of the other.
3
Understand why the consequence is impossible
The text states this consequence is impossible (محال). Reflect on why two agents cannot both have complete power over the same single action in a way that both can will it independently.
The commentary provides a brief explanation.
4
Distinguish this proof from the first
Note that this argument is based on power over all possible things, not on motives or wills. This is a distinct line of reasoning for tawḥīd.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات أن إله العالم واحد بناء على أصول المتكلمين: أن نقول: لو قدرنا وجود إلهين، قادرين على جميع الممكنات، لزم القطع بكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على عين مقدور الآخر. وهذا محال. فالقول بوجود الإلهين محال. أما الشرطية فظاهرة، وأما أن هذه الملازمة ممتنعة. فتقريره: أنه لما كان كل واحد منهما قادراً على جميع الممكنات، فكل ما كان مقدوراً لهذا،
The second proof for establishing that the God of the world is one, based on the principles of the mutakallimun: we say: if we suppose the existence of two gods, both powerful over all possible things, then it necessarily follows that each one of them is powerful over the very same object of power of the other. And this is impossible. So the statement of the existence of two gods is impossible. As for the conditional statement, it is obvious. And as for the impossibility of this implication, its explanation: because when each one of them is powerful over all possible things, then everything that is an object of power for this one,
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is the start of the second proof (الحجة الثانية) for God's oneness, based on the principles of the mutakallimun (أصول المتكلمين). The proof is a conditional: if two gods exist, each having power over all possible things (قادرين على جميع الممكنات), then each must be able to do the exact same action as the other (قادراً على عين مقدور الآخر). This leads to a contradiction (محال), because two agents cannot both have complete power over the same single action in a way that allows both to will it independently. Therefore, the existence of two such gods is impossible. The author notes that the conditional (if two exist, then each has power over the other's action) is obvious, and then begins to explain why the implication is impossible, but the text cuts off. The explanation likely continues on the next page.

Refuting Two Gods: The Argument from Identical Knowledge
A deductive proof showing two gods would have identical knowledge, leading to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the identity of knowledge between two supposed gods leads to contingency and impossibility, thereby proving tawhid.

Core Idea
If two gods existed, their knowledge would be interchangeable, making them contingent and thus impossible.

This lesson presents a powerful deductive argument against the existence of two gods by examining the nature of divine knowledge. If there were two gods, each would know the same things in the same way, making their knowledge identical. By the principle that what holds for one holds for its similar, this leads to the conclusion that each god's knowledge could subsist in the other's essence, implying that the specification of knowledge to one essence requires a cause. This cause would make the knowledge originated, contradicting the divine nature. The passage thus demonstrates the impossibility of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the logical flow and key terms.
Pay special attention to the phrase 'كل ما صح على الشيء، صح على مثله' – a crucial principle.
2
Unpack the argument's premises
Identify the steps: (1) Two gods' knowledge relates to the same object identically, making them similar. (2) Whatever is valid for one is valid for its similar. (3) Therefore, the knowledge of one can subsist in the other's essence.
Understand how interchangeability leads to the need for a specifier.
3
Follow the conclusion to impossibility
The specifier implies origination (حادث). If God's knowledge is originated, then God would be contingent, which is impossible for a necessary being. Hence, the assumption of two gods is false.
This is a classic a priori proof against shirk in the divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this argument reinforces the Islamic doctrine of tawhid by showing that multiplicity necessarily entails contingency. Also note that the author mentions deep debates about this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلومات ، فحينئذ يكون علم كل واحد منهما متعلقا بعين ما تعلق به علم الثاني ، وكل علمين تعلقا بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد فهما مثلان ، فيلزم أن يكون علم كل واحد منهما مثلا لعلم الآخر ، وكل ما صح على الشيء ، صح على مثله ، فكما صح على هذا العلم أن يكون قائماً بهذه الذات ، فكذلك وجب على العلم القائم بالثاني ، أن يكون قائماً بالأول ، فيثبت أن كل واحد يصح من هذين العلمين ، يصح قيامه بكل واحد منها بدلاً عن الآخر . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان اختصاص هذه الذات بهذا العلم دون الذات الأخرى وجب أن يكون بتخصيص [مخصص (۱)] وجعل جاعل ، وكل ما حصل بالفاعل الجاعل ، كان حادثا ، فلزم أن يكون علم الله محدثا ، وأن تكون قدرته محدثة، وكل ما كان كذلك امتنع كونه إلها . فيثبت : أن القول بوجود الإلمين يفضي إلى هذه المحالات ، فوجب أن يكون محالاً . وفي تقرير هذه الحجة وتزييفها أبحاث كثيرة غامضة .
Therefore, the knowledge of each would be related to the very same thing to which the knowledge of the second is related. Every two knowledges that relate to one object in one way are similar. So it follows that the knowledge of each is similar to the knowledge of the other. And whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its similar. Just as it is valid for this knowledge to subsist in this essence, so must the knowledge subsisting in the second be valid to subsist in the first. Thus it is established that every one of these two knowledges that is valid, its subsistence in each of them in place of the other is valid. When this is the case, the specification of this essence with this knowledge rather than the other essence must be by a specification of a specifier (تخصيص مخصص) and the making of a maker. Whatever is brought about by an agent-maker is originated. Therefore it follows that God's knowledge is originated, and His power is originated. Whatever is so is impossible to be a god. Thus it is established that the statement of the existence of two gods leads to these impossibilities. So it must be impossible. There are many deep investigations regarding the presentation and refutation of this argument.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a deductive proof against the existence of two gods. The argument proceeds from the nature of knowledge: if there were two gods, each would have knowledge of the same objects in the same way, making their knowledge identical. By the principle that what holds for one thing holds for its similar, the knowledge of one god could subsist in the essence of the other. This leads to the conclusion that the knowledge is not necessarily tied to a particular essence; its specification to one essence requires a specifier, implying contingency. Anything contingent is originated, so God's attributes would be originated, which is impossible for an eternal being. Therefore, the assumption of two gods is impossible. The author notes that this argument has been debated extensively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two gods could have distinct knowledge, each knowing different things or in different ways.
Correction: The argument shows that if they are perfect and omniscient, their knowledge must be identical in object and mode, leading to interchangeability and contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from knowledge, if there were two gods, what would be the consequence regarding their knowledge?
A) Their knowledge would be different
B) Their knowledge would be identical
C) Their knowledge would be dependent
D) Their knowledge would be non-existent

Key Takeaway
The assumption of two gods leads to the impossibility of their attributes being eternal, thus proving that God must be one and unique.

?

?

The Fourth Argument: Distinction Impossible
Al-Razi's fourth argument with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that two gods cannot be distinguished by time, place, or essence, and how the objection based on essence is refuted.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot be told apart — if they could, one wouldn't be God.

This lesson presents al-Razi's fourth argument against the possibility of two gods. The argument denies any basis for distinguishing between two supposed deities, then addresses and refutes a key objection that distinction could be by essence. Understanding this argument clarifies why tawhid is rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and objection
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: initial argument, objection, and then the answer.
2
Identify the three bases for distinction
The argument considers time, place, and essence. Understand why time and place are rejected for two eternal, immaterial beings.
3
Examine the objector's example
The objector gives the example of knowledge and power inhering in one substance, distinguished by essence not by time or place. Think about why this might seem plausible.
4
Follow the answer's logical structure
The answer splits into two cases: if the distinguishing feature is essential to divinity, then the one without it is not God; if not essential, both could lack it, so no distinction remains.
Trace how each case leads to the conclusion.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since no distinction is possible under any scenario, multiplicity of gods is impossible. This establishes the rational necessity of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قالوا : لو فرضنا الهين ، لأمتنع أن يمتاز أحدهما عن الآخر بالزمان ، لأنها قديمان، وبالمكان لما ثبت أن الإله يجب أن يكون منزها عن الحجمية والمكان ، وإذا لم يحصل الامتياز بينهما ، وجب أن لا يبقى التعدد وقيل عليه : لم لا يجوز أن يتميز أحدهما على الآخر بالماهية والحقيقة ؟ ألا ترى أنه إذا حلّ في الجوهر الواحد علم وقدرة ، فإنه لم يحصل الامتياز بينهما لا بالزمان ولا بالمكان ولا المحل ، ولكن حصل الامتياز بينهما بالماهية والحقيقة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر مهنا كذلك ؟ وأجيب عنه : بأنه لو وجد إلمان لامتنع أن يمتاز أحدهما عن الآخر بالزمان لأنها قديمان وبالمكان لأنها من الصفات ، وأما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن حصل الامتياز بينهما بأمر من الأمور ، فذاك الأمر إما أن يكون معتبراً في تحقق الإلهية ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، فإن كان ذلك الأمر معتبراً في الألمية [فالذي لم يحصل له ذلك الأمر ، وجب أن لا يكون إلهاً ، وإن لم يكن معتبراً في الإلهية (۱)] كان حصول الإله بدون تلك الصفة جائزاً . فإن فرضناهما حاصلين بدون تلك الصفة ، وجب أن لا يحصل الامتياز بينهما أصلاً فيثبت أنا لو فرضنا إلهمين ، وجب أن لا يحصل الامتياز بينهما بأمر من الأمور البتة، وإذا لم يحصل الامتياز البتة، لم يحصل التعدد البتة، فيثبت أن القول بالتعدد محال .
The fourth argument: They said: If two gods were supposed, it would be impossible for one to be distinguished from the other by time, because both are eternal; and by place, because it is established that God must be transcendent over volume and place. When no distinction occurs between them, multiplicity must not remain. Objection: Why is it not possible for one to be distinguished from the other by essence (ماهية) and reality (حقيقة)? Do you not see that when knowledge and power inhere in one substance, distinction between them does not occur by time, place, or locus, but distinction occurs between them by essence and reality. So why may the matter not be so here? Answer: If there were two gods, it would be impossible for one to be distinguished from the other by time because they are eternal, and by place because they are attributes (من الصفات). But if this is not the case, then if distinction occurs between them by some matter, that matter is either considered in the realization of divinity or not. If that matter is considered in divinity, then the one who does not possess it must not be a god. If it is not considered in divinity, then the existence of a god without that attribute is permissible. If we suppose them to exist without that attribute, then no distinction would occur between them at all. Thus it is established that if we suppose two gods, no distinction would occur between them by any matter whatsoever. When no distinction occurs, multiplicity does not occur. Thus it is established that the statement of multiplicity is impossible.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and an objection-response. The fourth argument states that two eternal beings cannot be distinguished by time (both eternal) or space (both immaterial). An objector suggests distinction by essence, using the example of knowledge and power inhering in one substance: they are distinguished by essence, not by time or place. The answer is carefully structured: if the distinguishing feature is essential to divinity, then the one lacking it is not a god; if it is not essential, then both can exist without it, so no distinction remains. Hence multiplicity is impossible. The argument is logical and refutes the possibility of any real distinction between two supposed gods.

زمان

ماهية
حقيقة

إلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could be distinguished by their essence or nature.
Correction: If the essence is essential to divinity, then a god lacking it is not a god; if not essential, both could lack it, so no distinction remains. Thus distinction by essence fails.

Checkpoint

B) The distinguishing feature must be essential to divinity, otherwise it doesn't matter

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that any possible basis for distinguishing two gods leads to contradiction, so multiplicity of gods is rationally impossible.

Sufficiency of One Creator
Argument that one God is sufficient for the world, so a second is unknown and unprovable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the epistemological argument that one God is sufficient to explain the world, making a second god unprovable and thus not to be affirmed.

Core Idea
One sufficient cause is enough; a second is epistemically unwarranted.

This lesson presents a concise argument for monotheism that focuses on the sufficiency of one Creator. The argument does not attempt to prove the impossibility of a second god, but rather shows that such a claim lacks both intuitive and inferential support. As you read, notice how the argument relies on the evidence of the world itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note that it is a single line of reasoning from sufficiency to unprovability.
2
Identify the two modes of knowledge
The argument denies knowledge of a second god both by necessity (ضرورة) and by inference (نظر). Understand why each is rejected.
Necessity is self-evident; inference is based on the world as evidence.
3
Understand the inferential step
See why the world's proof—whether through possibility or origination—only requires one Maker. This is the key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخاصة : إن وجود الإله الواحد كافي في حصول تدبير العالم وأما الزائد عليه فهو غير معلوم بالضرورة ولا بالنظر ، فلا يجوز إثباته . أما أنه غير معلوم بالضرورة [ فظاهر . وأما أنه غير معلوم بالنظر (۲) ] فلان الدليل الدال على وجود الصانع هو هذا العالم، إما بحسب جوازه ، أو بحسب حدوثه . وعلى التقديرين فالصانع الواحد كافي. فيثبت : أن الصانع الثاني لم يعلم وجوده لا بالضرورة ولا بالنظر البتة . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع إثباته . فوجب الاقتصار على القول بالإله الواحد . وههنا آخر الكلام في حكاية دلائل القائلين بالتوحيد
The special argument: The existence of one God is sufficient for the administration of the world. As for the extra, it is not known by necessity (ضرورة) nor by inference (نظر), so it is not permissible to affirm it. As for its not being known by necessity, it is apparent. As for its not being known by inference, because the proof indicating the existence of the Maker is this world, either in terms of its contingency or its origination. On both suppositions, one Maker is sufficient. Thus it is established that the existence of a second Maker is not known at all, neither by necessity nor by inference. When this is the case, its affirmation is impossible. So one must restrict oneself to the statement of one God. Here ends the account of the proofs of the proponents of tawheed.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological argument for monotheism. It states that the existence of one God is sufficient to account for the world's order. Any additional god cannot be known by direct necessity (i.e., self-evident) nor by inference from the world, because the world itself only proves a single sufficient cause. The argument is based on the principle that the proof for the Maker is the world itself—either through its possibility (imkan) or its origination (huduth). In neither case does it require more than one Maker. Therefore, affirming a second god is baseless. The passage concludes with a note that this ends the exposition of proofs for tawheed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a second god is possible; the argument shows it is unprovable and thus not to be asserted.
Correction: The argument does not claim a second god is impossible, but rather that it is epistemically unsupported—neither self-evident nor inferred from the world. Since the world's evidence is satisfied by one sufficient cause, asserting a second is unwarranted.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The special argument shows that a second god is epistemically unwarranted because one sufficient Creator is all the world's evidence requires.

Impossibility of Two Gods Due to Indistinguishability
Proof that two gods would be indistinguishable, hence multiplicity is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if two gods existed without a distinguishing attribute, they would be identical, making multiplicity impossible.

Core Idea
Indistinguishability implies identity; identity eliminates multiplicity.

This lesson presents a dense logical proof against the possibility of multiple gods. The argument examines what would happen if two supposed gods shared all the same attributes — they would be indistinguishable, and therefore identical. This reasoning is a cornerstone of Islamic theology's affirmation of God's oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: the author considers an attribute, then supposes two gods lacking it, leading to indistinguishability and impossibility of multiplicity.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the three key terms: الامتياز (distinction), التعدد (multiplicity), and محال (impossible). Understand how they function in the argument.
These terms are the backbone of the proof.
3
Follow the logical chain
Map the steps: (1) If an attribute is not essential, gods can exist without it. (2) Two such gods are indistinguishable. (3) Indistinguishability means they are identical. (4) Identity prevents multiplicity. Work through each link.
Use the guided breakdown to see the steps clearly.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that claiming multiple gods (التعدد) is impossible (محال). Ask yourself why this conclusion is necessary given the premises.
This shows the strength of the logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون إلهاً، وإن لم يكن معتبراً في الإلهية كان حصول الإله بدون تلك الصفة جائزاً. فإن فرضناهما حاصلين بدون تلك الصفة، وجب أن لا يحصل الامتياز بينهما أصلاً، فيثبت أنا لو فرضنا إلهمين، وجب أن لا يحصل الامتياز بينهما بأمر من الأمور البتة، وإذا لم يحصل الامتياز البتة، لم يحصل التعدد البتة، فيثبت أن القول بالتعدد محال.
He would be a god, and if it is not considered in divinity [i.e., that attribute is not necessary for divinity], then obtaining divinity without that attribute would be permissible. For if we suppose them (i.e., two supposed gods) existing without that attribute, then it would be necessary that no distinction occurs between them at all. Thus it is established that if we suppose two gods, it would be necessary that no distinction occurs between them by any means whatsoever. And when no distinction occurs at all, then no multiplicity occurs at all. Hence it is established that the claim of multiplicity is impossible.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof against the existence of multiple gods. It begins by considering an attribute that might be considered essential to divinity. If that attribute is not essential, then a god could exist without it. If two such gods exist without any distinguishing attribute, they would be indistinguishable, implying they are identical. Since multiplicity requires distinct individuals, the impossibility of distinction entails the impossibility of multiplicity. The argument is a classic application of the principle of identity: if two entities share all properties, they are one and the same. Therefore, the claim of multiple gods is logically impossible.

Key Terms
الامتياز
التعدد
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could differ in attributes even if they are both divine.
Correction: The argument shows that any attribute that is not essential can be absent in both, so they would be indistinguishable. Even if they shared some essential attributes, they could only differ in non-essential ones, but those differences could be absent, leading to indistinguishability. The logical conclusion is that multiplicity is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence if two gods exist without any distinguishing attribute?
A) They would be distinct.
B) They would be identical, making multiplicity impossible.
C) They would be separate but equal.
D) They would be in conflict.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity of gods is impossible because any two supposed gods would be indistinguishable and therefore identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the existence of two gods is impossible because they would be indistinguishable, how does that force you to confront any hidden 'partners' in your life—things you rely on as if they were independent sources of power? Are you truly living as if Allah alone is utterly unique and without rival?
?
The proof shows that any non-essential attribute can be absent without compromising divinity. How does this strip away your excuses for associating anything else with Allah? Can you point to any area of your life where you treat something as essential that only Allah deserves?
?
This argument rests on the principle that indistinguishability implies identity. When you look at your heart's attachments, are they distinguishable from your love for Allah? Or have you blurred the lines, making other loves indistinguishable from your devotion to Him? What must you separate today?

The Specific Argument for One God's Sufficiency
The specific argument: one God suffices for world governance, so a second is not proven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific argument for monotheism based on epistemic sufficiency, and why a second god cannot be rationally proven.

Core Idea
One God suffices to explain the world; a second god is not proven and thus not affirmed.

This lesson presents what the author calls 'the specific argument' for the unity of God. It is a crisp epistemic proof: whatever explains the world adequately requires no addition. If one God is sufficient to account for the governance of the world, positing a second god is unnecessary and rationally unjustifiable. The argument moves from what is known by necessity and reasoning to the conclusion that only one God can be affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic original carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise, argumentative style.
The passage uses technical terms like 'داررة' (necessity) and 'نظر' (reasoning).
2
Identify the two epistemic categories
The argument distinguishes between what is known by immediate intuition (darura) and what is known by reasoned inference (nazar). The second god is not known by either.
3
Follow the sufficiency reasoning
Observe how the author argues that the evidence from the world (whether through contingency or temporality) only proves one Maker. That one is sufficient, so a second is not needed.
This is the core logical move: sufficiency implies no proof for excess.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since a second god is neither self-evident nor proven by reasoning, affirming it is impossible. Therefore we must restrict ourselves to one God.
This conclusion is not just economical but epistemically required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخاصة: إن وجود الإله الواحد كافي في حصول تدبير العالم، وأما الزائد عليه فهو غير معلوم بالضرورة ولا بالنظر، فلا يجوز إثباته. أما أنه غير معلوم بالضرورة فظاهر. وأما أنه غير معلوم بالنظر فلأن الدليل الدال على وجود الصانع هو هذا العالم، إما بحسب جوازه، أو بحسب حدوثه. وعلى التقديرين فالصانع الواحد كافي. فيثبت: أن الصانع الثاني لم يعلم وجوده لا بالضرورة ولا بالنظر البتة. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع إثباته. فوجب الاقتصار على القول بالإله الواحد. وههنا آخر الكلام في حكاية دلائل القائلين بالتوحيد.
The specific argument: The existence of one God suffices for the governance of the world. As for any additional [god], it is not known by necessity nor by reasoning, so it is not permissible to affirm it. As for its not being known by necessity, it is obvious. As for its not being known by reasoning, because the evidence indicating the existence of the Maker is this world, either in terms of its contingency (jawaz) or its temporality (huduth). In both cases, one Maker is sufficient. Hence it is established that the second Maker is not known to exist, neither by necessity nor by reasoning at all. And if that is the case, its affirmation is impossible. Therefore one must restrict [oneself] to the statement of one God. And here ends the account of the proofs of the proponents of unity (tawhid).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is what the author calls 'the specific argument' for monotheism. It is an epistemic proof based on sufficiency. The argument runs: The world is governed, and the existence of one God is sufficient to explain that governance. Any additional god is not known by immediate intuition (darura) nor through demonstrative reasoning (nazar). Intuition does not deliver it, and reasoning, when it proves a Maker from the world (either through contingency or temporality), only requires one. Therefore the second god remains unproven, and claiming it is impermissible. The passage concludes the section on proofs of monotheists.

Key Terms
الحجة الخاصة
جواز
حدوث
كافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'sufficiency' is a weak argument that merely suggests economy but does not disprove other gods.
Correction: In kalam epistemology, if something is not proven by necessity or reasoning, it cannot be affirmed at all. Sufficiency shows that no rational proof requires a second god, so affirming it is epistemically invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the specific argument, why is a second maker not known by reasoning?
A) Because the world's evidence points to one maker.
B) Because the world is eternal.
C) Because there are two makers in nature.
D) Because reasoning cannot know God.

Key Takeaway
The specific argument shows that one God is sufficient to explain the world, and since a second god is not proven by necessity or reasoning, it cannot be affirmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you truly internalized that affirming anything about Allah that is not proven by necessity or reasoning is 'impossible' (as the passage says), what would change about the way you speak about Allah's actions in your daily life? Are there phrases or beliefs you casually hold that would need to be abandoned or re-evaluated?

The Dualist Argument from Good and Evil
Dualists argue that good and evil in the world require two distinct gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist argument that the coexistence of good and evil in the world implies two distinct divine agents.

Core Idea
Opposites in the world require opposite causes.

The dualists observe that the world contains both good and evil, pleasure and pain, health and sickness. They argue that a single cause cannot produce opposite effects, so there must be two gods—one good, one evil. This lesson examines their reasoning and the key assumptions behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualist passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the list of opposites they mention.
2
Identify the empirical basis
The dualists base their argument on observable opposites in the world. List them: good/evil, benefit/harm, pleasure/pain, health/sickness, sweet/bitter.
These are all pairs of opposites found in everyday experience.
3
Understand the reasoning
The argument links each effect to a corresponding agent: good effects require a good agent, evil effects an evil agent. Since one agent cannot be both good and evil, there must be two agents.
This is a form of 'like produces like' reasoning.
4
Consider the conclusion
The dualists conclude that there are two gods: one good and praiseworthy, one evil and blameworthy.
In Islamic theology, this conclusion is rejected; God creates both good and evil with perfect wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القائلون بالتثنية فقد تعلقوا بأشياء. الحجة الأولى: قالوا: وجدنا في هذا العالم خيراً، وشراً، ونفعاً وضراً، ولذة وألماً، وصحة وسقماً، ومراً ومكروهاً. ومعلوم أن فاعل الخير خير، وفاعل الشر شرير. والفاعل الواحد يمتنع أن يكون خيراً وشريراً معاً، وأن يكون ممدوحاً ومذموماً معاً. فلا بد من فاعلين ليكون أحدهما خيراً ممدوحاً، ويكون الثاني شريراً مذموماً.
As for the proponents of dualism (tathniya), they have clung to [several] things. The first proof: They said: We find in this world good and evil, benefit and harm, pleasure and pain, health and sickness, sweet and bitter. And it is known that the doer of good is good, and the doer of evil is evil. One single agent cannot be both good and evil, nor can it be both praised and blamed. Hence there must be two agents, so that one is good (praised) and the second is evil (blamed).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the first dualist argument, based on empirical observation of opposites in the world. The dualists note that we observe both good (khair) and evil (sharr), benefit and harm, etc. They then apply a moral principle: the agent of good is good, and the agent of evil is evil. Since a single agent cannot be both good and evil simultaneously, they conclude that there must be two distinct agents—one good and one evil—corresponding to the two types of phenomena in the world.

Key Terms
التثنية
خير
شر
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualist argument assumes moral properties are intrinsic to the agent; that a good agent can only produce good and an evil agent only evil.
Correction: Islamic theology affirms that one God creates both good and evil with wisdom and justice; goodness and evil are not necessarily tied to the agent's essence but to His will and purpose.

Checkpoint
On what empirical observation do the dualists base their first argument?
A) Unity of creation.
B) Coexistence of opposites like good and evil.
C) Order in the universe.
D) Miracles of prophets.

Key Takeaway
The dualist argument from good and evil is based on the coexistence of opposites and the principle that like requires like cause, leading to the conclusion of two gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The dualists claim that good can only come from a good source and evil from an evil source. Think of a moment you struggled to see God's wisdom in a trial. Does that struggle make you lean toward dualism in your heart? What would it mean for your faith to affirm that God alone creates both the sweet and the bitter?
