Second Dualist Argument: Concept of Necessary Existence
Dualists argue that the concept of necessary existence can apply to many beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how dualists argue that the concept of necessary existence does not by itself prevent multiplicity, and how they use a reductio to conclude that multiple necessary existents are possible.

Core Idea
The concept of necessary existence does not logically forbid sharing; multiplicity must be argued away.

This passage presents the second dualist argument against the uniqueness of God. The dualists claim that the very concept of 'necessary existence in itself' (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) does not logically exclude being shared by multiple beings. They then consider whether something else could prevent sharing, and argue that it cannot. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum aimed at showing that multiple necessary existents are conceptually possible. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the full range of challenges to tawhid in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is structured as a proof by contradiction.
2
Identify the dualist claim
The dualists first assert that the concept of necessary existence is not inherently exclusive. They argue that if it were, that would be known intuitively, but it is not.
This is the foundation of the whole argument.
3
Follow the consideration of an accompanying factor
They then consider two possibilities: either something accompanies the concept to prevent sharing, or nothing does. They analyse the first possibility by examining whether the accompanying thing is a quiddity or not.
Both subcases are argued to be impossible or ineffective.
4
Understand the reductio
If nothing accompanies the concept, then sharing is not impossible. They then argue that if sharing were possible, the beings would be merely possible (imkan khas), which contradicts the assumption that they are necessary. Therefore, the only consistent conclusion is that multiple necessary existents exist.
This is a classic reductio to show the necessity of multiplicity.
5
Consider the theological response
Reflect on why Muslim theologians reject this argument. They maintain that the concept of necessary existence does indeed entail uniqueness, but not by a mere conceptual analysis; rather, it is proven through other arguments.
This argument is a challenge that any student of kalam must be able to answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن المفهوم من قولنا: واجب الوجود لذاته، أمر لا يمتنع مفهومه من كونه مقولاً على كثيرين. إذ لو كان نفس هذا المفهوم مانعاً من هذه الكثرة، لكان من تصور معنى واجب الوجود لذاته، وجب أن يعلم ببديهة يمتنع كون هذا المفهوم مشتركاً فيه بين كثيرين، ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا أن نفس تصور هذا المعنى غير مانع من الشركة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إما أن يقال: قارنه مفهوم آخر، يمنع من هذه الشركة، أو لم يقارنه ذلك. والأول باطل لأن ذلك المقارن إما أن يكون ماهية من الماهيات أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فتلك الماهية من حيث إنها ماهية تكون محتملة للشركة. فإذا قيدنا ماهية وجوب الوجود بهذه الماهية الثانية، كان الحاصل أيضاً ماهية كلية لأن الكلي إذا تقيد بالكلي، كان الحاصل منهما كلياً أيضاً. والكلي لا يمنع من احتمال الشركة. وأما إن قلنا: إن هذا المقارن ليس له ماهية ولا حقيقة أصلاً، امتنع كونه مقارناً لشيء آخر. هذا إذا قلنا: إن مفهوم وجوب الوجود بالذات قارنه ما صار مانعاً من الشركة، وأما إذا لم يقارنه شيء أصلاً، فنقول: تلك الماهية كانت محتملة للشركة، ولم يقارنه ما يمنع من هذه الشركة، وهذا يقتضي أن تكون هذه الشركة غير ممتنعة أصلاً، وإذا لم تكن ممتنعة، فهي إما ممكنة بالإمكان الخاص، فحينئذ لا يكون شيء منها واجب الوجود لذاته، فحينئذ لا تكون الكثرة واقعة في الأشياء التي يكون كل واحد منها واجب الوجود لذاته، قد فرضنا أن الأمر كذلك. هذا خلف. وإذا بطل كون تلك الأشياء ممكنة بالإمكان الخاص ثبت كونها واجبة الوجود لذواتها. وهذا يقتضي وجود أشياء يكون كل واحد منها واجب الوجود لذاته.
The second proof: The concept of what we say: 'necessary of existence in itself' (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) is something whose concept is not prevented from being predicated of many. For if this very concept were a barrier to this multiplicity, then whoever conceives the meaning of 'necessary of existence in itself' would necessarily know by intuition that this concept cannot be shared among many. And since it is not the case, we know that the mere conception of this meaning does not prevent sharing. When this is established we say: Either it is said that another concept accompanied it that prevents this sharing, or it did not accompany it. The first is false because that accompanying [concept] is either a quiddity (mahiyya) among quiddities or not. If it is the first, then that quiddity, insofar as it is a quiddity, is susceptible to sharing. So if we restrict the quiddity of necessary existence with this second quiddity, the resultant is also a universal quiddity, because the universal when restricted by the universal yields a universal. And the universal does not prevent the possibility of sharing. If we say that this accompanying [concept] has no quiddity or reality at all, then its accompanying something else is impossible. This is if we say that the concept of necessary existence in itself was accompanied by something that made it a barrier to sharing. But if nothing accompanied it at all, we say: That quiddity was susceptible to sharing, and nothing accompanied it to prevent this sharing, and this necessitates that this sharing is not impossible at all. And if it is not impossible, then it is either possible with special possibility (imkan khas). In that case, none of them would be necessary existence in itself, and then multiplicity would not occur among things each of which is necessary existence in itself, even though we have supposed that it is the case. This is a contradiction (khalaf). And if it is false that those things are possible with special possibility, then it is established that they are necessary existent in themselves. And this necessitates the existence of things each of which is necessary existence in itself.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a complex dualist argument regarding the concept of necessary existence (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). The dualists claim that this concept is not inherently exclusive; it can be predicated of many. They argue that if the concept itself prevented sharing, that would be known intuitively, but it is not. Then they consider whether something else accompanies the concept to prevent sharing. They argue that any accompanying quiddity would be universal and thus not prevent sharing, and if the accompanying factor has no quiddity, it cannot attach. Therefore, if nothing prevents sharing, multiple necessary existents are possible. They then use a reductio (khalaf) to show that if those multiple existents were merely possible, they wouldn't be necessary—contradicting the assumption. So they conclude that multiple necessary existents must exist. This argument is a conceptual analysis aimed at showing that the notion of necessary existence does not by itself entail uniqueness.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
ماهية
شركة
كلي
إمكان خاص
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument conclusively proves that multiple necessary existents actually exist.
Correction: The argument only aims to show that the concept of necessary existence does not logically preclude sharing; it does not provide a demonstration of actual multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the dualist second argument, what follows if the concept of necessary existence is not inherently exclusive?
A) It can only apply to one being.
B) It can apply to many beings.
C) It cannot apply to any being.
D) It applies only to matter.

Key Takeaway
The dualists argue that the mere concept of necessary existence does not logically require uniqueness; a separate argument is needed to establish tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists claim that the concept of necessary existence does not logically forbid sharing. When you reflect on your own understanding of God's oneness, do you rely more on rational arguments or on a direct, intuitive certainty? Has this passage made you question the strength of your conviction?
?
If the dualist argument is logically sound, why do you still believe in one God? Go beyond the general answer; what is the specific reason that moves your heart to reject multiplicity? Does your belief rest on revelation, reason, or both?
?
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum to force a conclusion. Are there beliefs you hold that you've never truly tested by reductio? Can you think of a belief about God that you assume to be necessary, but that might actually be open to question? How would you defend it?

Third Dualist Argument from Deficiency of a Single Necessary Being
Dualists argue that if only one necessary existent existed, existence would be minimal and deficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist argument that a single necessary being would be at the lowest and most deficient rank, and why this argument is flawed from an Islamic theological perspective.

Core Idea
The dualists wrongly equate unity with deficiency, but Islamic theology sees unity as perfection.

This lesson examines the third dualist argument, which claims that if God were only one, existence would be minimal and deficient. The argument attempts to force a multiplicity of necessary beings. We'll explore the reasoning and its theological critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the argument is presented very concisely.
2
Identify the premise
The argument is based on a ranking of existence: the 'one' is considered the lowest and most deficient rank. Understand why the dualists think this.
They assume that unity implies fewness and lack of multiplicity.
3
Trace the conclusion
If the necessary being were only one, it would occupy the lowest rank, which is impossible. Therefore, the necessary being cannot be one—there must be at least two.
The dualists conclude that dualism is necessary to avoid deficiency.
4
Reflect on the Islamic response
Islamic theology rejects the premise that unity is deficiency. Instead, God's oneness is a perfection and the source of all existence.
The argument confuses numerical quantity with ontological value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إن أقل مرتبة من مراتب الوجود وأشدها نقصاناً الواحد، فلو كان واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا الواحد، لوجب أن يكون في نهاية القلة والنقص، وذلك محال.
The third proof: The lowest rank of the ranks of existence and the most deficient is the one. So if necessary existence in itself is nothing but the one, then it would necessarily be at the extreme of fewness and deficiency, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage contains the third dualist argument, which is based on a ranking of existence. The dualists assert that the 'one' (al-wahid) is the lowest and most deficient rank of existence. If God, who is necessary existence in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), were only one, then existence would be at its minimum and most deficient—a conclusion they deem impossible. They imply that to avoid this deficiency, there must be multiplicity (at least two) among necessary existents, thereby arguing for dualism.

Key Terms
مراتب الوجود
نقصان
قلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'one' is necessarily deficient; Islamic theology holds that God's unity is not a deficiency but a perfection.
Correction: Unity in God is not a numerical oneness that implies lack; rather, it is an absolute simplicity and perfection.

Checkpoint

A) Because unity is more perfect.
B) Because the one is the lowest and most deficient rank of existence.
C) Because multiplicity is always better.
D) Because existence requires plurality.

Key Takeaway
The dualist argument mistakenly treats unity as deficiency, but in Islamic theology, God's oneness is the highest perfection.

8 min
The Impossibility of Multiple Necessary Existents
Argument against multiple necessary existents via reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that proves there cannot be more than one necessary existent (wājib al-wujūd).

Core Idea
The mere concept of necessary existence does not prevent plurality; only a rigorous argument can.

This passage presents a sophisticated philosophical proof against the possibility of multiple necessary existents. The author begins by acknowledging that the mere concept of necessary existence does not inherently forbid sharing (shirkah). He then systematically examines whether any additional factor could preclude plurality, and shows that such a factor cannot exist. The argument culminates in a reductio ad absurdum: assuming multiple necessary existents leads to a contradiction, proving that only one can exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main dilemma
Read the first part of the passage where the author sets up two possibilities: either the concept of necessary existence is accompanied by another concept that prevents sharing, or it is not.
Notice that the author starts by asserting that the mere concept does not prevent sharing.
2
Follow the refutation of the first possibility
Trace the reasoning that rejects the idea of an accompanying concept. The author splits this into two sub-cases: the concept is a quiddity or not. Understand why both are problematic.
If the accompanying concept is a quiddity, it is universal; if it is not, it has no reality.
3
Understand the reductio in the second possibility
Examine what happens if no concept accompanies: the quiddity remains susceptible to sharing. This leads to a contradiction because if sharing is possible, the entities cannot be necessary existents, contradicting the assumption.
The conclusion is that multiple necessary existents are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك ، علمنا أن نفس تصور هذا المعنى غير مانع من الشركة
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن يقال : قارنه مفهوم آخر ، يمنع من هذه الشركة ، أو لم يقارنه ذلك . والأول باطل لأن ذلك المقارن إما أن يكون ماهية من الماهيات أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فتلك الماهية من حيث انها ماهية تكون محتملة للشركة . فإذا قيدنا ماهية وجوب الوجود بهذه الماهية الثانية ، كان الحاصل [ أيضاً ماهية كلية لأن الكلي إذا تقيد بالكلي ، كـان الحاصل (۱) ] منهما كلياً أيضاً. والكلي لا يمنع من احتمال الشركة . وأما إن قلنا : إن هذا المقارن ليس له ماهية ولا حقيقة أصلا ، امتنع كونه مقارناً لشيء آخر .
هذا إذا قلنا : إن مفهوم وجوب الوجود بالذات قارنه ما صار مانعاً من الشركة ، وأما إذا لم يقارنه شيء أصلاً ، فنقول : تلك الماهية كانت محتملة للشركة ، ولم يقارنه ما يمنع من هذه الشركة، وهذا يقتضي أن تكون هذه الشركة غير ممتنعة أصلاً ، وإذا لم تكن ممتنعة ، فهي إما ممكنة بالإمكان الخاص ، فحينئذ [ لا يكون شيء منها واجب الوجود لذاته ، فحينئذ (۲) ] لا تكون الكثرة واقعة في الأشياء التي يكون كل واحد منها واجب الوجود لذاته، قد فرضنا أن الأمر كذلك. هذا خلف . وإذا بطل كون تلك الأشياء ممكنة بالإمكان الخاص ثبت كونها واجبة الوجود لذواتها. وهذا يقتضي وجود أشياء يكون كل واحد منها واجب الوجود لذاته
Likewise, we know that the mere conception of this meaning does not prevent sharing (al-shirkah). If this is established, we say: either it is said that another concept accompanies it, preventing this sharing, or it does not accompany it. The first is false because that accompaniment either is a quiddity (māhiyyah) among quiddities or is not such. If the first, then that quiddity, insofar as it is a quiddity, is susceptible to sharing. So if we qualify the quiddity of necessary existence (wujūb al-wujūd) by this second quiddity, the result is [also a universal quiddity because when the universal is qualified by the universal, the result] from them is also universal. And the universal does not prevent the possibility of sharing. But if we say that this accompaniment has no quiddity or reality at all, it is impossible for it to accompany something else. This is if we say that the concept of necessary existence in itself is accompanied by that which prevents sharing. But if it is not accompanied by anything at all, we say: that quiddity was susceptible to sharing, and nothing prevents this sharing, and this necessitates that this sharing is not impossible at all. And if it is not impossible, then it is either possible with specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) — in which case [none of them is necessary existence in itself, so then] multiplicity does not occur among things each of which is necessary existence in itself; we have assumed that it is so. This is a contradiction (khalaf). And if the possibility of those things with specific possibility is invalidated, then their being necessary existence in themselves is established. And this necessitates the existence of things each of which is necessary existence in itself.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a complex argument against the possibility of multiple necessary existents. The author begins by noting that the mere concept of necessary existence does not inherently preclude multiplicity (shirkah). He then considers two possibilities: either this concept is accompanied by another concept that prevents sharing, or it is not. The first option is refuted by examining the nature of the accompanying concept. If it is a quiddity (māhiyyah), it is universal and thus cannot prevent sharing; if it is not a quiddity, it has no reality and cannot serve as a distinguisher. The second option leads to a dilemma: if no concept prevents sharing, then plurality is not impossible. That plurality would then be either impossible (but it's not) or possible with specific possibility. If possible, then none of the things could be necessary existents, contradicting the assumption that there are multiple necessary existents. Hence, the assumption is false, and the only conclusion is that there cannot be multiple necessary existents.

Key Terms
شِركة (shirkah)
ماهية (māhiyyah)
وجوب الوجود (wujūb al-wujūd)
كلي (kullī)
إمكان خاص (imkān khāṣṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding a particularizing concept to 'wajib al-wujud' would make it unique and prevent sharing.
Correction: The argument shows that any added concept is either a quiddity (universal) or non-existent, so it cannot prevent sharing.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the possibility of sharing (shirkah) among necessary existents?
A) Sharing is possible if an additional concept accompanies the necessary existent.
B) Sharing is impossible regardless of any accompanying concept.
C) Sharing is possible only if the accompanying concept is a quiddity.
D) Sharing is impossible only if no concept accompanies the necessary existent.

Key Takeaway
Multiple necessary existents are impossible because the assumption leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument dismantles any intellectual excuse for associating partners with Allah. Have you ever, even in your heart, considered that something else could be ultimately independent of Allah? What is that thing, and how does this passage force you to reprioritize your trust?
?
The passage shows that the very idea of multiple necessary existents leads to a contradiction. In your daily life, where do you act as if there are competing ultimate authorities—your desires, people, or systems? How can you align your actions with the singularity of Allah's necessity?
?
If you fully internalize that only Allah is Wajib al-Wujud, what would change about how you perceive your own existence and dependency? Write a specific commitment to remind yourself daily of this truth.

beginner
5 min
Third Proof: The One is the Lowest Rank
A brief reductio proof that the Necessary Existent cannot be only one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the Necessary Existent cannot be a mere numerical one, because that would imply deficiency.

Core Idea
Being merely 'one' is the lowest rank, not a perfection.

This lesson presents a brief but powerful proof that the Necessary Being cannot be reduced to a single, undifferentiated unity. The author argues that 'one' in the numerical sense is the lowest and most deficient rank of existence. Since the Necessary Existent must be perfect, it cannot be just one. This proof counters claims that God is a simple numerical unity without any multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the concise structure: a premise, a conditional, and a conclusion.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the key premise
The author states: 'The lowest rank of existence and the most deficient is the one.' What does 'one' mean here? It means a single, undifferentiated entity without any composition or multiplicity.
This is an assertion about the nature of numerical unity.
3
Understand the reductio
If the necessary existent were only this kind of 'one', it would be at the extreme of paucity and deficiency. But deficiency is impossible for the necessary existent, so the assumption is false.
Therefore, the necessary existent must have some multiplicity or richness.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument matters. It shows that true perfection includes internal richness, not bare simplicity. The Necessary Being must encompass all perfections.
This argument is used against certain theological views that overemphasize divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن أقل مرتبة من مراتب الوجود وأشدها نقصانـا الواحد ، فلو كان واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا الواحد ، لوجب أن يكون في نهاية القلة والنقص ، وذلك محال
The third proof: The lowest rank of the ranks of existence and the most deficient in deficiency is the one. So if the necessary existent in itself were nothing but the one, it would necessarily be at the extreme of paucity and deficiency, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This is a short proof by reductio ad absurdum. The author asserts that the lowest and most deficient level of existence is that of the one (al-wāḥid). If the necessary existent (wājib al-wujūd) were only one, then it would be at that deficient level. But deficiency is impossible for the necessary existent, so the premise that the necessary existent is only one must be false. This proof is used to support the necessity of multiplicity in the Necessary Being, often in the context of trinitarian or dualistic claims.

Key Terms
حجة ثالثة
مراتب الوجود
واحد
قلة
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'one' always implies perfection and completeness.
Correction: Here, the author argues that numerical oneness (without any multiplicity) is actually the lowest and most deficient rank, not a perfection.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof conclude about the necessary existent being only one?
A) It is necessary for perfection.
B) It is impossible because it would entail deficiency.
C) It is possible but unlikely.
D) It is mandatory for uniqueness.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be a mere numerical one, because that would imply deficiency, contradicting its perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', do you ever imagine Allah as a simple 'one' devoid of internal richness? How does this proof challenge that mental image, and what will you do to ensure your understanding of tawḥīd is elevated beyond a deficient numerical oneness?
?
If the lowest rank of existence is the one, then what does that demand from you when you proclaim Allah's oneness? Are you willing to let go of any simplistic or reductive ideas about God, even if they feel comfortable?

Refutation of the First Objection: The Good God and Evil
The author refutes the first objection against dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dualistic assumption of a good god and an evil god leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
A good god who cannot stop evil is not divine; one who can but does not is evil.

The first objection against the unity of the necessary existent posits two gods, one good and one evil. The author shows that this dualism collapses because the good god either cannot prevent evil (making him deficient) or can but does not (making him evil). Either way, the good god is not truly good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then read the literal translation. Focus on the logical structure of the argument.
The passage is a conditional argument with three branches.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The author examines three possibilities: 1) The good god cannot repel the evil god. 2) He can and does repel. 3) He can but does not. Notice the conclusion in each case.
3
Understand the final conclusion
Each scenario leads to an absurdity, proving that the statement of 'the two' (good and evil gods) is false.
The phrase 'على كل التقديرات' means 'in all respects'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن نقول : الإله الخير إن لم يقدر على دفع الشرير عن الشر ، فهو ناقص حقير ، ولا يصلح للإلهية ، وإن كان قادراً عليه . فإن فعله فقد اندفع الشرير وظهر عجزه ، فلا يصلح للإنمية ، وإن لم يدفعه عن الشر مع كونه قادراً على ذلك الدفع ، فحينئذ يكون الإله الخير ، راضياً بفعل الشر. والراضي بفعل الشر شرير ، فيلزم أن يكون الإله الخير شريراً . وذلك محال . فيثبت : أن القول بالأثنين باطل على كل التقديرات
·
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : ماهية واجب الوجود بالذات اقتضى ذلك التعين بعينه ، فلا جرم كان واجب الوجود ، يجب أن يكون ذلك المعين ولهذا السبب كان واجب الوجود لذاته واحد ؟
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إنه لو كان كل الإنسان إلا الواحد ، فحينئذ يكون تمام ماهية الإنسان محصورة في ذلك الشخص ، أما إذا كانت الإنسانية مشتركا فيها بين أشخاص كثيرين، لم يكن تمام هذه الماهية ، موجوداً في شخص واحد ، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن الأول أكمل [ والله أعلم (۱) ]
And the answer to the first doubt (shubhah): We say: The good god (al-ilāh al-khayr), if he is not able to repel the evil one (al-sharīr) from evil, then he is deficient and contemptible, and he is not fit for divinity. And if he is able to do so, then if he does it, the evil one is repelled and his impotence appears, so he is not fit for divinity? [literally unclear]; and if he does not repel him from evil despite being able to repel, then the good god becomes pleased with the action of evil. And the one pleased with evil action is evil, so it follows that the good god is evil. That is impossible. Thus it is established that the statement of the two is false in all respects.
And the answer to the second doubt: Why is it not permissible to say: The quiddity of the necessary existent in itself necessitated that very determination (ta‘ayyun bi‘aynihi), so necessarily the necessary existent must be that determined one, and for this reason the necessary existent in itself is one?
And the answer to the third doubt: If every human except one were [something], then the whole quiddity of humanity would be confined to that person. But if humanity is shared among many persons, then the whole of this quiddity is not present in a single person. And it is known by necessity that the first is more perfect. [And God knows best.]
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage contains the author's responses to three objections (shubah) against the doctrine of the unity of the necessary existent. The first objection concerns the problem of evil in a dualistic system with a good god and an evil god. The answer argues that the good god cannot be truly good if he fails to prevent evil, leading to a contradiction. The second objection questions why the necessary existent must be determined as this particular one; the answer simply states that its essence necessitates that determination. The third objection uses an analogy with the quiddity of humanity: if humanity were instantiated in only one person, that person would contain the whole essence, making him more perfect; this supports the idea that the one necessary existent is complete and perfect.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah)
الإله الخير (good god)
الشرير (evil one)
الأثنين (the two)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument implies that God is impotent or evil if He allows evil.
Correction: The argument is directed against dualism, not against the existence of evil in a monotheistic framework. It shows that if there were two gods, the good one would be logically compromised. In Islamic theology, evil exists by divine wisdom, but God is not pleased with it in a moral sense.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what follows if the good god is able to repel the evil god but does not?
A) The good god is deficient.
B) The evil god becomes good.
C) The good god is pleased with evil and becomes evil.
D) The evil god is repelled.

Key Takeaway
The dualistic notion of a good and an evil god is logically impossible because the good god cannot be both good and coexistent with an evil god.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering or see evil in the world, do you ever secretly doubt that God is both all-powerful and completely good? How does this logical argument expose the weakness of those doubts and call you to put your trust in God alone?
?
The argument shows that if God were unable to stop evil, He would be deficient. Do you live as if God is truly sovereign over all evil, or do you sometimes act as if evil is beyond His control? What change in your daily trust does this demand?
?
If you were to defend the idea that God allows evil for a wise purpose, how would you answer the dualist's claim that this makes God 'pleased with evil'? Does this argument force you to deepen your understanding of God's justice and wisdom, and how does that affect your worship?

Refutation of the Second Objection: Determination of the Necessary Existent
The author refutes the second objection against the unity of the necessary existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessary existent must be a specific determined entity and cannot be multiple.

Core Idea
The essence of the necessary existent necessitates its own determination as one.

The second objection questions why the necessary existent must be this particular determined being rather than another. The author answers by stating that the very quiddity (essence) of the necessary existent demands that specific determination, and that is why it is one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short Arabic answer and its translation. It is a single rhetorical question that contains the answer.
The passage is phrased as a question but functions as a statement.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The author asserts that the quiddity (mahiyyah) of the necessary existent in itself necessitates its particular determination (ta‘ayyun). This determination is not arbitrary.
The phrase 'اقتضى ذلك التعين بعينه' means 'necessitated that very determination'.
3
Connect to the unity of God
Because the essence necessitates that specific determination, it cannot share its quiddity with any other, hence it is necessarily one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's essence necessarily determines every aspect of His being, including His actions and decrees, does that make you more certain of His wisdom, or does it challenge your sense of free will in relation to His plan? How will you respond to that tension?
?

Can you accept that His choice for you is the best possible, even when you don't see why? Commit today to trust His determination over your own.
?
The unity of God is not just a logical conclusion but a call to single-hearted devotion. If God's essence requires Him to be one, does your life reflect that unity by not dividing your ultimate allegiance? What specific attachment will you surrender today to align with this truth?

Refutation of the Third Objection: Analogy with Humanity
The author refutes the third objection using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy between the quiddity of humanity and the unity of the necessary existent.

Core Idea
If a quiddity is fully instantiated in one being, that being is more perfect; the necessary existent is such a being.

The third objection likely argues that the necessary existent could be multiple. The author counters with an analogy: if humanity were confined to a single person, that person would contain the whole essence and be more perfect. Similarly, the necessary existent, being complete, must be one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English of the third answer. Note the conditional statement.
The text has a missing part; it starts with 'إنه لو كان كل الإنسان إلا الواحد' which seems incomplete.
2
Understand the analogy
Consider the quiddity of humanity. If all humans except one were gone, that one person would contain the whole essence of humanity. If many people share humanity, the quiddity is distributed.
3
Apply to the necessary existent
The necessary existent is like the single person containing the whole quiddity. It is more perfect and therefore must be one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's oneness is more perfect than any imagined multiplicity? Examine your actions: do you ever rely on created things as if they share God's power? Will you repent and return to exclusive reliance on Him today?
?
